Obligation to Follow a Scholar
Verse asserting obligatory taqlid of a scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that following a qualified scholar in religious matters is obligatory according to the poem.

Core Idea
Taqlid of a scholar is a duty, not an option.

This verse addresses a fundamental question: must we rely on scholars for religious guidance? The poet asserts that it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among the Imams, as transmitted by the community with clear expression. Let's examine the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the words 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'taqlid' (following).
The term 'hibr' means a deeply learned scholar.
2
Identify the ruling
The verse states that taqlid is obligatory. Reflect on why this is important for maintaining correct practice.
This is a practical ruling, not a theoretical one.
3
Consider the source
The poet says the people narrated it with understandable expression, indicating the basis is transmitted knowledge.
4
Apply to your life
Think about how you seek knowledge from qualified scholars today. This verse encourages reliance on experts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٢ - فواجبٌ تَقليدُ حَبْرٍ مِنهُمُ كَذَا حَكَى القَوْمُ بِلَفْظِ يُفْهَمُ ٨٣ - وَأَثْبِتَنُ للأَوْلِيَا الْكَرَامَهُ وَمَنْ نَفَـاهـا إِنْبِذَنْ كَلامَة ٨٤ - وَعِنْدَنا أَنَّ الدُّعاءَ يَنْفَعُ كما مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْدًا يُسْمَعُ
82. So it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among them, likewise the people narrated with an understandable expression. 83. And affirm for the awliya' the karama (miracle), and whoever denies it, reject his speech. 84. And with us, supplication benefits, as from the Qur'an a promise is heard.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
Verse 82 states that it is obligatory (wājib) to perform taqlīd (following) a scholar (ḥibr) from among the Imams, as transmitted by the people with understandable wording. Verse 83 commands affirming karāmāt (miraculous favors) for the awliyā' (saints), and that whoever denies it should have his speech rejected (inbidh). Verse 84 affirms the efficacy of supplication (du'ā'), that it benefits, as the Qur'an contains a promise that supplication is heard.

Key Terms
تقليد
حبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does verse 82 say about following a scholar?
A) It is optional
B) It is forbidden
C) It is obligatory
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
Following a qualified scholar is a religious obligation, not a choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever disregarded a scholar's opinion because it was inconvenient? What does that say about your commitment to the obligation stated in this verse?
?
If you truly believe that following a scholar is obligatory, what steps will you take this week to ensure you are actively seeking and implementing scholarly guidance?

Affirming Karamah for the Awliya
Verse commanding affirmation of karamah for awliya and rejecting deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Muslims must affirm the occurrence of miraculous favors (karamah) from Allah through His righteous servants (awliya).

Core Idea
Denying karamah is a serious error; affirming it is obligatory.

This verse tackles a controversial topic: miracles performed by saints. The poet commands affirming such miracles and warns against those who deny them. Let's see what the text says and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic: 'wa-athbitanna lil-awliya al-karamah'. Note the emphatic command form.
'Karamah' means a miracle granted by Allah to a righteous person, as distinct from prophethood.
2
Understand the command
The poet says 'affirm' (athbitan), indicating this is a required belief. Opposing it leads to rejection of that person's speech.
The consequence for denial is 'inbidh kalamah' – reject his statement.
3
Reflect on the significance
Karamah confirms the honor Allah bestows on His friends. Denying it implies disbelief in Allah's power to support them.
4
Apply the lesson
When you hear of a pious person's miracle, do not dismiss it. Accept it as possible and respect the awliya.

Source Text

82. So it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among them, likewise the people narrated with an understandable expression. 83. And affirm for the awliya' the karama (miracle), and whoever denies it, reject his speech. 84. And with us, supplication benefits, as from the Qur'an a promise is heard.

Commentary
Verse 82 states that it is obligatory (wājib) to perform taqlīd (following) a scholar (ḥibr) from among the Imams, as transmitted by the people with understandable wording. Verse 83 commands affirming karāmāt (miraculous favors) for the awliyā' (saints), and that whoever denies it should have his speech rejected (inbidh). Verse 84 affirms the efficacy of supplication (du'ā'), that it benefits, as the Qur'an contains a promise that supplication is heard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that miracles only occurred for prophets, not saints.
Correction: Karamah is established for awliya as a grace from Allah, as affirmed by the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Checkpoint
What does verse 83 command us to do regarding the awliya?
A) Deny their karama
B) Question their karama
C) Affirm their karama
D) Ignore them

Key Takeaway
Believing in the karamah of the awliya is required; denial is a serious fault.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a story about a saint's miracle as superstition? How does this verse challenge your willingness to accept what Allah may have granted to His awliya?
?
If you were among those who subtly doubt the karamah of the awliya, what would it take for you to wholeheartedly affirm it, knowing that denial leads to having your speech rejected?

The Benefit of Supplication
Verse affirming that supplication benefits, based on Quranic promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that supplication (du'a) is beneficial and effective, as promised in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Du'a works – Allah has promised to respond to those who call upon Him.

This verse addresses the common question: does prayer really make a difference? The poet affirms the benefit of du'a, citing the Qur'anic promise that supplication is heard. Let's explore the verse and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read the Arabic: 'wa-indana anna al-du'a yanfa'u'. Notice the phrase 'yanfa'u' (benefits).
The poet says 'with us' indicating this is a core belief of Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Understand the proof
The benefit is based on the Qur'anic promise ('wa'dan yusma'u'). Allah says in the Qur'an that He responds to supplication.
Refer to verses like 'Call upon Me; I will respond to you' (Qur'an 40:60).
3
Reflect on practical benefit
Du'a is a means of obtaining good and repelling harm. It changes destiny and brings comfort.
4
Apply in worship
Strengthen your conviction in du'a. Make it a habit to supplicate with sincerity, knowing it benefits.

Source Text

82. So it is obligatory to follow a scholar from among them, likewise the people narrated with an understandable expression. 83. And affirm for the awliya' the karama (miracle), and whoever denies it, reject his speech. 84. And with us, supplication benefits, as from the Qur'an a promise is heard.

Commentary
Verse 82 states that it is obligatory (wājib) to perform taqlīd (following) a scholar (ḥibr) from among the Imams, as transmitted by the people with understandable wording. Verse 83 commands affirming karāmāt (miraculous favors) for the awliyā' (saints), and that whoever denies it should have his speech rejected (inbidh). Verse 84 affirms the efficacy of supplication (du'ā'), that it benefits, as the Qur'an contains a promise that supplication is heard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that du'a is pointless because everything is predestined.
Correction: Du'a is itself part of destiny; it is a means ordained by Allah to bring about outcomes.

Checkpoint
Why does the poet say supplication benefits?
A) Because of personal experience
B) Because the Qur'an promises it
C) Because scholars said so
D) Because it is a tradition

Key Takeaway
Supplication is beneficial because Allah has promised to respond in the Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you truly expect Allah to respond, or do you recite it out of habit? How can you transform your du'a into an act of unwavering trust in Allah's promise?
?
If you knew with certainty that Allah will answer every sincere du'a, how would your daily life and priorities change starting today?
?
What is one specific du'a you have been hesitant to make because you doubted it would be answered? Will you make it now with conviction in the Qur'anic promise?

Affirming Karamat of Awliya and Rejecting Deniers
Sanusi affirms saintly miracles and warns against deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to affirm saintly miracles (karamat) and the ruling on those who deny them according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Miracles of saints are real; denying them invalidates one's speech.

In this line from the Jawharah, Sanusi presents a concise but weighty creedal point: the miracles of Allah's friends (awliya) are a reality that must be affirmed. He does not stop at affirmation, but adds a clear warning—whoever denies these karamat is to have his speech rejected. This establishes that denying saintly miracles contradicts established Sunni doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic line carefully, noting the imperative verb 'ithbitanna' (affirm) and the conditional phrase 'wa man nafaha'. Then read the literal translation.
The Arabic uses a strong imperative form.
2
Understand what karamat are
Recognize that karamat are extraordinary events granted by Allah to His righteous servants, distinct from prophetic miracles (mu'jizat).
The passage does not define karamat, but the context is the Sunni belief in their reality.
3
Grasp the consequence of denial
Sanusi says whoever denies karamat, 'inbidhan kalamah'—reject his speech. This means his word is not accepted in matters of creed, as he has contradicted a known truth.
This is a strong censure, but does not automatically declare disbelief.
4
Reflect on the importance
Why is this point included in a creed poem? Because denying saintly miracles can lead to denying Allah's power and favor to His friends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَثْبِتَنُ للأَوْلِيَا الْكَرَامَهُ وَمَنْ نَفَـاهـا إِنْبِذَنْ كَلامَة
And affirm for the saints the miracles (al-karāmah), and whoever denies it, reject his speech.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a command to affirm the miracles (karamat) of the awliya (saints). It states that whoever denies these miracles is to be rejected—meaning his statement is not accepted, as he contradicts the orthodox Sunni position. The line is part of the Nazm and asserts the reality of saintly miracles as a point of creed.

Key Terms
الأَوْلِيَا
الْكَرَامَهُ
إِنْبِذَنْ كَلامَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think saintly miracles (karamat) are not real or are just coincidences.
Correction: Karamat are real and are part of Sunni creed; denying them contradicts established belief.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what should be done to someone who denies the karamat of the awliya?
A) He is to be declared a disbeliever
B) His speech is to be rejected
C) He is to be forgiven
D) He is to be punished physically

Key Takeaway
Affirming the miracles of saints is obligatory in Sunni Islam, and rejecting them invalidates one's credibility in creedal matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a saintly miracle happened before your eyes today, would you accept it as from Allah, or would doubt creep in because of modern rationalism? Your answer reveals the strength of your faith in Allah's ongoing power.
?
Have you ever dismissed a story of a saint's miracle as exaggeration? If so, what does that say about your belief in Allah's ability to act through His righteous servants? Repent for any such doubt and affirm your certainty.
?
If you encounter someone denying karamat, will you have the courage to correct them with evidence, or will you remain silent to avoid conflict? Your silence might be complicity in spreading falsehood. Decide now how you will act.

Understanding al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam
Commentator defines al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam with rhetorical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and rhetorical devices behind the terms al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam in the first line of the poem.

Core Idea
Al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam signify the universal reach and lofty status of the Prophet's Sunnah.

The commentator unpacks two key terms from the opening line: al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam. These are more than simple geographical or physical terms—they carry theological depth through metaphor. This lesson will help you see how language is used to elevate the Sunnah's authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall sense. Notice the definitions and the rhetorical explanations.
2
Examine al-Khafiqayn
Identify what al-Khafiqayn literally means (east and west) and how the commentator expands it to all directions. Understand the mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) of the winds flapping.
The winds flapping attribute action to the location.
3
Examine al-A'lam
See how al-A'lam (banners) is used as a kinaya (metaphor) for high status and importance. Connect it to the Ahl al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالسُّنَّة: طريقة سيد التقلين سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وطريقته: كلُّ ما جاء به القرآن الكريم؛ لما ورد في الحديث من أنه كان: كان خُلقه القرآن. والخافقين في الأصل: المشرق والمغرب، والمراد بهما عموم الجهات، وتسمية هاتين الجهتين خافقين؛ لأنَّ الرَّياح تخفق فيهما: تضطرب ذاهبة وجائية، فهو مجاز عقلي بإسناد الفعل إلى مكان حصوله. والأعلام: جمع عَلَم - بفتح العين المهملة واللام جميعاً ـ وهو في هذه الفقرة بمعنى الراية، وقد جرت العادة بألا تُنصبَ الرَّايَاتُ إِلَّا فوق رؤوس عُظماء الناس؛ فهذه الفقرة كناية عن ثبوت صفة الرّفعة وعِظم الشَّأن لأهل السنة المحمدية. محي.
And what is meant by al-Sunnah: the way of the Master of the Two Weighty Things (al-thaqalayn), our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and his way: everything that the Noble Quran brought; because of what is reported in the hadith that he was: 'His character was the Quran.' And al-Khafiqayn originally: the east and the west, and what is meant by them is all directions. And these two directions are called khafiqayn because the winds flap (takhfiq) in them: they move back and forth, so it is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) by attributing the action to the place where it occurs. And al-A'lam: plural of 'alam - with fat-ha on the 'ayn and laam - and it in this passage means the banner. And it is customary that banners are only raised above the heads of great people; so this passage is a metaphor (kinaya) for the establishment of the attribute of elevation and great importance for the Muhammadian Ahl al-Sunnah. Muhyee.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines two terms from the author's first line: al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam. First, the commentator reiterates the definition of al-Sunnah as the Prophet's way, which is everything from the Quran, referencing the hadith that his character was the Quran. Then, he explains al-Khafiqayn: originally the east and west, but meaning all directions. The reason for the name is that winds 'flap' in these directions, going back and forth; this is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) where the action is attributed to the place. Next, he explains al-A'lam: plural of 'alam, here meaning banner (raya). He notes that banners are only raised over the heads of important people, so the phrase is a metaphor (kinaya) for the high status and immense importance of the Muhammadian Ahl al-Sunnah. The passage concludes with the commentator's abbreviation 'Muhyee'.

Key Terms
الخافقين
الأعلام
الراية
المجاز العقلي
الكناية
السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Khafiqayn refers only to the east and west literally, without the extended meaning of all directions.
Correction: The commentator explains that al-Khafiqayn, while originally east and west, are intended to encompass all directions (عموم الجهات).

Checkpoint
What do 'al-A'lam' mean in the first line according to the commentator?
A) Mountains
B) Banners
C) High ranks
D) Knowledges

Key Takeaway
Al-Khafiqayn and al-A'lam are rich terms that, through metaphor, convey the universal scope and high station of the Prophet's Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The winds flap in both east and west — the Prophet's Sunnah reaches every direction. In which direction of your life have you shut out the Sunnah? Will you open it today, or keep it locked?
?
Banners are raised only over the heads of great people. Do you live as if the Ahl al-Sunnah — the Prophet's true followers — deserve that banner, or do you act in a way that lowers it? What one habit must change for you to honour this station?
?
The Prophet's character was the Quran — his Sunnah is the Quran lived out. Have you separated the Book from the way? Right now, is there a verse you believe but a Sunnah you ignore? Repent for that gap and close it before you sleep tonight.

Defining Shubah and A'lam with Rhetorical Notes
Commentator defines shubah (specious doubts) and a'lam (mountains) with etymology and rhetorical figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of shubah (specious doubts) and a'lam (mountains) as used in the text, and identify the rhetorical figures (tibaq, shibh al-tibaq, tamm jinass) noted by the commentator.

Core Idea
Shubah are doubts that resemble truth, and a'lam here means mountains—rhetorical artistry enriches the text.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two key terms from the second line of the poem: shubah (specious doubts) and a'lam (mountains). He also highlights several rhetorical devices that the poet employs, such as antithesis and paronomasia. Understanding these terms and figures deepens appreciation of the text's precision and beauty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the two main sections: definitions and rhetorical notes.
2
Analyze the definition of shubah
Examine the vocalization, meaning, and two possible etymologies: from resemblance (mushabahah) or confusion (ishtibah). Understand why it's called a specious doubt.
3
Understand a'lam as mountains
Note that a'lam in this context does not mean 'flags' or 'notables' but specifically 'mountains' (jibal). This is a contextual meaning.
4
Identify the rhetorical figures
The commentator names three figures: tibaq (antithesis) between 'raised' and 'placed'; shibh al-tibaq (near-antithesis) between 'Ahl al-Sunnah' and 'opponents' and between 'clear proofs' and 'shubah'; and tamm jinass (complete paronomasia) between 'a'laman' in two lines.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the commentator includes these rhetorical notes. They show the eloquence of the text and help avoid misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشُّبَه - بضم الشَّين المعجمة وفتح الباء الموحدة ـ جمع شبهة، وهي ما يعرض للناظر في أمر من الأمور، ولم يصل إلى درجة العلم واليقين، وهي في الأصل مأخوذة إما من المُشابهة بحسب الظاهر - تشبه الدليل الصحيح، وإن كانت عند التحقيق المُماثلة، لأنّها بمعنى زائفة غير رائجة، وإما من الاشتباه بمعنى الالتباس؛ لأنَّها تُوقع مَنْ عَرضِتْ له في الالتباس واختلاطِ الصَّحيح بالفاسد في نظره. - والأعلام: جمع علم، وهو في هذه الفقرة بمعنى الجبل، وفي قوله: (رفع) و(وضع) طباق وفي قوله: (أهل السُّنَّة مع قوله : (المخالفين) وقوله : (واضح أدلتهم مع قوله: (شبه) شبه الطباق، وفي قوله : (أعلاما) في الفقرة الأولى مع قوله : (أعلاما) في الثانية جناس تام. محي.
Al-Shubah - with dammah on the shin and fathah on the ba - plural of shubhah, and it is what appears to the observer concerning some matter, and has not reached the level of knowledge and certainty. And it is originally derived either from al-mushabahah (resemblance) in appearance - it resembles a correct proof, even if upon investigation it is dissimilar, because it is false and not prevalent; or from al-ishtibah (confusion) meaning ambiguity, because it causes the one to whom it appears to fall into confusion and mixture of the correct and corrupt in his view. - And al-A'lam: plural of 'alam, and it in this passage means the mountain. And in His saying: (raised) and (placed) there is tibaq (antithesis). And in His saying: (Ahl al-Sunnah) with His saying: (the opponents) and His saying: (clear proofs) with His saying: (shubah) there is shibh al-tibaq. And in His saying: (a'laman) in the first passage with His saying: (a'laman) in the second there is tamm jinass (complete paronomasia). Muhyee.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the terms al-shubah (specious doubts) and al-a'lam (mountains) from the author's second line. The commentator begins by giving the vocalization and definition of shubah: they are appearances of truth that fall short of certainty. He explains the etymology: either from 'resemblance' (mushabahah) because they resemble a correct proof but are actually false, or from 'confusion' (ishtibah) because they cause confusion between truth and falsehood. Then he defines a'lam in this context as mountains (jibal). He then notes several rhetorical figures: tibaq (antithesis) between 'raised' and 'placed'; shibh al-tibaq (near-antithesis) between 'Ahl al-Sunnah' and 'opponents' and between 'clear proofs' and 'shubah'; and tamm jinass (complete paronomasia) between 'a'laman' in the first line and 'a'laman' in the second line. The passage ends with the abbreviation 'Muhyee'.

Key Terms
الشبه
الشبهة
المشابهة
الاشتباه
الأعلام
الجبل
الطباق
شبه الطباق
الجناس التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shubah are deliberate falsehoods or lies.
Correction: The passage explains that shubah are genuine doubts that appear true to the observer, not intentional lies. They are specious because they resemble truth but are actually false.

Checkpoint
What does 'a'lam' mean in the second line according to the commentator?
A) Banners
B) Mountains
C) High ranks
D) Notifications

Key Takeaway
Shubah are specious doubts that resemble truth, and a'lam in this line means mountains; the commentator also points out three rhetorical devices: antithesis, near-antithesis, and complete paronomasia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has a specious doubt (shubhah) clouded your judgment, and how did you seek certainty?
?
Can you identify a time when confusing truth with falsehood led you astray? What did you learn from that experience?

Meaning of 'Din' and Its Application to Pre-Islamic Religion
Definition of 'din' and its relationship to milla, shar', shari'a; pre-Islamic religion lacking Tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'din' (religion) is defined in Islamic theology and why it can refer to beliefs devoid of Tawhid.

Core Idea
Din, milla, shar', and shari'a are one essence viewed from different angles.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key term used in the earlier text: 'din' (religion). He explains that before the Prophet's mission, religion was empty of true Tawhid. He then unpacks the relationship between four closely related terms: din, milla, shar', and shari'a. Understanding these distinctions helps us grasp how the Quran applies the word 'din' to non-Islamic faiths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's definitions come through naturally.
Pay attention to the initial phrase about religion being devoid of Tawhid.
2
Identify the four terms and their perspectives
The passage lists din, milla, shar', and shari'a. Notice how each term highlights a different aspect: submission/recompense, dictation by angel, legislation.
They are identical in essence but differ in consideration.
3
Consider the Quranic citation
Reflect on why 'din' can be used for false religions. The author cites Quran 3:85 to show that the term is applied based on the claim of its adherents.
This explains the earlier statement about pre-Islamic religion being devoid of Tawhid.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider how this clarifies the term 'din' in theological discussions. This prevents misunderstanding that 'din' always means Islam in the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته . (وَقَدْ خَلَا الدِّينُ) أي تجرَّدَ عَنِ التَّوْحِيدِ جُملَةٌ حاليَّةٌ مُقيّدة . قوله : (الدين) هو والملة والشرع والشريعة متحدة بالذات مختلفة بالاعتبار، فالأحكام من حيث إنا ندين أي ننقاد لها ونُدَان أي : نُجازى عليها دين ، ومِن حيث إن المَلَكَ يُمْليها للرسول والرسول يمليها علينا مِلَّةٌ ، ومن حيث شَرَعَها لنا أي: نَصبَها وبيَّنها: شرع وشريعةٌ ، وإطلاق الدين على الخالي من التوحيد باعتبار زعم أصحابه ، كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ﴾ [آل عمران: ۸۵] . الأمير.
Explanation of the meaning of religion (din) and its lack of Tawhid before his mission. 'And the religion was devoid' i.e., stripped of Tawhid, a circumstantial clause that restricts. His saying: (al-din) — it, al-milla, al-shar', and al-shari'a are identical in essence but different in consideration. The rulings, from the perspective that we submit to them (nadinu) and are recompensed (nudanu) by them, are din; from the perspective that the angel dictates them to the messenger and the messenger dictates them to us, they are milla; and from the perspective that He legislated them (shara'aha) for us, i.e., established them and explained them, they are shar' and shari'a. And the application of 'din' to what is devoid of Tawhid is based on the claim of its adherents, as Allah said: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam...' [Al-Imran: 85]. al-Amir.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'din' in the context of pre-Islamic religion being devoid of Tawhid. It first states that 'the religion was devoid of Tawhid' as a circumstantial clause. Then it clarifies that the words din, milla, shar', and shari'a are synonymous in essence but differ in consideration: din emphasizes submission and recompense, milla emphasizes dictation by the angel, and shar'/shari'a emphasize legislation. The application of 'din' to non-Islamic religions is based on the claim of their adherents, as the Quran uses the term for other religions.

Key Takeaway
The terms din, milla, shar', and shari'a share one essence but highlight different aspects of divine guidance, and 'din' can refer to any belief system its followers claim.

The Meaning of 'Din' and Its Application to False Religions
Definition of 'din' and objection about Jahiliyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and terminological definitions of 'din' and why it is valid to apply the term to false religions like Jahiliyyah.

Core Idea
The word 'din' can refer to any system of submission, true or false.

This lesson defines the key term 'din' (religion) from both linguistic and technical perspectives. It also addresses a potential objection: if 'din' is used for the true religion of Islam, how can we call the polytheistic practices of Jahiliyyah a 'din'? The commentator clarifies this using linguistic evidence and a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meanings
Read the passage's list of linguistic meanings for 'din': obedience, worship, requital, and reckoning. Note that the word covers a range of ideas.
2
Learn the terminological definition
The terminological meaning is specific: what Allah has prescribed through His prophet. Compare it to the broader linguistic meaning.
3
Consider the objection
The author's phrase implies that the religion of Jahiliyyah can be called 'din'. Why would that be problematic? The objection is that they were polytheists.
4
See the answer and proof
The commentator explains that 'din' applies to any system people submit to, true or false. The Quran uses 'din' for false religions in verse 3:85.
5
Notice the literary device
The passage ends with a note on paronomasia (jinas) between two uses of 'tawhid' – one in its technical sense and one in its linguistic sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدين: ما ورد به الشرع من التعبّد، ويقال: للطاعة، والعبادة، والجزاء والحساب. وعرفوه بأنه: كما ذكر الشارح و(خلا) معناه: تجرد، ووقع في بعض نسخ المتن (عرا) وليس بشيء فإنه يقال: عري يعرى، بوزان: رضي يرضى، بمعنى خلا يخلو، ويقال: عرا يعرو، بمعنى نزل. (والدين) لغةً يطلق على عدة معان: الطاعة والعبادة، والجزاء، والحساب، ويطلق اصطلاحا على ما شرعه الله تعالى على لسان نبيه من الأحكام. فإن قلت: تعبير المصنف يدل على أنه يجوز أن يطلق على ما كان عليه أهل الجاهلية دين، مع أنهم كانوا مشركين وعبدة أوثان، قلنا: لا مانع من إطلاق لفظ الدين على ذلك؛ لأن الدين يطلق ويراد به ما يتدين به وينقاد الناس له، سواء أكان حقا أم باطلا، ويدل لصحة ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ﴾. وقول المصنف: (عن التوحيد) جار ومجرور متعلق بقوله (خلا)، والمراد من التوحيد في هذه الجملة معناه اللغوي، وعلى ذلك يكون بين كلمة التوحيد في الجملة السابقة وكلمة التوحيد في هذه الجملة جناس تام.
And al-din: that which the Sacred Law has brought of worship, and it is said: for obedience, worship, requital, and reckoning. And they defined it as: as the commentator mentioned, and 'khala' means: was stripped, and in some copies of the text (occurred) 'ara' and it is nothing, for it is said: 'ariya ya'ra'... meaning 'khala', and it is said: 'ara ya'ru' meaning 'alighted'. And (al-din) linguistically is applied to several meanings: obedience, worship, requital, and reckoning, and terminologically it is applied to what Allah has prescribed upon the tongue of His prophet of rulings. Then if you say: The author's expression indicates that it is permissible to apply it to what the people of the Jahiliyyah were upon as 'din', even though they were polytheists and idol worshippers, we say: There is no impediment to applying the word 'din' to that, because 'din' is applied and intended as that which one practices as a religion and to which people submit, whether it be true or false. And the proof for the validity of that is His statement: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Quran 3:85). And the author's statement: ('an al-tawhid) is a prepositional phrase connected to 'khala', and what is meant by al-tawhid in this sentence is its linguistic meaning, and accordingly there is complete paronomasia (jinas) between the word al-tawhid in the previous sentence and the word al-tawhid in this sentence.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the term 'din' (religion). The commentator first gives a definition: 'what the Sacred Law has brought of worship' and also notes that it is said to mean obedience, worship, requital, and reckoning. He then clarifies that the verb 'khala' in the text means 'to be stripped' and notes a variant reading 'ara' which he dismisses. Next, he distinguishes between the linguistic and terminological meanings of 'din': linguistically, it applies to multiple meanings including obedience, worship, requital, and reckoning; terminologically, it applies to what Allah has prescribed through His prophet. He then raises an objection: the author's phrase 'wa qad khala al-din 'an al-tawhid' implies that the religion of the Jahiliyyah can be called 'din' even though they were polytheists. The answer is that there is no problem: the word 'din' is used for whatever people submit to as their religion, whether true or false, as evidenced by the Quranic verse 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam...' which uses 'din' for false religions. Finally, he explains that in this context, 'al-tawhid' is used in its linguistic sense (meaning oneness or uniqueness) rather than the technical theological sense, creating a pun (jinas) with the earlier occurrence of 'al-tawhid' in its technical sense.

Key Takeaway
The word 'din' is broader than just Islam; it applies to any religious practice that people submit to, whether true or false.

The Meaning of al-Din in Language and Law
Definition of 'al-din' linguistically and technically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of the term 'al-din' as used by Islamic scholars.

Core Idea
Al-din has multiple linguistic meanings and a specific technical sense as divine law.

Before we discuss religion in theology, we must agree on what 'religion' means. This lesson unpacks the word 'al-din' as used in the Qur'an and scholarship, covering its linguistic range and precise technical definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the definitions and distinctions.
The passage includes the author's opening explanation and two important footnotes.
2
Identify the four linguistic meanings
The passage explicitly lists obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning as linguistic meanings of 'din'. Note them and see how they relate to the root concept.
The Arabic terms are: طاعة, عبادة, جزاء, حساب.
3
Understand the technical definition
After the linguistic meanings, the passage gives a technical definition: what Allah legislates upon the tongue of His prophet. This is narrower than the linguistic sense.
The definition begins with 'ويطلق اصطلاحا على ما شرعه الله تعالى'.
4
Distinguish obedience and worship
Footnote (1) explains that in language, obedience and worship are synonyms, but technically they differ: obedience is compliance without intention, worship requires knowledge and intention.
This distinction is crucial for later theological discussions.
5
Consider the lexical clarification
The passage also clarifies the word 'khala' (to be empty) and mentions a variant reading. This shows attention to precise wording.
The variant 'ara' is rejected in favor of 'khala'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته لنبي، أي: جاء من عند الله بالتَّوحيد في حالِ تعدُّد المعبودات الباطلة وخلو الدِّين ، أي : فراغِه عن التوحيد والتَّفرد والدين: ما ورد به الشَّرع مِن التَّعبُّد، ويقال: للطَّاعة ، والعبادة (١) ، والجزاء والحساب. = وعرَّفوه (۲) بأنه : كما ذكر الشارح و(خلا) معناه: تَجَرَّدَ ، ووقع في بعض نسخ المتن (عرا) وليس بشيء فإنه يقال: «عرِيَ يَعْرَى ، بوزان: رضي يرضى، بمعنى خلا يخلو ، ويقال: «عرا يعرو»، بمعنى نزل . (والدين) لغةً يطلق على عدة معان الطاعة والعبادة، والجزاء، والحساب، ويطلق اصطلاحا على ما شرعه الله تعالى على لسان نبيه من الأحكام» فإن قلت: تعبیر المصنف يدلّ على أنه يجوز أن يطلق على ما كان عليه أهل الجاهلية دين ، مع أنَّهم كانوا مشركين وعبدة أوثان، قلنا: لا مانع من إطلاق لفظ الدِّين على ذلك ؛ لأنَّ الدين يطلق ویراد به ما يُتَدَيَّنُ به وينقادُ النَّاسُ له، سواء أكان حقا أم باطلا ، ويدل لصحة ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۸٥] . وقول المصنف : ( عن التَّوحيد) جارّ ومجرور متعلق بقوله (خلا)، والمراد من التوحيد في هذه الجملة معناه اللغوي، وعلى ذلك يكون بين كلمة التوحيد» في الجملة السابقة وكلمة «التوحيد» في هذه الجملة جناس تام. محي. (۱) قوله: (ويقال للطاعة) أي: يطلق أو يحمل الدين على الطاعة لغة وشرعا. وقوله: (والعبادة) تقدم أن الطاعة والعبادة في اللغة بمعنى واحد ، وفي الاصطلاح متغايران ، فالطاعة: امتثال الأمر واجتناب النهي، ولا يتوقف على معرفة ،المطاع، ولا على النية . وأما العبادة: فهي ما تتوقف على معرفة المعبود وعلى النية. فعلى المعنى اللغوي عطف العبادة على الطاعة من عطف المرادف ، وعلى المعنى المصطلح عليه فهما من عطف الخاص على العام. الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (وعرفوه أي عرَّفهُ أهل الشرع، وليس المقصود منه التبرّي، بل التقوية، وظاهره شهرة هذا التعريف، وليس كذلك مع ما فيه من الخفاء، والأوضح ما أفاده أولا من قوله : ما ورد به الشرع. فإنه اصطلاحي أيضا، وأما ما أشار له بقوله : ويقال ... الخ فمما يشترك فيه الشرع واللغة. الأمير والشنواني.
Explanation of the meaning of the religion (al-din) and its being devoid of tawhid before the mission of the prophet, i.e., he came from Allah with tawhid in the state of multiplicity of false objects of worship and the religion being devoid, i.e., empty of tawhid and uniqueness. And al-din: what the Law has brought of worship, and it is said: for obedience, worship (1), recompense, and reckoning. = And they defined it (2) as: as the commentator mentioned. And (khala) means: it became empty; and in some copies of the text (ara) occurs, but that is nothing, for it is said: 'ariya ya'ra, like radiya yarda, meaning khala yakhlu, and it is said: 'ara ya'ru, meaning he descended. (And al-din) linguistically is applied to several meanings: obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning; and technically it is applied to what Allah the Exalted has legislated upon the tongue of His prophet of rulings. If you say: The author's expression indicates that it is permissible to apply the term 'din' to what the people of the Jahiliyyah followed, despite their being polytheists and idol worshippers. We say: There is no prohibition against applying the word 'din' to that, because al-din is applied and meant by it that which is adopted as a religion and to which people submit, whether it is true or false. And proof of that is the saying of the Most High: {And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him} [Al 'Imran: 85]. And the author's statement: ('an al-tawhid) is a preposition and noun phrase connected to his saying (khala); and what is intended by 'al-tawhid' in this sentence is its linguistic meaning. And on that basis, there is between the word 'al-tawhid' in the previous sentence and the word 'al-tawhid' in this sentence a perfect paronomasia. Muhi. (1) His saying: (and it is said for obedience) meaning: al-din is applied or carried for obedience linguistically and legally. And his saying: (and worship) it has preceded that obedience and worship in language are synonymous, but in technical terms they are different: obedience is compliance with the command and avoidance of the prohibition, and it does not depend on knowledge of the one obeyed nor on intention. As for worship, it depends on knowledge of the worshipped and on intention. So according to the linguistic meaning, the conjunction of worship to obedience is conjoining of synonyms, and according to the technical meaning they are conjoining of the specific to the general. Al-Shanawani. (2) His saying: (and they defined it) meaning the people of the Law defined it; and the intent is not to avoid it, but to strengthen it. And its apparent meaning is the fame of this definition, but that is not the case given what it contains of obscurity. And the clearest is what he first stated: 'what the Law has brought', for that is also technical. And as for what he indicated by his saying 'and it is said ...' it is something shared between the Law and language. Al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an extended definition and explanation of the term 'al-din' (religion) as used in the context of the prophetic mission. It begins by explaining the phrase 'khuluww al-din 'an al-tawhid' (the religion's being devoid of tawhid) before the Prophet's mission, clarifying that the Prophet came with tawhid when false objects of worship were numerous. The author then gives the linguistic meanings of 'al-din': obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning, and notes its technical meaning: what Allah legislates through His prophets. He addresses an objection: the author's wording seems to allow calling the practices of the pre-Islamic Arabs 'a religion' even though they were polytheists. The answer is that 'din' can be used for any system people submit to, whether true or false, and the Quran itself uses it for false religions (e.g., 'other than Islam'). The passage then analyzes the grammatical relationship of 'an al-tawhid' to 'khala' and notes the paronomasia between two occurrences of 'tawhid'. Finally, the commentary (by al-Shanawani and al-Amir) clarifies that 'obedience' and 'worship' are synonyms linguistically but differ technically, and that the definition 'what the Law has brought' is a technical one, while the other usages are shared between language and law.

عبادة
جزاء
حساب

Can False Religions Be Called Din?
Objection and answer regarding applying 'din' to false religions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars permit using the term 'din' for false religions and the evidence from the Quran.

Core Idea
Din can refer to any system people submit to, whether true or false.

The previous lesson established the meanings of 'din'. But can we call the practices of the pre-Islamic Arabs 'a religion'? This lesson answers that question, drawing on Quranic evidence and clarifying the author's wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: objection (فإن قلت) and answer (قلنا).
This is a classic kalam dialectical format.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites verses Al 'Imran 85 to prove that 'din' can be used for non-Islamic religions. Reflect on how the Quran itself applies the term.
The verse explicitly says 'other than Islam' as a din.
3
Analyze the grammatical note
The author explains the grammatical connection of 'an al-tawhid' to 'khala' and points out a paronomasia (jinas) between two occurrences of 'tawhid'.
The two 'tawhid's have different referents: one is the concept, the other is the linguistic term.
4
Summarize the ruling
Conclude that the term 'din' is not restricted to true religion. This is an important principle for interfaith discourse.
This does not mean all religions are true, only that the word can be applied.

Source Text

بيان معنى الدين وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته لنبي، أي: جاء من عند الله بالتَّوحيد في حالِ تعدُّد المعبودات الباطلة وخلو الدِّين ، أي : فراغِه عن التوحيد والتَّفرد والدين: ما ورد به الشَّرع مِن التَّعبُّد، ويقال: للطَّاعة ، والعبادة (١) ، والجزاء والحساب. = وعرَّفوه (۲) بأنه : كما ذكر الشارح و(خلا) معناه: تَجَرَّدَ ، ووقع في بعض نسخ المتن (عرا) وليس بشيء فإنه يقال: «عرِيَ يَعْرَى ، بوزان: رضي يرضى، بمعنى خلا يخلو ، ويقال: «عرا يعرو»، بمعنى نزل . (والدين) لغةً يطلق على عدة معان الطاعة والعبادة، والجزاء، والحساب، ويطلق اصطلاحا على ما شرعه الله تعالى على لسان نبيه من الأحكام» فإن قلت: تعبیر المصنف يدلّ على أنه يجوز أن يطلق على ما كان عليه أهل الجاهلية دين ، مع أنَّهم كانوا مشركين وعبدة أوثان، قلنا: لا مانع من إطلاق لفظ الدِّين على ذلك ؛ لأنَّ الدين يطلق ویراد به ما يُتَدَيَّنُ به وينقادُ النَّاسُ له، سواء أكان حقا أم باطلا ، ويدل لصحة ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۸٥] . وقول المصنف : ( عن التَّوحيد) جارّ ومجرور متعلق بقوله (خلا)، والمراد من التوحيد في هذه الجملة معناه اللغوي، وعلى ذلك يكون بين كلمة التوحيد» في الجملة السابقة وكلمة «التوحيد» في هذه الجملة جناس تام. محي. (۱) قوله: (ويقال للطاعة) أي: يطلق أو يحمل الدين على الطاعة لغة وشرعا. وقوله: (والعبادة) تقدم أن الطاعة والعبادة في اللغة بمعنى واحد ، وفي الاصطلاح متغايران ، فالطاعة: امتثال الأمر واجتناب النهي، ولا يتوقف على معرفة ،المطاع، ولا على النية . وأما العبادة: فهي ما تتوقف على معرفة المعبود وعلى النية. فعلى المعنى اللغوي عطف العبادة على الطاعة من عطف المرادف ، وعلى المعنى المصطلح عليه فهما من عطف الخاص على العام. الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (وعرفوه أي عرَّفهُ أهل الشرع، وليس المقصود منه التبرّي، بل التقوية، وظاهره شهرة هذا التعريف، وليس كذلك مع ما فيه من الخفاء، والأوضح ما أفاده أولا من قوله : ما ورد به الشرع. فإنه اصطلاحي أيضا، وأما ما أشار له بقوله : ويقال ... الخ فمما يشترك فيه الشرع واللغة. الأمير والشنواني.
Explanation of the meaning of the religion (al-din) and its being devoid of tawhid before the mission of the prophet, i.e., he came from Allah with tawhid in the state of multiplicity of false objects of worship and the religion being devoid, i.e., empty of tawhid and uniqueness. And al-din: what the Law has brought of worship, and it is said: for obedience, worship (1), recompense, and reckoning. = And they defined it (2) as: as the commentator mentioned. And (khala) means: it became empty; and in some copies of the text (ara) occurs, but that is nothing, for it is said: 'ariya ya'ra, like radiya yarda, meaning khala yakhlu, and it is said: 'ara ya'ru, meaning he descended. (And al-din) linguistically is applied to several meanings: obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning; and technically it is applied to what Allah the Exalted has legislated upon the tongue of His prophet of rulings. If you say: The author's expression indicates that it is permissible to apply the term 'din' to what the people of the Jahiliyyah followed, despite their being polytheists and idol worshippers. We say: There is no prohibition against applying the word 'din' to that, because al-din is applied and meant by it that which is adopted as a religion and to which people submit, whether it is true or false. And proof of that is the saying of the Most High: {And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him} [Al 'Imran: 85]. And the author's statement: ('an al-tawhid) is a preposition and noun phrase connected to his saying (khala); and what is intended by 'al-tawhid' in this sentence is its linguistic meaning. And on that basis, there is between the word 'al-tawhid' in the previous sentence and the word 'al-tawhid' in this sentence a perfect paronomasia. Muhi. (1) His saying: (and it is said for obedience) meaning: al-din is applied or carried for obedience linguistically and legally. And his saying: (and worship) it has preceded that obedience and worship in language are synonymous, but in technical terms they are different: obedience is compliance with the command and avoidance of the prohibition, and it does not depend on knowledge of the one obeyed nor on intention. As for worship, it depends on knowledge of the worshipped and on intention. So according to the linguistic meaning, the conjunction of worship to obedience is conjoining of synonyms, and according to the technical meaning they are conjoining of the specific to the general. Al-Shanawani. (2) His saying: (and they defined it) meaning the people of the Law defined it; and the intent is not to avoid it, but to strengthen it. And its apparent meaning is the fame of this definition, but that is not the case given what it contains of obscurity. And the clearest is what he first stated: 'what the Law has brought', for that is also technical. And as for what he indicated by his saying 'and it is said ...' it is something shared between the Law and language. Al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an extended definition and explanation of the term 'al-din' (religion) as used in the context of the prophetic mission. It begins by explaining the phrase 'khuluww al-din 'an al-tawhid' (the religion's being devoid of tawhid) before the Prophet's mission, clarifying that the Prophet came with tawhid when false objects of worship were numerous. The author then gives the linguistic meanings of 'al-din': obedience, worship, recompense, and reckoning, and notes its technical meaning: what Allah legislates through His prophets. He addresses an objection: the author's wording seems to allow calling the practices of the pre-Islamic Arabs 'a religion' even though they were polytheists. The answer is that 'din' can be used for any system people submit to, whether true or false, and the Quran itself uses it for false religions (e.g., 'other than Islam'). The passage then analyzes the grammatical relationship of 'an al-tawhid' to 'khala' and notes the paronomasia between two occurrences of 'tawhid'. Finally, the commentary (by al-Shanawani and al-Amir) clarifies that 'obedience' and 'worship' are synonyms linguistically but differ technically, and that the definition 'what the Law has brought' is a technical one, while the other usages are shared between language and law.

توحيد
جاهلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'din' in Arabic only refers to the true religion of Islam.
Correction: Linguistically, 'din' can refer to any system of belief or practice, true or false, as shown by the Quranic verse and scholarly consensus.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author give to support using 'din' for false religions?
A) A hadith from the Prophet
B) A verse from the Quran (3:85)
C) Consensus of the companions
D) Logical deduction alone

Key Takeaway
The term 'din' is applicable to any religion or system people follow, irrespective of its truth or falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'my deen', are you referring to the set of beliefs and practices you truly submit to? Or have you reduced it to a label that doesn't demand your full submission?
?
The lesson defines 'din' as 'that to which people submit'. Is there any person, desire, or ideology besides Allah that you actually submit to in your daily choices? Will you repent today and put your submission back where it belongs?
?
If only Islam is accepted as a 'din' in the sight of Allah, are you living in a way that reflects that exclusivity? Or are you, in your heart, submitting to other 'din' from the world around you?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Technical Definition of al-Din as a Divine Institution
Definition of 'al-din' as a divine institution with explanatory commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise technical definition of 'al-din' in Islamic theology as a divine institution that drives rational beings by their own praiseworthy choice towards eternal good.

Core Idea
Religion is not just any set of rules; it is a divine law that guides rational beings to eternal happiness through their free will.

This passage presents the technical definition of 'al-din' as used by scholars of kalam. Each component of the definition — 'divine', 'driving', 'to those of intellect', 'by their praiseworthy choice', 'to what is essentially good' — serves to exclude other phenomena, clarifying exactly what constitutes religion in the Islamic theological sense. Let's examine how these elements work together.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its components
First, read the Arabic definition and its translation. Notice that it is constructed as a series of restrictive phrases: 'divine', 'driving', 'to those of intellect', 'by their praiseworthy choice', 'to what is essentially good'.
This is a multi-part definition; each part narrows the meaning.
2
Understand each restrictor
For each phrase, consider what it excludes. For example, 'divine' excludes human-made systems, 'driving' excludes non-motivational divine acts, etc. The accompanying commentary explains these exclusions in detail.
Pay special attention to the distinction between divine and human institutions.
3
Address the objection about ijtihad
The text includes an objection: if ijtihad involves human effort, are its rulings divine? Follow how the author argues that such rulings are still divine in origin, merely discovered by scholars.
This clarifies that 'divine' does not mean 'directly revealed without interpretation'.
4
Appreciate the precision of the definition
Reflect on how this definition distinguishes Islam from other systems and emphasizes the role of free will and ultimate salvation. This technical definition is foundational for kalam arguments.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته (۳) وضع إلهي (۱) سائق (۲) لذوي العقولِ ، باختيارهم المحمود (٤) ، إلى ما هو خير لهم بالذات(ه). أي : أحكام وضعها الله (٦) تعالى للعباد ، باعثةٌ إلى الخير الذاتي، وهو ... (۱) قوله: (إلهي) أي : هذه الأحكام الموضوعة منسوبة للإله وهو الله ، وهو فصلٌ مُخرج للأوضاع البشرية، أي التي بكسبهم ، وذلك نحو القوانين السياسية التي ترجع إليها سياسة العالم، كعلم إصلاح المنزل، والأوضاع الصناعية كالنجارة فلا تسمى دينا، وكالكتب التي كان الحكماء يؤلفونها قديما في سياسة الرعية وإصلاح المدن فيحكم بها ملوك من لا شرع لهم ، فإنه وإن كان الخالق لكل الأشياء هو الله ، إلا أن البشر لهم في هذه كسب. فإن قلت: يلزم حينئذ أن أحكام الفقه الاجتهادية ليست من الدين لأن البشر لهم فيها كسب، وإنما منه ما ورد نصا لا خلاف فيه · أجيب: بأنها من الدين قطعا وهي موضوع إلهي غاية الأمر أنها تخفى علينا، والمجتهد يُعاني إظهارها والاستدلال عليها بقواعد الشرع، ولا مدخل له في وضعها. الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (سائق) أي: باعث وحامل ؛ لأن المكلف إذا سمع ما يترتب على فعل الفرض من الثواب انساق إلى فعله، أو سمع ما يحصل له على فعل الحرام من العقاب انساق لتركه ، وهو فصل ثانٍ مخرجُ للأوضاع الإلهية غير السائقة كإنبات الأرض وإمطار السماء، كذا مثل جماعة . الشنواني . (۳) قوله: (باختيارهم) خرج القهري كالألم السائق للأنين رغما ، وفيه: أنه لا يلزم من هذا الوضع الهداية ، إذ قد يتخلف هذا الاختيار عمن أراد الله إضلاله. الأمير. (٤) قوله : (المحمود) هو منصوب على أنه معمول لـ (اختيار) أي : يسوقهم بسبب اختيارهم الأمر المحمود وهو الإسلام، وبالجر صفة لاختيار. فخرج به المذموم كالقدرة على الكفر ؛ فإنه وضع إلهي سائق لذوي العقول باختيارهم المذموم ، وكحب الدنيا فإنه وضع إلهي يبعث ذوي العقول على ترك الزكاة باختيارهم المذموم ، ومتى كان الاختيار محمودا لا يسوق إلا إلى خير ، فقوله: (إلى ما هو خير لهم ذكره توصلا لقوله: (بالذات). الشنواني . (ه) قوله : (بالذات والخير بالذات هو السعادة الأبدية، خرجت الأوضاع الإلهية السائقة لمجرد صلاح الدنيا كملكات الصنائع المخلوقة في الإنسان. الأمير . (1) قوله : (وضعها الله) اعلم أنَّ الحكم الشرعي كما تقدم يُطلق على النسب التامة التي وردت [؟]
Explanation of the meaning of the religion and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission (3) A divine institution (1), driving (2) those possessed of intellects, by their choice that is praiseworthy (4), to what is good for them essentially (5). That is: rulings placed by Allah (6) the Exalted for the servants, impelling to essential goodness, which is ... (1) His saying: ('divine') i.e., these instituted rulings are attributed to the Divine, who is Allah, and it is a differentia excluding human institutions, i.e., those acquired by their effort, such as political laws by which the governance of the world is conducted, like the science of household management, and industrial institutions like carpentry, which are not called religion, and like the books that philosophers used to compose in ancient times on ruling subjects and reforming cities, by which kings of those who have no revealed law judge. For although the Creator of all things is Allah, humans have acquisition in these. If you say: It follows that the ijtihad-based rulings of jurisprudence are not part of religion because humans have acquisition in them, rather what is from it is only that which is transmitted textually without disagreement. The answer: They are certainly from the religion and are a divine institution; the only matter is that they are hidden from us, and the mujtahid undertakes to manifest them and deduce them by the rules of the Law, and he has no entry in their institution. Al-Shanawani. (2) His saying: ('driving') i.e., impelling and motivating; because the responsible person, when he hears what is entailed by doing the obligatory act of reward, is driven to do it, or hears what comes to him from doing the forbidden act of punishment, is driven to abandon it. And it is a second differentia excluding divine institutions that are not driving, like causing vegetation to grow and sending down rain, as a group exemplified. Al-Shanawani. (3) His saying: ('by their choice') excludes the coercive, like pain that drives a person to moan involuntarily. And in it: guidance is not necessary from this institution, for this choice may fail to occur concerning whom Allah wills to misguide. Al-Amir. (4) His saying: ('the praiseworthy') is accusative as an object of 'choice', i.e., He drives them by their choice of the praiseworthy thing, which is Islam, and in the genitive, it is an adjective for 'choice'. Thus is excluded the blameworthy, like the ability to disbelieve; for it is a divine institution driving those possessed of intellects by their blameworthy choice, and like love of the world, for it is a divine institution that impels those possessed of intellects to abandon zakah by their blameworthy choice. And whenever the choice is praiseworthy, it does not drive except to good. So his saying: ('to what is good for them') was mentioned as a means to his saying: ('essentially'). Al-Shanawani. (5) His saying: ('essentially') and essential good is eternal happiness; thus are excluded divine institutions that drive merely for worldly benefit, like the crafts created in humans. Al-Amir. (1) His saying: ('placed by Allah') know that the legal ruling, as has preceded, is applied to the complete relations that have been transmitted [؟]
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the technical definition of 'al-din' as a divine institution (wad' ilahi) that drives people of intellect, by their own praiseworthy choice, towards what is essentially good for them. Each restrictive phrase ('divine', 'driving', 'to those of intellect', 'by their praiseworthy choice', 'to what is essentially good') serves as a differentia excluding other phenomena. The commentary by al-Shanawani and al-Amir explains each element: 'divine' excludes human-made institutions (political laws, crafts, philosophical books) and addresses an objection about ijtihad-based law, showing it is still divine in origin but discovered by jurists. 'Driving' excludes non-motivational divine acts like causing plants to grow. 'To those of intellect' excludes non-human or non-rational beings. 'By their choice' excludes coercion, and 'praiseworthy' excludes blameworthy choices like ability for kufr or love of dunya. 'Essentially good' means eternal happiness, excluding worldly benefits. The passage emphasizes that religion is not merely any law but specifically a divine law that guides rational beings by their own free will to ultimate salvation.

سائق
ذوو العقول
اختيار محمود
خير بالذات
سعادة أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ijtihad-based rulings are not divine because humans exert effort, so they are not part of din.
Correction: They are indeed part of din because they are based on divine sources; the mujtahid only reveals what is already divinely instituted.

Checkpoint
What is excluded by the term 'إلهي' (divine) in the definition of din?
A) Laws derived from ijtihad
B) Human-made institutions like political laws and crafts
C) Non-coercive acts
D) Acts that lead to worldly benefit

Key Takeaway
The technical definition of din as a divine institution driving rational beings by their praiseworthy choice to eternal good precisely distinguishes true religion from human systems and underscores the roles of divine origin, free will, and ultimate salvation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily religious practices, how many of them are driven by a praiseworthy choice that seeks eternal good, and how many are merely inherited habits or social conformity? Will you honestly identify one practice this week that you can renew with conscious intention for Allah's pleasure?
?
The definition excludes blameworthy choices like the ability to disbelieve or love of the world. Is there any area in your life where you are using your free will to pursue worldly benefit while calling it religion? What would it cost you to realign that choice toward essential good?
?
If ijtihad-based rulings are still divine, how do you treat scholarly interpretations that differ from your own? Do you recognize them as part of Allah's religion even when they challenge your comfort? Can you obey a ruling from a scholar without resentment, knowing it is a divine institution?

Understanding al-Haqq and al-Sidq: Truth and Veracity
Commentator defines and distinguishes al-haqq and al-sidq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-haqq and al-sidq, their common ground in correspondence with reality, and the subtle distinction in the direction of correspondence.

Core Idea
Truth and veracity both mean correspondence with reality, but they differ in where the correspondence begins.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks two key terms in Islamic theology and logic: al-haqq (truth) and al-sidq (veracity). While both involve a match between a statement and reality, scholars have identified a nuanced difference based on etymology and usage. This lesson will guide you through their definitions and the reasoning behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator defines both terms and highlights their common core.
Pay attention to the term 'mutābaqah' (correspondence).
2
Identify the shared definition
Both al-haqq and al-sidq are defined as correspondence between a judgment (ḥukm) and reality (wāqiʻ). This is their essential commonality.
3
Understand the distinction
The commentators differed in emphasis: for al-haqq, the starting point is reality; for al-sidq, it is the judgment. This directional difference arises from etymology.
Al-haqq derives from 'ḥaqqa' (to be established), which applies primarily to reality.
4
Explore the alternative view
Some scholars considered them identical, but the passage explains why the majority distinguished them based on usage and derivation.
The alternative view is mentioned but not preferred in this commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين، وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته
(۲)
والمذاهب ، باعتبار اشتمالها عليه (١) ، وضده : الباطل (٢) .
=
التحقق ، والحكم في هذا الاعتبار جعل أصلا ثانيا حتى قيس به الواقع والصفتان متلازمتان؛ لأن المفاعلة للمشاركة، ولا تفاوت بين الحق والصدق إلا بما تقدم، ويقال هذا كلام صدق أي: مطابق للواقع، وهذا كلام حق أي مطابق للواقع، أي: إن ما أفاده الكلام مطابق للواقع، فالأسهل أنهما شيء واحد وهو مطابق الخبر للواقع، فالواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيره ولا يقاس على غيره، فتلاحظ أن غيره هل طابقه أو لا، لا أنه هل طابق غيره أو لا وإن كانت المفاعلة من الجانبين، ألا ترى أنك تقول: جالس الوزير السلطان، ولا تقول: جالس السلطان الوزير الشنواني. (۱) اعلم أنَّ العلماء النَّحارير فسروا الحقِّ بأنه: «الحكم الذي طابقه الواقع» وجعلوه ضد الباطل الذي يفسر بأنه: «الحكم الذي خالفه الواقع وعلى هذا تكون إضافة (مطابقة) إلى (الحكم) في كلام الشَّارح من إضافة المصدر إلى مفعوله، ويكون قوله: (الواقع) مرفوعا على أنه فاعل المصدر. وفسروا الصدق بأنه: «الحكم الذي طابق الواقع وجعلوا ضده الكذب، الذي يُفسر بأنه: الحكم الذي خالف الواقع ففي كل من الحقِّ والصدق تطابق بين الحكم الذي أفاده الكلام والواقع، وتطابق بين الواقع والحكم الذي أفاده الكلام؛ لأن المطابقة مُفاعلة من الجانبين، يصنع كل واحد من المرفوع على أنه فاعل، والمنصوب على أنه مفعول الآخر به، إلّا أنهم لما أرادوا أن يفرقوا بين الاثنين اللذين هما: «الحق المفاعلة أن ومعنى بالآخر مثل صنيع والصدق جعلوا ابتداء المطابقة في جانب الحق من جهة الواقع، وابتداء المطابقة في جانب الصدق من جهة الحكم والذي دعاهم إلى هذا هو النظر في وضع الكلمتين «الحق» والصدق فإنَّهم رأوا أن الحق مأخوذ من قولهم: «حق الشيء» إذا ثبت، والثابت إنما هو الواقع؛ فناسب اعتبار مبدأ المطابقة في جانب الحق من جهة الواقع، فهما في هذا الرأي متفقان ذاتا مختلفان اعتباراً، واختار بعض المحققين أنَّ الحق والصدق شيء واحد، د، وأنَّ كل واحد منهما عبارة عن (مطابقة الخبر للواقع، وذلك لأن الواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيره. محي. (۲) قوله: ( وضده الباطل) أي: يضاده إطلاقا واستعمالا، فهو عدم مطابقة الحكم الواقع، فيقال: قول باطل ودين باطل، أي: لم يطابق الواقع. فقولك: الله معدوم له نسبة كلامية، وهي ثبوت العدم الله تعالى والنسبة الواقعية ثبوت الوجود، فالنسبة الكلامية غير مطابقة للنسبة الواقعية، فعدم المطابقة هو الباطل. الشنواني.
Statement of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. (2) And the sects, in consideration of their inclusion of it, and its opposite is falsehood. = Verification, and the judgment in this consideration is made a second principle such that reality is measured by it, and the two attributes are inseparable; because mutual interaction requires participation, and there is no difference between truth (al-haqq) and veracity (al-sidq) except what has preceded. It is said: 'This is speech of veracity,' meaning corresponding to reality, and 'This is speech of truth,' meaning corresponding to reality; i.e., that what the speech conveys corresponds to reality. So the easiest is that they are one thing, which is the correspondence of the report to reality. So reality is something fixed in itself, by which other things are measured and it is not measured by others. So you observe whether other things correspond to it or not, not whether it corresponds to others or not, even though mutual interaction is from both sides. Do you not see that you say: 'The minister sat with the sultan,' and you do not say: 'The sultan sat with the minister'? Al-Shanawāni. (1) Know that the penetrating scholars interpreted truth (al-haqq) as 'the judgment which reality corresponds to,' and they made it opposite falsehood (al-bāṭil), which is interpreted as 'the judgment which reality contradicts.' According to this, the addition of 'correspondence' to 'judgment' in the commentator's words is the addition of the verbal noun to its object, and his word 'reality' is in the nominative as the agent of the verbal noun. And they interpreted veracity (al-sidq) as 'the judgment which corresponds to reality,' and they made its opposite falsehood (al-kidhb), which is interpreted as 'the judgment which contradicts reality.' So in both truth and veracity there is a correspondence between the judgment conveyed by speech and reality, and a correspondence between reality and the judgment conveyed by speech; because correspondence is a mutual interaction from both sides, each of the two (the subject and object) acts like the other. However, when they wanted to distinguish between the two, which are 'truth' and 'veracity', they made the beginning of correspondence in the case of truth from the side of reality, and the beginning of correspondence in the case of veracity from the side of judgment. What led them to this is consideration of the usage of the two words 'truth' and 'veracity': they saw that truth is derived from the saying 'the thing is true' when it is established, and the established is only reality; so it was appropriate to consider the beginning of correspondence in the case of truth from the side of reality. Thus, in this view they agree in essence but differ in consideration. And some investigators chose that truth and veracity are one thing, and that each of them is an expression of 'the correspondence of the report to reality,' because reality is something fixed in itself by which other things are measured. Al-Miḥyī. (2) His saying 'and its opposite is falsehood' means that it opposes it in usage and application; it is the non-correspondence of the judgment to reality. So it is said: 'A false statement' and 'A false religion,' i.e., it does not correspond to reality. For your saying 'God is nonexistent' has a verbal relation, which is the affirmation of non-existence to God Almighty, while the real relation is the affirmation of existence. So the verbal relation does not correspond to the real relation, and non-correspondence is falsehood. Al-Shanawāni.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a detailed philological and theological explanation of the terms 'al-haqq' (truth) and 'al-sidq' (veracity), along with their opposite 'al-bāṭil' (falsehood). The commentator begins by stating that both terms refer to the correspondence of a judgment or report with reality (al-wāqi‘). He notes that the simplest view is that they are identical—both mean the report matches reality. He then introduces a subtle distinction: although correspondence is mutual (the report corresponds to reality and reality corresponds to the report), the direction of emphasis differs. For 'al-haqq', the starting point of correspondence is reality (al-wāqi‘)—the judgment is true because reality is fixed and the judgment aligns with it. For 'al-sidq', the starting point is the judgment—the speech is veracious because it matches reality. This distinction is rooted in etymology: 'haqq' derives from the verb 'ḥaqqa' meaning 'to be established,' and reality is that which is established; 'sidq' is linked to the act of truthful speech. The commentator then references a view that treats both as synonymous, adding that some later scholars (al-Muḥaqqiqūn) considered them identical. Finally, he explains 'al-bāṭil' as the absence of correspondence, giving the example of claiming God does not exist—a verbal statement that does not match the real state of affairs. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that reality is the fixed standard (aṣl) against which all judgments are measured.

Key Terms
الحق
الصدق
الباطل
الواقع
المطابقة
الكذب
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-haqq and al-sidq are completely interchangeable without any nuance.
Correction: While they share the core meaning of correspondence with reality, scholars distinguish them by the direction of correspondence: al-haqq emphasizes reality as the standard, al-sidq emphasizes the speech's conformity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is al-haqq distinguished from al-sidq?
A) In al-haqq, correspondence begins from reality; in al-sidq, it begins from the judgment.
B) Al-haqq applies only to God; al-sidq applies only to human speech.
C) Al-haqq refers to past events; al-sidq refers to future events.
D) They are completely identical with no distinction at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-haqq and al-sidq both denote correspondence between speech and reality, but al-haqq emphasizes reality as the standard, while al-sidq emphasizes the speech's conformity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you ensure that your words correspond to reality (sidq), or do you expect reality to conform to your words (haqq)? Which attitude reflects your daily conversations? This lesson challenges you to examine the direction of correspondence in your own speech. Write down one specific instance today where you can consciously align your words with reality.
?
If truth (al-haqq) originates from reality, and veracity (al-sidq) from your statement, how does this affect your understanding of your responsibility towards truth? Are you more concerned with being right (haqq) or being truthful (sidq)? Reflect on a recent situation where you insisted on being right but may have distorted reality. What would it mean to prioritize sidq over haqq?
?
The passage mentions that some scholars consider haqq and sidq identical. But if they are identical, does that eliminate any excuse for not being truthful? How does this lesson confront the excuses you might make for not being completely honest?
