Three Scholarly Opinions on the Word Rabb
Commentator presents three grammatical analyses of the word Rabb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three grammatical analyses of the word 'Rabb' and the theological objections to each.

Core Idea
The word 'Rabb' carries deep grammatical and theological implications.

The word 'Rabb' (Lord) is central to Islamic theology. Scholars have debated its grammatical form, with each analysis carrying specific implications for how we understand God's attributes. This passage presents three scholarly opinions, along with the objections raised against each, culminating in the preferred view of the verifiers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation to understand the arguments and counterarguments.
Pay attention to how each opinion is introduced and critiqued.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists three opinions at the end: descriptive adjective, verbal noun, and active participle. Notice how each is defined and the evidence provided.
The first opinion is from al-Kashshaf, the second is attributed to many scholars, the third is from the verifiers.
3
Understand the objections
For the first opinion, the objection is that the verb is transitive. For the second, it leads to an ugly exaggeration. The third opinion avoids both issues.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why the grammatical analysis matters for theology. How does calling Allah 'Rabb' as an active participle differ from the other options?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ بعض العلماء ذهب إلى أنَّ «ربا» صفة مشبهة، وهو الذي يُشعر به كلام صاحب «الكشاف»، وقد اعترض على هذا بأنَّ الفعل المستعمل من هذه المادة متعد، قالوا: ربَّه يَربُّه، بمعنى أمره وتعبّده، ومنه قول صفوان بن أمية بن الخلف الجُمَحِيِّ لأبي سفيان يوم حرب حنين ـ وقد رأى أنَّ أبا سفيانَ سَرَّهُ أنْ تتغلب هوازن على المسلمين ـ الأَنْ يَرُبَّنِي رجلٌ مِن قريش خيرٌ مِن أنْ يَرُبَّني رجلٌ مِن هوازن، وقد عُلم أنَّ الصفة المشبهة لا تشتق من الفعل اللازم، ولهذا اضطرَّ شُرَّاح «الكشَّافِ» إلى الاعتذار عما يفيده ظاهر كلام الزمخشري بأنَّا نقدر نقل الفعل المتعدي إلى اللازم ثم نشتق منه الصفة المشبهة، وذهب قوم إلى أن (ربا) مصدر بمعنى التربية على مثال ما قاله الشارح، قال شيخ الإسلام: «ووقع في عبارة كثير من العلماء أنه مصدر بمعنى التربية، وهو تبليغ الشيء شيئًا فشيئًا إلى الحد الذي أراده المربي، أطلق عليه تعالى مبالغة بدعوى أنه تعالى هو عين التربية، ولا يخفى ما فيه من البشاعة اهـ. ولما رأى المحققون أنَّ كونه صفة مشبهة بمعنى: مالك؛ لا يخلو عن تَعسُّف، وأنَّ كونه مصدرًا بمعنى التّربية؛ لا يخلو عن سوء أدب، ذكروا أنه اسم فاعل خُفِّف بحذف ألفه، وأصله (رابب) بمعنى مُربي أو بمعنى مالك، ونظيره (بر) حيث ذكروا أن أصله (بارر) ثم حذفت الألف وأُدغم المِثلان، فتلخص أنَّ للعلماء في كلمة (رب) ثلاثة أقوال: قيل: هي صفة مشبهة، وقيل: هي مصدر، وقيل: هي اسم فاعل. محي.
Know that some scholars held that "Rabb" is a descriptive adjective (ṣifa mushabbaha), and that is what the author of al-Kashshāf's words indicate. This was objected to on the basis that the verb derived from this root is transitive; they said: rabbahu yarubbuhu, meaning he commanded him and he worshipped him, and from this is the saying of Ṣafwān ibn Umayya ibn al-Khalaf al-Jumaḥī to Abū Sufyān on the day of the battle of Ḥunayn — when he saw that Abū Sufyān was pleased that Hawāzin would overcome the Muslims —: 'That a man from Quraysh should lord over me is better than that a man from Hawāzin should lord over me.' And it is known that the descriptive adjective is not derived from an intransitive verb, and for this reason the commentators of al-Kashshāf were forced to apologize for what the apparent meaning of Zamakhsharī's speech indicates, by saying we assume transferring the transitive verb to intransitive then derive the descriptive adjective from it. And a group went that (Rabb) is a verbal noun (maṣdar) meaning upbringing, on the example of what the commentator said. Shaykh al-Islām said: 'It occurs in the expression of many scholars that it is a maṣdar meaning upbringing, which is bringing a thing gradually to the limit desired by the upbringer; it is applied to Allah Almighty by way of exaggeration, claiming that He Almighty is the essence of upbringing, and the ugliness in that is not hidden.' And when the verifiers saw that its being a descriptive adjective meaning owner is not free from strain, and that its being a maṣdar meaning upbringing is not free from bad manners, they mentioned that it is an active participle (ism fāʿil) lightened by dropping its alif, and its original is (rābib) meaning upbringer or meaning owner, and its parallel is (barr) where they mentioned its original is (bārīr) then the alif was dropped and the two similar letters were assimilated. So it is concluded that for the scholars regarding the word (Rabb) there are three opinions: it is said it is a descriptive adjective, and it is said it is a verbal noun, and it is said it is an active participle.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the word 'Rabb' (Lord). It presents three scholarly opinions on its grammatical status and the objections to each. The passage begins by stating that some scholars consider 'Rabb' a sifa mushabbaha (descriptive adjective), as indicated by the author of al-Kashshaf. The objection is raised that the verb from this root is transitive, whereas a sifa mushabbaha is typically derived from intransitive verbs. An example from pre-Islamic poetry is given to show transitive usage. Commentators on al-Kashshaf attempt to resolve this by assuming the verb is transferred to intransitive before deriving the adjective. Another opinion holds that 'Rabb' is a masdar (verbal noun) meaning 'upbringing.' Shaykh al-Islam critiques this as implying an ugly exaggeration if applied to Allah. Because the first opinion involves strain and the second bad manners, the verifiers propose that 'Rabb' is an ism fa'il (active participle) originally 'raabib,' meaning 'upbringer' or 'owner,' lightened by dropping the alif, analogous to 'barr' from 'baarir.' The passage concludes with the three opinions: sifa mushabbaha, masdar, and ism fa'il.

Key Terms
رب
صفة مشبهة
مصدر
اسم فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The grammatical analysis of the word 'Rabb' is purely linguistic and has no theological significance.
Correction: Each grammatical analysis carries implications for how we understand God's attributes and names. The preferred view avoids theological pitfalls like exaggeration or strain.

Checkpoint
What is the third and preferred opinion on the grammatical status of the word 'Rabb'?
A) Descriptive adjective (sifa mushabbaha)
B) Verbal noun (masdar)
C) Active participle (ism fa'il)
D) Comparative noun (ism tafdil)

Key Takeaway
The word 'Rabb' is grammatically an active participle (ism fa'il) according to the preferred view, avoiding the theological problems of the other analyses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Rabb' in your daily prayers, do you consciously remember that He is the One who nurtures you gradually and owns you completely, or have you been repeating it without reflection? Starting today, pause at each 'Rabb' and intend that meaning – will you commit to this for the next week?
?
The scholars rejected one interpretation of 'Rabb' because it showed 'bad manners' towards Allah.Think about your own speech about Allah – are there phrases you use carelessly that might imply something unworthy of Him? What specific phrase will you correct starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even a single word of Allah's names required deep investigation to avoid error. How much effort have you personally invested in understanding the names of Allah you invoke? Will you set aside time this week to study the meaning of just one name – and how will you change your worship as a result?

Permissible Uses of the Word Rabb: Four Forms and Their Rulings
Commentator classifies four uses of 'Rabb' and their legal permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four grammatical forms of the word 'Rabb' and which are legally permissible to use for other than Allah.

Core Idea
The word 'Rabb' has four forms, but only the plural is legally permissible for others.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a critical nuance: how can the word 'Rabb' (Lord) be used for creation? The commentator classifies four grammatical forms and evaluates each for legal permissibility. Understanding this distinction prevents misuse of a divine title and clarifies the boundaries of Islamic theological language.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Note the four distinct forms mentioned.
The passage is dense; take your time to grasp the structure.
2
Identify the four forms and their rulings
List the four forms: plural, singular with idafa, singular bare (no 'al', no idafa), singular with 'al'. For each, note the commentator's ruling on permissibility for other than Allah.
The plural is clearly permissible; the singular with idafa has two opinions; the other two are impermissible.
3
Examine the evidence for singular with idafa
Note the hadith prohibiting 'my lord' for humans, and the counter-evidence from the Yusuf verses. Understand the answer that those verses were from previous law.
This shows how Quranic abrogation applies to linguistic usage.
4
Understand the rejection of poetic evidence
The commentator explicitly states that legal rulings (shar'i) are not derived from poetry, especially from pre-Islamic times. This is crucial for distinguishing legal from linguistic permissibility.
Poetic examples only support linguistic usage, not legal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يستعمل لفظ (رب) مفردًا ومجرَّداً مِن «أل» ومِن الإضافة، وقد يستعمل مفردًا مقرونا بـ«أل»؛ وقد يستعمل مفرداً مضافًا؛ وقد يستعمل جَمْعًا. فأَمَّا استعماله جمعاً، فيجوز أن يُطلق على غير الله تعالى، ومنه في القرآن الكريم: {ءَأَرْبَابٌ مُتَفَرِّقُونَ خَيْرٌ أَمِ اللَّهُ الْوَاحِدُ الْقَهَّارُ} [يوسف: 39]. وأما استعماله مفردًا مضافًا فقيل: يجوز أن يُطلق على غيره تعالى مطلقا، تقول: ربُّ الدَّار، وربُّ الإبل، بمعنى صاحبها؛ وقيل: إن أُضيف إلى العاقل لم يجز أن يطلق على غيره؛ وقد استُدلَّ لهذا القول بحديث رواه الشيخان عن أبي هريرة: «لا يقل أحدكم: أطعم ربَّك، وضى: ربَّك، ولا يقل أحد: ربي، وَلْيَقُلْ سيدي ومولاي...». ويعارض هذا قوله تعالى على لسان يوسف: {ارجع إلَى رَبِّكَ} [يوسف: 50]، وقوله على لسانه أيضا: {إنَّه رَبِّي أَحْسَنَ مَثْوَاىَ} [يوسف: 23]. ويمكن أن يُجاب عن الآيتين الكريمتين: بأنه شَرْعُ مَن قبلنا، وليس شرعًاً لنا إذا ورد في شرعنا ما يمنعه. وأمَّا استعماله مفردًا مجرَّداً مِن «أل» ومِن الإضافة؛ فالصواب أنه لا يجوز إطلاقه على غير الله تعالى؛ وأما قول النَّابِغَة الذبياني: نَحُبُّ إلى النُّعْمَانِ حَتَّى نَنَالَهُ فِدِّى لَكَ مِن رَبِّ طَرِيفِي وَتَالِدِي فلا يدل على الجواز؛ لأنَّ الأحكام الشرعية لا تؤخذ عن الشُّعراء خصوصاً أهل الجاهلية. وأما استعماله مفردًا مقرونا بـ «أل» فلا يجوز إطلاقه على غيره تعالى، وأما قول الحارث بن حِلَّزَة في المُنْذِرِ بن مَاءِ السَّماء: وَهُوَ الرَّبُّ والشَّهِيدُ عَلَى يَوْمِ الحِيارَيْنِ وَالبَلَاءُ بَلَاءُ فمثل قول النَّابِغة الذبياني المتقدم، لا يصح أن يكون مستندا على حكم شرعي، نعم؛ إنْ كان المراد جواز إطلاق هذا اللفظ أو ذاك عند أهل اللغة على كذا أو كذا صح الاستدلال بمثل قول النابغة والحارث، بل قول مثلهما في ذلك أولى أن يُستدلَّ به، وينبغي أن يكون المراد بالجواز في هذا الموضع الجواز الشَّرعي، لا الجواز اللغوي. محي.
The word (Rabb) may be used singular and bare of 'al' and of idafa, and it may be used singular with 'al', and it may be used singular in idafa, and it may be used plural. As for its usage plural, it is permissible to apply it to other than Allah Almighty, and from that in the Noble Qur'an: {Are many lords better or Allah the One, the Subduer?} [Yusuf: 39]. As for its usage singular in idafa, it is said: it is permissible to apply it to other than Him Almighty absolutely; you say: lord of the house, lord of the camels, meaning its owner. And it is said: if it is added to a rational being, it is not permissible to apply it to other than Him. And this saying was supported by the hadith narrated by the two shaykhs from Abu Hurayra: 'Let none of you say: Feed your lord, and let him not say: my lord, but let him say: my master.' And this is contradicted by the saying of Allah Almighty on the tongue of Yusuf: {Return to your lord} [Yusuf: 50], and His saying on his tongue also: {Indeed my lord has made good my stay} [Yusuf: 23]. And it can be answered concerning the two noble verses: that it is the law of those before us, and it is not a law for us if something prohibiting it occurs in our law. As for its usage singular bare of 'al' and of idafa, the correct view is that it is not permissible to apply it to other than Allah Almighty. As for the saying of al-Nabigha al-Dhubyani: 'We love al-Nu'man until we attain it, may I be ransomed for you from a lord of my recent and ancient possessions,' it does not indicate permissibility, because legal rulings are not taken from poets, especially those of the Jahiliyya. As for its usage singular with 'al', it is not permissible to apply it to other than Him Almighty. As for the saying of al-Harith ibn Hilliza concerning al-Mundhir ibn Ma' al-Sama': 'And He is the Lord and the Witness on the day of al-Hiyarayn, and tribulation is tribulation,' it is like the previous saying of al-Nabigha al-Dhubyani; it is not valid to be relied upon for a legal ruling. Yes, if what is meant is the permissibility of applying this word or that according to the linguists to such and such, then it is correct to use as evidence the like of the sayings of al-Nabigha and al-Harith, indeed the like of them in that is more appropriate to be used as evidence. And it should be that what is meant by permissibility in this context is legal permissibility, not linguistic permissibility.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage classifies the four ways the word 'Rabb' can be used: (1) plural, (2) singular with idafa (possession), (3) singular bare (without 'al' and without idafa), and (4) singular with 'al'. For each, it discusses whether it is permissible to use it for other than Allah. The passage first states that the plural form ('arbab') is permissible for others, citing the Quranic verse where idols are called 'arbab'. For the singular with idafa, there is a difference of opinion: some allow it absolutely (e.g., 'lord of the house'), while others restrict it to non-rational beings, citing the hadith prohibiting calling someone 'my lord' ('Rabi') for humans. The objection from the Yusuf verses is answered by saying those were previous laws, abrogated. For the singular bare form, it is not permissible for others; poetic examples are rejected as evidence because legal rulings are not based on poetry, especially from the Jahiliyya. For the singular with 'al', it is also not permissible for others; again poetic evidence is dismissed. The conclusion distinguishes legal permissibility from linguistic permissibility: the latter can be supported by poetry, but the former cannot.

Key Terms
رب
جواز شرعي
جواز لغوي
إضافة
أل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because pre-Islamic poets used 'Rabb' for humans, it must be legally permissible to do so.
Correction: Legal rulings are not derived from poetry; poetic usage indicates linguistic permissibility, not legal. The passage explicitly states that legal rulings (shar'i) are not taken from poets.

Checkpoint
Which usage of the word 'Rabb' is permissible to apply to other than Allah, according to the passage?
A) Singular with 'al'
B) Singular bare without 'al' and without idafa
C) Plural
D) Singular with idafa to a rational being

Key Takeaway
Only the plural form 'arbāb' is legally permissible for other than Allah; all singular forms are reserved for Allah or subject to dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you casually address someone as 'master' or 'lord' in your daily conversations, do you ever pause to consider that only Allah is truly ar-Rabb? How can you train your tongue to preserve His exclusive right to this title?
?
The passage distinguishes between legal and linguistic permissibility. In your heart, do you sometimes excuse careless speech about Allah because 'it's just a figure of speech'? What would it take for you to feel the weight of every word you utter concerning Him?
?
If a pre-Islamic poet could use the word 'Rabb' for a human, that doesn't make it right in Islam. But today, what modern phrases or titles do you hear that subtly attribute lordship to others? Will you commit to avoiding them, even if it feels awkward or unusual?

Understanding the Terms Al, Sahab, and Hizb
Author defines the terms al, sahab, and hizb with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the terms al, sahab, and hizb, including the expansive definition of companionship based on meeting the Prophet.

Core Idea
Companionship includes anyone who met the Prophet, even if blind or non-human.

This passage from the Sharh clarifies three key terms used in Islamic discourse: al (family), sahab (companions), and hizb (party). The author provides an expansive definition of companionship, including individuals who may not have physically seen the Prophet but met him in some way, and even extends it to angels. Understanding these definitions is crucial for correctly applying these terms in theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice how the author defines each term.
The passage is concise but packed with examples.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the three terms al, sahab, hizb and note the definitions given.
Pay special attention to the examples of sahab.
3
Understand the logic behind companionship
Reflect on why the author includes blind individuals, prophets like Jesus, and even angels. What is the criterion?
The criterion is meeting (liqa'), not sight or recognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الآل والصحب والحزب
فيدخلُ ابن أم مكتوم ونحوه من العميان؛ وعيسى والخضر وإلياس عليهم الصلاة والسلام؛ لحصول اللقاء؛ ولأنه لا يُشترط فيه التعارف؛ إذ لا تنافي بين مقام الصحبة والنبوة والملكية، فعيسى آخر الصحابة موتا، والملائكة صحابة باقون إلى الآن؛ لتكليفهم بشريعته.
(و) على (حزبه) أي: جماعته.
The meaning of 'al' (family), 'sahab' (companions), and 'hizb' (party). Thus, Ibn Umm Maktum and similar blind individuals enter (the definition), as well as Jesus, al-Khidr, and Elias (peace be upon them), due to the occurrence of meeting; and because acquaintance is not required in it, since there is no contradiction between the status of companionship and prophethood and angelic nature. So Jesus is the last of the companions to die, and the angels are companions remaining until now, because they are obligated by his Law. (And) 'upon his hizb', that is, his group.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines three terms: al (family), sahab (companions), and hizb (party). The author explains that 'sahab' includes anyone who met the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), even if blind like Ibn Umm Maktum, and even non-human figures like Jesus, al-Khidr, and Elias, because meeting does not require visual perception or mutual recognition. He then argues that there is no contradiction between being a prophet or an angel and being a companion, using Jesus as an example who died later than other companions, and angels who remain companions to this day because they are obligated by the Prophet's law. Finally, he interprets 'hizb' as meaning group or party.

Correction: The author clarifies that meeting (liqa') is sufficient, as demonstrated by the inclusion of the blind Ibn Umm Maktum and even non-human beings like angels.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The term 'sahab' (companion) is defined broadly by meeting the Prophet, regardless of visual perception or human status, and this even includes angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author includes Jesus among the companions, emphasizing that no status contradicts companionship. How does this lesson challenge you to expand your circle of brotherhood? Identify one person you have excluded from your prayers or concern, and include them today.

Definition of al-Bayan in Usul al-Din
Commentary defines bayan and compares with ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-bayan in the context of usul al-din and its distinction from ta'wil.

Core Idea
Al-bayan is the extraction from ambiguity to manifestation.

This lesson introduces the foundational term 'al-bayan' (clarification) as it functions in the science of usul al-din. The commentator explains why this art requires bayan and provides a technical definition, distinguishing it from ta'wil. You will learn how bayan serves as a method for proving theological issues with decisive evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the sequence from need to definition to distinction.
Pay special attention to Jurjani's two definitions.
2
Understand the need for bayan
The passage begins by stating that usul al-din needs bayan. Ask yourself: why does this science require clarification?
The answer is to depict issues and prove them with decisive evidence.
3
Grasp the definition of bayan
Identify the core definition: extraction from ambiguity to manifestation. Compare it with Jurjani's two definitions.
These definitions emphasize revealing what was hidden.
4
Distinguish bayan from ta'wil
The passage explains that ta'wil is for speech with no clear meaning at first glance, while bayan is for speech that is understood but obscure to some.
This distinction is crucial for proper interpretation of theological texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحامل للمصنف على جعل منظومته في علم أصول الدين ( يَحْتَاجُ ) أي : الفن المُلقَّبُ بأصول الدين ( لِلتَّبْيين) أي : التَّوضيح ؛ بتصوير مسائله وإثباتها بقواطع الأدلة والبيان: إخراج الشَّيء مِن حَيّز الإشكال إلى حيز التجلّي. عرَّف السيد الشريف الجرجاني البيان بتعريفين؛ أحدهما ما ذكره الشارح هنا، قال: البيان: إظهار المعنى وإيضاح ما كان مستورًا قبله، وقيل : هو الإخراج من حيز الإشكال، والفرق بين التأويل والبيان أن التأويل ما يذكر في كلام لا يفهم منه معنى محصل في أول وهلة، والبيان ما يذكر فيما يفهم ذلك لنوع خفاء بالنسبة إلى البعض . اهـ . محي. قوله: (من حيز الإشكال الحيز في الأصل اسم للمكان ، ثم إنه تجوز فيه هنا عن الصفة ، ثم إن جعلت الإضافة بيانية أي حيز هو الإشكال، وحيز هو التجلي. قال ابن قاسم: فإن قلت : الحيز في ذلك التعريف مجاز وهو ممتنع فيها. ويجاب: بأن التجوز في الحد لا مطلقا، بل يجوز عند وضوح المعنى مع فهم المراد ، ولعل استحالة ثبوت الحيز للمعاني كالإشكال والإيضاح قرينة على المقصود. اهـ. والحاصل: أن الحيّز إنما يكون للأمور المحسوسة ، فشبه الإشكال بجرم ذي حيّز على طريق الاستعارة بالكناية ، وإثباتُ الحيّز تخييل ، أو أنه مستعار للصفة استعارة تصريحية ، والإضافة للبيان ، وكذا يقال فيما بعده ، ولك أن تجعله من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه بجامع الاشتمال ، فالحيز مستعمل في حقيقته. فإن قلت ما معنى إخراج الشيء من الإشكال إلى التجلي . قلت المراد إزالة الإشكال عينه. الشنواني.
The reason that moved the author to make his poem in the science of the principles of religion (he needs), i.e., the art called the principles of religion, for clarification, i.e., elucidation, by depicting its issues and proving them with decisive evidences. And clarification (al-bayan): extracting something from the realm of ambiguity to the realm of manifestation. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani defined bayan with two definitions; one of them is what the commentator mentioned here: he said: bayan is revealing the meaning and making clear what was hidden before it, and it was said: it is extraction from the realm of ambiguity. And the difference between interpretation (ta’wil) and bayan is that ta’wil is what is mentioned in speech from which no consolidated meaning is understood at first glance, while bayan is what is mentioned in speech that is understood but with a kind of obscurity for some. End quote. Mayh. His saying: (from the realm of ambiguity) – al-hayz in origin is a name for place, then it is used metaphorically here for attribute. Then if the idafa is made bayaniyya, i.e., a realm that is ambiguity, and a realm that is manifestation. Ibn Qasim said: If you say: al-hayz in that definition is a metaphor and that is impossible in definitions. The answer is: using figurative language in a definition is not absolutely prohibited; rather it is permissible when the meaning is clear and the intent is understood. Perhaps the impossibility of assigning a hayz to meanings such as ambiguity and clarification is a clue to the intended meaning. End quote. The conclusion: al-hayz is only for physical things, so ambiguity is likened to a physical body having a hayz by way of implicit metaphor, and affirming the hayz is imaginary, or it is borrowed for the attribute by explicit metaphor, and the idafa is for clarification. The same is said for what follows. You may also consider it as the addition of the object of comparison to the subject of comparison by virtue of inclusion, so hayz is used in its real sense. If you say: what is the meaning of extracting something from ambiguity to manifestation? I say: the intent is the removal of ambiguity itself. Al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-bayan' (clarification) as it is used in the science of usul al-din (principles of religion). The commentator begins by stating that the art of usul al-din stands in need of clarification (al-tabyin), which means elucidation through depicting its issues and proving them with decisive evidences. He then provides a more technical definition: al-bayan is the extraction of something from the realm of ambiguity to the realm of manifestation. This is followed by a citation from al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, who gives two definitions of al-bayan: (1) revealing the meaning and clarifying what was previously hidden, and (2) extraction from ambiguity. Al-Jurjani distinguishes al-bayan from ta'wil (interpretation): ta'wil is used when the speech yields no consolidated meaning at first glance, while al-bayan is used when the speech is understood but remains somewhat obscure to some. The commentator then explains the term 'hayz' (realm) in the definition. Originally, hayz means a physical place, but here it is used metaphorically for an attribute. He discusses the grammatical structure (idafa bayaniyya) and addresses an objection from Ibn Qasim about the impossibility of using a metaphor in a definition. The answer is that figurative language is permissible in definitions when the meaning is clear. The commentator concludes that hayz is properly for physical objects, so the use in 'realm of ambiguity' is a metaphor: ambiguity is likened to a body having a place, and the affirmation of hayz is a poetic image. Alternatively, hayz can be considered a loanword for the attribute. Finally, he clarifies that the phrase 'extracting something from ambiguity to manifestation' simply means removing ambiguity itself.

Key Terms
البيان
أصول الدين
التبيين
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-bayan' simply means 'explanation' without the technical nuance of removing ambiguity as a methodological step in kalam.
Correction: In kalam, bayan specifically refers to the extraction from ambiguity to manifestation, a precise term with a defined scope.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between ta'wil and bayan?
A) Ta'wil is used in legal rulings, bayan in creedal matters.
B) Ta'wil is used when speech yields no consolidated meaning at first glance; bayan is used when speech is understood but obscure to some.
C) Ta'wil is a synonym for bayan.
D) Ta'wil is for ambiguous verses, bayan for clear verses.

Key Takeaway
Al-bayan is a specialized term meaning extraction from ambiguity, distinct from ta'wil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face ambiguity in a religious text, do you actively seek clear, evidence-based clarification (bayan) or do you lean towards personal interpretation (ta'wil)? How does your answer reflect your commitment to sound methodology?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological issue as 'complicated' without patiently working to extract its meaning from decisive proofs? What does that reveal about your diligence in seeking truth?
?
This lesson distinguishes bayan (clarification of what is partially understood) from ta'wil (interpretation of what is initially incomprehensible). Which of these describes your typical approach to unfamiliar Islamic concepts, and what change might be necessary?

The Metaphor of al-Hayz in the Definition of al-Bayan
Explanation of the metaphor of hayz and defense of its use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'hayz' is used metaphorically in the definition of bayan and why the use of metaphor in definitions is permissible.

Core Idea
Hayz is used metaphorically for attributes to clarify bayan's function.

This lesson delves into the metaphorical use of 'hayz' (realm) in the definition of 'al-bayan'. The commentator explains the original meaning of hayz as place, its metaphorical extension to attributes, and addresses a potential objection about the use of metaphor in definitions. Understanding this helps appreciate the precision of theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hayz
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the discussion of hayz. Note the original meaning and the metaphorical use.
The text discusses the idafa construction and the objection by Ibn Qasim.
2
Understand the metaphor and objection
Hayz is originally a place, but here used for ambiguity. Ibn Qasim objects that metaphor is impossible in definitions. Ask yourself: why might he think that?
The objection is based on a strict view of definitions requiring literal language.
3
Grasp the resolution
The answer states that metaphor is permissible when the meaning is clear. The commentator provides two interpretations: implicit metaphor or explicit metaphor, and concludes that hayz can be used in its real sense through the addition of the object of comparison.
This shows flexibility in theological language.

?

?

8 min
Are Book Names Generic or Personal Proper Nouns?
The commentator discusses whether 'arjuza' is a proper noun or generic name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether names of books (like 'arjuza') are generic or personal proper nouns, and the grammatical implications of this debate.

Core Idea
The naming of a poem reveals deeper assumptions about language and reality.

In this lesson, we examine a theological-linguistic objection: is the word 'arjuza' a generic term for any similar poem, or does it uniquely refer to the author's poem? The commentator defends the latter view and then critiques his own commentator's grammatical solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
First, read the passage and identify the objector's claim: that 'arjuza' is a generic proper noun (or generic name), implying it could apply to any poem of that meter.
The objector thinks book names are like generic proper nouns (أعلام الأجناس).
2
See the chosen answer
The commentator rejects the objection and states that book names are personal proper nouns (أعلام شخصية), so 'arjuza' refers only to the poem in the author's mind.
This is the position the commentator chooses.
3
Analyze the critique of the response
The commentator then critiques the earlier commentator's answer, arguing that the implied construct (تقدير المضاف) should be placed before the predicate (خبر), not before the subject (مبتدأ), using the analogy of removing shoes before reaching the riverbank.
This shows the depth of grammatical reasoning behind naming disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصله: أنا لا نسلم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره؛ لأن هذا أسماء الكتب من قبيل أعلام الأجناس ، أو أسماء الأجناس ، ونحن لا نقول به ، بل الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وحده بناء على أنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل الأعلام الشخصية، وهو ما نختاره. بقي هنا شيء، وهو أَنَّا لا نرتضي جواب الشارح على الاعتراضين؛ حتى على فرض تسليم كل ما جاء في كلام المعترض ، وذلك لأنه جعل تقدير المضاف قبل المبتدأ، والذي نختاره على هذا الفرض أن نجعل تقدير المضاف قبل الخبر؛ فيكون التقدير: (وهذه الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مجمل جنس أرجوزة... الخ) لأنَّ التقدير في الأول يكون بمنزلة نزع الخُف قبل الوصول لشاطئ النهر كما ذكره المحقق الخَيَّالي ، فإنَّ أول الكلام وقع موقعه ، ولم يأت الاعتراض إلا بسبب آخره ؛ فليكن التقدير في آخره.
And the summary: we do not accept that the 'arjuza' is a name for what is in the mind of the author and others, because this opinion considers names of books as types of generic proper nouns or generic names, and we do not accept that. Rather, the 'arjuza' is a name for what is in the mind of the author alone, based on the view that names of books are personal proper nouns, and this is what we choose. There remains something here: we do not approve of the commentator's response to the two objections; even assuming all that the objector said is accepted, because he placed the implied construct (taqdir al-mudaf) before the subject (mubtada'), whereas we choose on this assumption to place the implied construct before the predicate (khabar); so the estimation would be: 'These words present in the mind are the summary of the genus of arjuza... etc.' Because the estimation in the first place is like removing the shoe before reaching the riverbank, as mentioned by the investigator al-Khayyali. The beginning of the speech is in its proper place, and the objection only came because of its end; so let the estimation be at its end.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding whether the word 'arjuza' refers to a specific poem in the author's mind or is a general term. The objector holds that book names are generic proper nouns, so 'arjuza' could apply to any similar poem. The answer argues that book names are personal proper nouns, so 'arjuza' refers only to the author's poem. Then the commentator critiques the commentator's response, suggesting an alternative grammatical estimation to resolve the objection without disturbing the beginning of the sentence.

Hope and Acceptance: Defining Key Terms in the Author's Supplication
Definitions of hope and acceptance with clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of hope (ar-rajā') and acceptance (al-qubūl) as used in kalam, including their conditions and nuances.

Core Idea
Hope requires effort; acceptance requires contentment without objection.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks two crucial terms from the opening supplication of the poem: hope and acceptance. These are not merely emotional states but carry specific theological meanings. Understanding them correctly prevents common misunderstandings about reliance on God and the nature of divine reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the verse line at the beginning sets up the supplication.
2
Examine the definition of hope
The commentator defines hope as the heart's attachment to a future good while taking the means. Note that without effort it becomes blameworthy covetousness.
This distinguishes Islamic hope from mere wishful thinking.
3
Examine the definition of acceptance
Acceptance is defined in two ways: being pleased without objection, and rewarding correct action. Note the interdependence of these two interpretations.
The condition of leaving objection is emphasized to exclude displeasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والله أَرْجُو فِي القَبُول نَافِعًا بها مُرِيْدًا فِي الثَّوَابِ طَامِعا. وَاللهَ أَرْجُو فِي حُصول القَبُولِ والرَّجَاءُ عُرفًا: تَعلَّق القلب بمرغوب في حصوله في المستقبل، مع الأخذ في أسباب الحصول. (٢) قوله: (مع الأخذ في الأسباب أما مع ترك الأسباب فطمع ، فخرج الطمع المذموم كأن يطلب الرحمة وينهمك في المعاصي. والقبولُ للشَّيء : الرّضا به، مع ترك الاعتراض(۱) على فاعله، وقيل: (٢) الإثابة (۲) على العمل الصحيح. (1) قوله: (مع ترك الاعتراض لعل أصل العبارة بمعنى (ترك الاعتراض) - أي: إبدال (مع) بـ (ترك) - تفسير للرضا، وقال شيخ شيوخنا القطب الملوي: إنما زاد هذا القيد لأن الرضا قد يكون مع الاعتراض، أي: ولو بوجه ما ، ولذلك قال ابن مالك: (وتقتضي رضا بغير سخط وأراد بالاعتراض اللوم ، فإذا وجد لومٌ فلا يكون مقبولا ، ورضا الله كناية عن إنعامه وإرادته . الشنواني . (۲) قوله (الإثابة) هذا تفسير ثان للقبول ، وهو وما قبله متلازمان الشنواني.
And I hope from Allah for acceptance, benefiting with it, desiring, for reward, hoping. And I hope from Allah for obtaining acceptance. And hope (ar-rajā') conventionally is: the heart's attachment to something desired in its future attainment, while taking the means of attainment. (2) His statement: 'with taking the means' - as for leaving the means, it is covetousness (tama'), so blameworthy covetousness is excluded, such as one who seeks mercy while persisting in sins. And acceptance (al-qubūl) of a thing is: being pleased with it, while leaving objection (1) against its doer, and it is said: (2) rewarding (al-ithāba) for correct action. (1) His statement: 'while leaving objection' - perhaps the original expression is 'leaving objection' - i.e., substituting 'with' for 'leaving' - is an interpretation of being pleased. And the master of our masters, al-Qutb al-Malawi, said: He only added this restriction because being pleased may occur with objection, i.e., even in some manner. Therefore Ibn Malik said: 'It necessitates being pleased without displeasure.' And he meant by objection blame, so if blame exists it is not accepted. And Allah's being pleased is a metaphor for His bestowal and will. Al-Shanwani. (2) His statement: 'rewarding' is a second interpretation of acceptance, and it and the previous are interdependent. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in the author's supplication: ar-rajā' (hope) and al-qubūl (acceptance). The definition of hope is given as the heart's attachment to a desired outcome in the future while actively taking the means to achieve it. The footnote clarifies that hope without effort is blameworthy covetousness. Then acceptance is defined first as being pleased with something without objecting to its doer, and second as rewarding correct action. The footnotes explain that the condition of leaving objection is necessary because one could be pleased while still objecting, and that acceptance and rewarding are interdependent.

Benefiting from the Poem: Objection and Answers
The commentator defines 'benefit' and addresses an objection about the pronoun's referent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defines 'benefit' as the opposite of harm and resolves the objection that the pronoun in 'bihā' refers to words while benefit comes from meanings.

Core Idea
A pronoun can refer to words or meanings; context and rhetorical usage resolve apparent contradictions.

In this short passage, the commentator clarifies the term nāfi‘an (benefiting) in the supplication and then tackles a subtle objection about the pronoun 'bihā'. The objection seems to create a problem: the poem is words, but the benefit is from meanings. How can the pronoun work? The commentator offers two elegant solutions from Arabic rhetorical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of benefit
Read the definition: benefit is the opposite of harm, that by which ease and assistance are obtained. Notice the lexical note about the word 'harm' being pronounced with fatḥ or ḍamma.
This is background for the objection.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the urjūza (poem) is the mere words, but the hoped-for benefit is from its meanings, not its words. So the pronoun 'bihā' seems inconsistent. Why is this a problem? Because the natural reading would be that the pronoun refers to the poem as words.
Classical Arabic poetry and theological texts often separate words and meanings.
3
Learn the two answers
The commentator gives two answers: (1) deletion of a genitive – i.e., the pronoun refers to 'its content' or 'its signification'; (2) istikhdām (rhetorical usage) – the word urjūza is used first to mean the words, and the pronoun refers back to it with the meaning of the meanings. Compare the two and see how they preserve the linguistic consistency.
Istikhdām is a recognized figure where a word is used in two different senses in the same context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نافعًا) حالٌ مِن الاسم الكريم ، والنَّفْعُ : ضِدُّ الضَّرَّ (۳) ، يُطلق على مَا يحصل به رِفقٌ ومعُونةٌ ، وضمير (بهَا) للأُرجوزة ، أو الجوهرة (٤) . (۳) قوله : ( والنفع ضد الضر النفع ما يتوصل به إلى الخير والضر: ما يتوصل به إلى الشر. وهو بفتح الضاد مصدر ، وبضمها اسم مصدر الشنواني. (٤) اعتُرِضَ على هذا التعبير بأنَّ الأرجوزة على ما تقدَّم اسم للألفاظ ، والنفع المرجو منها إنما يكون بمعانيها لا بألفاظها! ويجاب على هذا الاعتراض بأحد جوابين: الأول : أنَّ الكلام في قوله : (بها) على حذف مضاف أي بمضمونها أو بمدلولها ، ونحو ذلك . والثاني: أنَّ في الكلام استخداماً ، وذلك أَنه ذكر الأرجوزة أولا بمعنى الألفاظ ، وأعاد الضمير عليها بمعنى المعاني .
['Benefiting' (nāfi‘an)] is a circumstantial accusative from the noble name [Allah], and benefit (an-naf‘u) is the opposite of harm (aḍ-ḍarr) (3), it is applied to that by which ease and assistance are obtained. And the pronoun 'bihā' refers to the urjūza [poem] or the jawhara [gem] (4). (3) His statement: 'and benefit is the opposite of harm: benefit is that by which good is attained, and harm is that by which evil is attained. It is with fath on the ḍād as a verbal noun, and with ḍamma as a noun of source. Al-Shanwani.' (4) An objection was raised against this expression that the urjūza, as previously stated, is a name for the words, and the benefit hoped for from it is only through its meanings, not its words! This objection is answered with one of two answers: First: that the speech in his saying 'bihā' involves deletion of a genitive, i.e., 'by its content' or 'by its signification', and the like. Second: that there is rhetorical usage (istikhdām) in the speech, that he mentioned the urjūza first meaning the words, and then referred back to it with the pronoun meaning the meanings.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'nāfi‘an' (benefiting) in the supplication and addresses an objection about the pronoun 'bihā'. First, the definition of benefit as the opposite of harm, used for that which brings ease and assistance. Then it identifies the referent of 'bihā' as the urjūza or jawhara. The objection points out that the urjūza is the wording, but benefit comes from its meanings, not its words. Two answers are given: either 'bihā' implies an omitted genitive such as 'by its content', or it involves the rhetorical figure of istikhdām, where the word is used first in one sense (words) and the pronoun refers back to it in another sense (meanings).

Classification of Premises of Speculative Reasoning
Classification of premises and their epistemic outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the premises of speculative reasoning (nazar) are classified into certain and conjectural, and how each type leads to a different cognitive outcome: knowledge, belief, or opinion.

Core Idea
The epistemic status of premises determines whether thinking yields knowledge, belief, or mere opinion.

Before engaging with theological arguments, it is essential to understand what makes a proof valid or merely probable. Sanusi explains that the premises we use in reasoning can be certain or conjectural, and even certainty can be based on evidence or mere imitation. This classification clarifies why some arguments produce certain knowledge while others yield only belief or opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: classification then examples.
The passage uses technical terms like 'nazar', 'majzum biha', 'maznun biha'.
2
Identify the two main categories
Look for where the commentator divides premises into certain and conjectural. Under certain, note the two sources: evidence and blind acceptance.
3
Understand the outcomes
Each type of premise leads to a distinct mental state: knowledge (ilm), belief (i'tiqad), or opinion (zann). Match each example to its outcome.
The example of the thief illustrates opinion from conjectural premises.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why certainty from blind acceptance is not knowledge. The passage guards against this confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ مقدمات النَّظر ، أي : الدَّليل إما أن تكون مجزوما بها ، وإما أن تكون مظنونةٌ . والأوَّل: إما أن يكون سبب الجزم هو الاستدلال عليها ، وإما أن يكون سبب الجزم هو التقليد. وإِنَّما من يكون الفكر موصلا إلى علم ، إذا كانت مقدماته مجزوما بها عن دليل، كقولك: «العالم متغيّر وكل متغيّر حادث ، إذا قلت هذا وأنت تعرف أنَّ دليلَ الصُّغرى : المشاهدة على ما قلنا قبل ، ودليل الكبرى : أنه يستحيل أن يطرأ العدمُ على القديم، ويكون الفكر موصلا إلى اعتقاد إن كانت مقدماته مجزوما بها عن تقليد لا عن نظر ، كقولك: «العالم حادث وكلُّ حادث فلا بدَّ له مِن محدث»، إذا قلت ذلك مقلدا ، ولكنَّك جازم بكلِّ مقدِّمة من المقدمتين ، وإِنَّما يكون الفكر موصلا إلى الظَّنّ إذا كانت مقدماته أو بعضها مظنونا بها كقولك: «هذا رجل يدور بالسلاح في الليل، فهو لص».
Know that the premises of speculative reasoning (al-nazar), i.e., the proof (al-dalil), are either certain (majzum biha) or conjectural (maznun biha). The first: either the cause of certainty is inference (al-istidlal) upon them, or the cause of certainty is blind acceptance (al-taqlid). Indeed, thought (al-fikr) leads to knowledge (ilm) only if its premises are certain by way of evidence, as when you say: 'The world is changing and everything changing is originated (hadith),' when you say this and you know that the proof of the minor premise is observation (al-mushahada) as we said before, and the proof of the major premise is that it is impossible for non-existence to occur upon the eternal (al-qadim). And thought leads to belief (i'tiqad) if its premises are certain by way of blind acceptance not by way of inference, as when you say: 'The world is originated and every originated thing necessarily has an originator,' if you say that as a blind follower, but you are certain of each of the two premises. And thought leads to opinion (al-zann) only if its premises or some of them are conjectural, as when you say: 'This man is roaming with weapons at night, so he is a thief.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the premises of speculative reasoning (nazar) and the resulting types of mental assent. The commentator begins by stating that the premises of nazar, which constitute the proof, are either certain (majzum biha) or conjectural (maznun biha). Certain premises are further divided: the cause of certainty can either be inference (istidlāl) or blind acceptance (taqlīd). The commentator then explains the outcome of each type: when the premises are certain and derived from evidence, thinking leads to knowledge (ʿilm). Example: 'the world is changing, every changing thing is originated'—the minor premise is known by observation, the major by the impossibility of non-existence befalling the eternal. When the premises are certain but only through blind acceptance, thinking leads to belief (iʿtiqād), not knowledge. Example: 'the world is originated, every originated thing has an originator'—uttered as a follower, though certain. Finally, when premises are conjectural, thinking leads to opinion (ẓann). Example: 'a man with weapons at night is a thief.' The passage thus draws a clear distinction between knowledge, belief, and opinion based on the epistemic status of premises.

Key Terms
النظر
مقدمات
مجزوم بها
مظنونة
الاستدلال
التقليد
علم
اعتقاد
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any certainty, whether from evidence or imitation, produces knowledge.
Correction: The passage specifies that only certainty based on evidence (istidlal) yields knowledge; certainty from blind acceptance (taqlid) yields only belief, not knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the result when premises are certain due to blind acceptance (taqlid)?
A) Knowledge (ilm)
B) Opinion (zann)
C) Belief (i'tiqad)
D) Doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
The epistemic quality of premises determines whether reasoning produces knowledge, belief, or opinion; only premises that are certain by evidence lead to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson distinguishes between certainty from evidence and certainty from blind acceptance. When you affirm your belief in God, which type of certainty do you possess? Have you personally examined the proofs, or are you relying on what you have been taught without verification? What would it take for you to move from belief to knowledge?
?
Consider an area of your faith where you hold a strong opinion but lack certain evidence—like the example of the man with a weapon. Are there beliefs you defend passionately that are actually built on conjecture rather than solid proof? How would your heart change if you sought evidence until you attained certainty?

Condition of Capability for Verbal Attestation
Defines capability condition and introduces disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition of capability for verbal attestation and the nature of the scholarly disagreement about its role in faith.

Core Idea
Verbal attestation is required only of those who are able; even then, scholars disagree.

This lesson examines a key condition for the requirement of uttering the shahadatayn: capability. The passage clarifies that the mute and one who dies before speaking are exempt. It then introduces a well-known disagreement among scholars about whether verbal attestation is a condition for the validity of iman or an essential part of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first a definition, then an exception, then a mention of disagreement.
The passage is compact; each part is important.
2
Identify the excluded groups
Find who is excluded from the requirement of nutq. The passage mentions the mute (al-akhras) and one who dies before speaking. Ask yourself why they are excluded.
The common thread is inability.
3
Understand the disagreement and its basis
The passage says there is a difference of opinion (khilaf) about the entry of nutq into iman, and that this disagreement is 'accompanied by verification' (bi al-tahqiq), meaning each side has proofs. Consider what the two positions might be.
The author is not taking sides here, just presenting the fact of disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: للمتمكن منه القادر» يخرج به الأخرسُ فلا يُطالب بالنُّطق ، كَمَن اختَرَمَتْهُ المَنِيَّة قبل النُّطق به مِن غير تراخ. (فيه) أي: في جهة اعتبار مدخليَّته في الإيمان (الخُلْفُ) أي: الاختلاف ملتبسا (بِالتَّحْقِيقِ) أي: بالأدلَّة القائمة على دعوى كلّ من الفريقين.
And our saying: 'for the one capable, the able' excludes the mute, so he is not required to speak, like one whom death overtakes before speaking without delay. (In it) meaning: in the aspect of considering its entry into iman, (the disagreement) meaning: the difference, accompanied (by verification) meaning: with the proofs established upon the claim of each of the two groups.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines the condition of capability for the verbal attestation (nutq bi al-shahadatayn). It states that the requirement applies only to one who is capable and able (al-mutamakkin minhu al-qadir). This excludes the mute (al-akhras), who is not required to speak. Similarly, one who dies before speaking is not held accountable. Then it introduces the disagreement (khilaf) about the role of nutq in iman. The author clarifies that this disagreement is 'accompanied by verification' (bi al-tahqiq), meaning that each party relies on proofs (adilla) for their claim.

Misconception: All people must verbally attest to the shahadah to be considered believers.
Correction: The mute and one who dies without opportunity are exempt because they lacked capability.

Verbal attestation is only required of those who are capable; the scholarly disagreement about its role in iman is based on proofs.

Defining Circularity and Infinite Regress
Definition and classification of circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and classifications of circularity (al-dawr) and infinite regress (al-tasalsul) as presented in classical kalam logic.

Core Idea
Circularity traps reasoning in a loop; infinite regress stretches it without end.

In this passage, the author introduces two fundamental logical problems that can undermine any causal or rational argument. Understanding their precise definitions and types is essential for evaluating proofs about the existence and attributes of God. The passage breaks down circularity into explicit and implicit forms, and infinite regress into four categories based on existence and order.

Guided Path
1
Read and locate definitions
Find the definitions of al-dawr and al-tasalsul in the text. Note the key Arabic terms: الدور, التسلسل.
The definition of circularity involves mutual dependence.
2
Classify circularity
Identify the two types of circularity: explicit (direct loop, one rank) and implicit (indirect loop, multiple ranks). Understand the difference using the examples with A, B, C.
Explicit: A depends on B and B depends on A. Implicit: A depends on B, B depends on C, C depends on A.
3
Classify infinite regress
Work through the four types of tasalsul. Pay attention to the criteria: simultaneous existence and order. Note which types are considered impossible by the philosopher.
The four types: (1) non-simultaneous (e.g., temporal events), (2) simultaneous without order (e.g., souls), (3) simultaneous with natural order (e.g., causes), (4) simultaneous with positional order (e.g., bodies). The last two are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: فأخبرني عن حقيقة الدور والتسلسل، قلت: أما الدور فهو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف على ذلك الشيء، فإن كان بمرتبة واحدة يسمى «الدور المصرح» كما إذا توقف (أ) على (ب) وتوقف (ب) على (أ)، وإن كان بمراتب سمي: «الدور المضمر» كما إذا توقف (أ) على (ب) وتوقف (ب) على (ج) وتوقف (ج) على (أ). وأما التسلسل: فهو ترتيب أمور غير متناهية، وهو على أربعة أقسام، وذلك لأنه إما أن يكون الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود، وإما ألا يكون فيها كالتسلسل في الحوادث، والتسلسل في الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود إما أن يكون فيها ترتيب أو لا، فالذي لا ترتيب فيه مثل التسلسل في النفوس الناطقة، والتسلسل في الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود مع الترتيب إما أن يكون ترتبها طبيعياً كالتسلسل في العلل والمعلولات والصفات والموصوفات، وإما أن يكون ترتيبها وضعياً كالتسلسل في الأجسام، والمستحيل عند الحكيم الأخيران دون الأولين.
If you say: 'Tell me about the reality of circularity and infinite regress.' I say: 'As for circularity (al-dawr), it is the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that very thing. If it is of one rank, it is called 'explicit circularity' (al-dawr al-muṣarraḥ), as when (A) depends on (B) and (B) depends on (A). If it is of multiple ranks, it is called 'implicit circularity' (al-dawr al-muḍmar), as when (A) depends on (B), (B) depends on (C), and (C) depends on (A). As for infinite regress (al-tasalsul), it is the arrangement of infinite things, and it is of four divisions. That is because either the singulars are simultaneous in existence or they are not, like regress in temporal events. As for regress in singulars simultaneous in existence, either there is order in them or not. That which has no order is like regress in rational souls. As for regress in singulars simultaneous in existence with order, either their order is natural, like regress in causes and effects and attributes and their subjects, or their order is positional, like regress in bodies. According to the philosopher (al-ḥakīm), the last two are impossible, unlike the first two.'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage defines two important logical concepts: circularity (al-dawr) and infinite regress (al-tasalsul). It is a definition and classification passage. Circularity is defined as the dependence of something on that which depends on it. It is divided into two types: explicit (muṣarraḥ) when the dependence is direct between two things in one rank, e.g., A depends on B and B depends on A; and implicit (muḍmar) when it involves multiple ranks, e.g., A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A. Infinite regress is defined as an arrangement (tartīb) of infinite things. It is classified into four divisions based on two criteria: whether the singulars (āḥād) are simultaneous in existence or not, and whether there is order among them. The first division: singulars not simultaneous in existence (like regress in temporal events). The second: singulars simultaneous in existence without order (like regress in rational souls). The third: singulars simultaneous in existence with natural order (like causes and effects). The fourth: singulars simultaneous in existence with positional order (like bodies). According to the philosopher (al-ḥakīm), the last two (natural and positional order) are impossible, while the first two are not necessarily impossible in themselves, though in theology they may be rejected.

Key Terms
الدور
المصرح
المضمر
التسلسل
الآحاد
ترتيب طبيعي
ترتيب وضعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinite regresses are logically impossible.
Correction: The philosopher holds that only regresses in simultaneous singulars with order (natural or positional) are impossible; those without order or in temporal events are not inherently impossible.

Checkpoint
What is an example of implicit circularity (al-dawr al-muḍmar)?
A) A depends on B and B depends on A
B) A depends on B, B depends on C, and C depends on A
C) A depends on B, B depends on C, etc. ad infinitum
D) A depends on itself

Key Takeaway
Circularity and infinite regress are distinguished by whether the dependence loop is direct or multi-step, and whether the infinite series involves ordered simultaneous existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you consider the chain of reasons for your most fundamental conviction about God, does it eventually terminate in something self-evident, or are you relying on an infinite regress? What would it mean for your faith if it were the latter?
?
This lesson distinguishes between possible and impossible infinite regresses. Are there areas in your life where you accept an infinite regress (e.g., always seeking more knowledge, more possessions) that leaves you without a foundation? How can you bring that to an end?

Circularity: Explicit and Implicit
Sanusi defines circularity and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of circularity (dawr) as defined in Sunni kalam and be able to distinguish between explicit and implicit circularity.

Core Idea
Circularity is when A depends on B and B depends on A, directly or indirectly.

In theological reasoning, some arguments appear to go in a loop. The classical term for this is dawr (circularity). Here, Sanusi defines it precisely and divides it into two types: explicit (direct loop) and implicit (indirect loop). This distinction is crucial for identifying fallacious arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of dawr
Read the Arabic passage defining circularity and its translation. Notice the general definition: dependence of a thing on that which depends on it.
The definition is reciprocal dependence.
2
Identify the two types
Find the two types: explicit (one rank) and implicit (multiple ranks). Note how the examples with letters illustrate each.
Explicit is a direct loop; implicit is an indirect loop.
3
Reflect on why circularity is problematic
Consider why circularity is impossible in a proof. The dependence loop means no independent starting point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: فأخبرني عن حقيقة الدور والتسلسل، قلتُ: أما الدور فهو توقف الشيء على ما يتوقف على ذلك الشيء، فإن كان بمرتبة واحدة يسمى «الدور المصرح كما إذا توقف (أ) على (ب) وتوقف (ب) على (أ)، وإن كان بمراتب سُمِّي: «الدور المُضمر ، كما إذا توقف (أ) على (ب) وتوقف (ب) على (ج) وتوقف (ج) على (أ). في وأما التسلسل : فهو ترتيب أمورٍ غير متناهية ، وهو على أربعة أقسام، وذلك لأنه إما أن يكون الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود ، وإما ألا يكون فيها كالتسلسل في الحوادث ، والتسلسل في الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود إما أن يكون فيها ترتيب أو لا ، فالذي لا ترتيب فيه مثل التسلسل في النفوس الناطقة، والتسلسل في الآحاد المجتمعة في الوجود مع الترتيب إما أن يكون ترتبها طبيعيًّا ؛ كالتسلسل في العلل والمعلولات والصفات والموصوفات ، وإما أنْ يكونَ ترتيبها وضعيًّا كالتسلسل في الأجسام، والمستحيل عند الحكيم الأخيران دون الأولين.
If you say: 'Inform me about the reality of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul).' I say: 'As for circularity, it is the dependence of a thing on that which depends on that thing. If it is of one rank, it is called explicit circularity (al-dawr al-musarrah), as when (A) depends on (B) and (B) depends on (A). If it is of multiple ranks, it is called implicit circularity (al-dawr al-mudmar), as when (A) depends on (B), (B) depends on (C), and (C) depends on (A). As for infinite regress, it is an ordering of non-finite matters, and it has four divisions. This is because it either involves individuals coexisting in existence or not, like regress in events. Regress in coexisting individuals either involves an order or not. That which has no order is like regress in rational souls. Regress in coexisting individuals with order is either natural, like regress in causes and effects and attributes and their possessors, or conventional, like regress in bodies. According to the philosopher, the last two are impossible, not the first two.'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage provides definitions in response to an objection. First, circularity (dawr) is defined as reciprocal dependence. It is further divided into explicit (direct) and implicit (indirect) circularity, using letters A, B, C as examples. Second, infinite regress (tasalsul) is defined as an infinite chain. The author then categorizes it into four types based on whether the terms coexist in existence, and whether they have a natural or conventional order. The last two types (natural and conventional order) are considered impossible by philosophers, while the first two (regress in non-coexisting events and in coexisting un-ordered entities) are possible.

Misconception: Circularity only happens when two items directly depend on each other.
Correction: Implicit circularity involves a chain of dependencies that ultimately loops back, even with intermediate steps.

Key Takeaway
Circularity is any reciprocal dependence, whether direct (explicit) or indirect (implicit).

Infinite Regress: Four Divisions
Sanusi defines infinite regress and divides it into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of infinite regress (tasalsul) and its four divisions as explained by Sanusi, and know which types are considered impossible.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is an endless chain, but not all forms are impossible.

After defining circularity, Sanusi turns to infinite regress (tasalsul). He defines it as an ordering of non-finite matters and then divides it into four categories based on whether the terms coexist in existence and whether they have an order. This classification helps distinguish between impossible regresses (like infinite causal chains) and possible ones (like infinite souls).

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tasalsul
Read the Arabic passage defining infinite regress and its translation. Notice the general definition: an ordering of non-finite matters.
2
Identify the first division
Distinguish between regress in coexisting individuals (e.g., souls) and regress in non-coexisting (events).
3
Subdivide coexisting regress
Coexisting regress can be unordered (rational souls) or ordered. Ordered regress is further divided into natural (causes/effects) and conventional (bodies).
4
Note the ruling
According to the philosopher, the last two (ordered natural and conventional) are impossible; the first two are possible.

Proof of the Impossibility of Circularity
Sanusi proves circularity false by showing it leads to a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that circularity (dawr) leads to a contradiction and is therefore impossible.

Core Idea
Circularity forces the same thing to be both prior and posterior to itself, which is self-contradictory.

In this passage, Sanusi provides a crisp logical demonstration against circular causality. He responds to a hypothetical question by showing that reciprocal dependence inevitably produces a contradiction. The proof is simple but powerful: if A depends on B and B depends on A, then each must be both prior and posterior to itself, which is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
The passage opens with a request to prove the falsity of circularity. Note that the question also mentions infinite regress, but the author focuses on circularity first.
2
Follow the logical argument
Read the proof carefully: circularity forces the same thing to be both prior and posterior to itself. The example of Zayd and 'Amr illustrates this.
3
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion is that circularity leads to the conjunction of two contradictories, which is false. Therefore circularity itself is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: فأخبرني عن الدليل على بطلان الدور والتسلسل . قلت: أما الدليل على بطلان الدور: فأنه يلزم عليه كون الشيء الواحد سابقا على نفسه مسبوقاً بها ، فإذا فرضنا أن زيداً أوجد عمراً ، وعمرا أوجد زيدًا ، لزم أن يكون زيد متقدماً على نفسه متأخرا عنها وأن يكون عمرو كذلك ، وذلك يؤدي إلى اجتماع النقيضين ، واجتماع النقيضين باطل ، فما أدى إليه : وهو الدور باطل .
If you say: 'Inform me about the proof for the falsity of circularity and infinite regress.' I say: 'As for the proof for the falsity of circularity: it necessitates that the same thing be prior to itself and posterior to it. If we suppose that Zayd originated 'Amr, and 'Amr originated Zayd, it would be necessary that Zayd be prior to himself and posterior to it, and similarly 'Amr. That leads to the conjunction of two contradictories, and the conjunction of two contradictories is false. So that which leads to it—namely circularity—is false.'
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents the proof against circularity (dawr) in response to an objection. The argument shows that circular causality makes a thing both prior and posterior to itself, which is contradictory. Using the example of Zayd and 'Amr creating each other, it demonstrates that such a scenario requires each to be prior to and posterior to themselves, impossible. Therefore, circularity is false.

Key Terms
دور
سابق
مسبوق
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think circularity is impossible only in efficient causation.
Correction: This proof applies to any reciprocal dependence, not just efficient causation.

Checkpoint
What logical contradiction does circularity lead to?
A) A thing being both one and many
B) A thing being both prior and posterior to itself
C) A thing being both necessary and contingent
D) A thing being both cause and effect of different things

Key Takeaway
Circularity is false because it forces a thing to be both prior and posterior to itself, a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If circularity is impossible, then your existence and every good thing you have must ultimately trace back to a single, uncaused source. Are you living today as if that source is your foundation, or are you still looking for security in things that would eventually require circular reasoning to sustain?
?
The proof shows that a thing cannot be its own cause. In what area of your life are you trying to be your own source—seeking self-sufficiency apart from Allah—and what step can you take today to break that circular illusion?

Proof of the Impossibility of Infinite Regress
The author presents the famous proof against infinite regress using two parallel series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Proof of Cutting and Application and why an actual infinite regress is impossible in the external world.

Core Idea
An actual infinite regress leads to contradiction.

How can we prove that a chain of causes cannot go back infinitely? Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) developed a powerful proof known as dalil al-qat' wa al-tatbiq. By comparing two hypothetical infinite series, the proof shows that assuming an actual infinite regress yields an absurdity. This lesson explains that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text of the proof and its literal translation. Familiarize yourself with the key terms.
2
Understand the setup: two series
The proof imagines two series starting at different points (now and the Flood) and extending infinitely backward. Grasp why this setup is chosen.
The starting points are arbitrary but fixed.
3
Apply the comparison (tatbiq)
The proof applies the two series onto each other. Two outcomes are possible: they are equal or they differ. Consider each case and why both lead to contradiction.
If equal, a smaller series equals a larger one; if different, the difference is finite, making both series finite.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the assumption of an actual infinite regress leads to contradiction, it is impossible. This validates the need for a First Cause.
This proof is foundational in kalam arguments for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل على بطلان التسلسل : فقد ذكر له العلماء وجوها لكن أشهرها وعمدتها الذي يسمونه: دليل القطع والتطبيق وحاصله : أن نفرض سلسلتين تبدأ إحداهما من الآن إلى ما لا نهاية له في الأزل، وتبدأ الأخرى من قبل الآن وليكن من عهد الطُّوفان إلى ما لا نهاية له في الأزل أيضا ، ثم نُطبّقُ السلسلتين إحداهما على الأخرى ، فلا يخلو حالهما إما أن تتساويا وإما أن تتفاوتا ، فإن تساوتا لزم مساواة الناقص للزائد، وإن تفاوتتا ، فإنَّ مِقدار التفاوت بينهما معلوم، وهو ما كان من الطوفان إلى الآن، وهو مقدار متناه، والذي يزيد بمقدار متناه يكون متناهياً أيضاً.
As for the proof for the falsity of infinite regress (tasalsul), scholars have mentioned aspects for it, but the most famous and the main one is what they call the proof of cutting and application (dalil al-qat' wa al-tatbiq). Its summary is: we suppose two series, one beginning from now to infinity in the past, and the other beginning from before now, let it be from the time of the Flood to infinity in the past also. Then we apply one series to the other. The situation is either that they are equal or that they differ. If they are equal, then equality of the deficient to the exceeding is necessary. If they differ, then the amount of difference between them is known, and it is what was from the Flood to now, and it is finite, and whatever increases by a finite amount is also finite.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the famous proof against actual infinite regress (tasalsul) known as the Proof of Cutting and Application (dalil al-qat' wa al-tatbiq). The argument posits two infinite series starting from different points (now and the Flood) and extending infinitely backward. By comparing them, if they are equal, then a smaller series (from now) equals a larger series (from Flood), which is absurd. If they differ, the difference is finite (Flood to now), so both must be finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity. Hence, an actual infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
تسلسل
دليل القطع والتطبيق
سلسلتين
متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Proof of Cutting and Application proves that infinity is impossible in any context.
Correction: The proof only rules out actual infinite regress in the external world (e.g., an infinite chain of causes). It does not deny potential infinity or mathematical infinity.

Checkpoint
What does the Proof of Cutting and Application show?
A) Two infinite series can be equal
B) An actual infinite regress leads to a contradiction
C) Infinite regress is possible if the series are different
D) The difference between two series is always infinite

Key Takeaway
An actual infinite regress is impossible because it leads to logical contradictions, as shown by the Proof of Cutting and Application.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This proof shows that an endless past is impossible. Your own life is finite, yet you are connected to a chain that must ultimately reach the Eternal. Does the thought that you depend entirely on an uncaused Cause make you feel small, or does it fill you with awe and gratitude? Take a moment to consciously place your existence in Allah's hands and thank Him for being your ultimate support.
?
Consider the vastness of created beings and the apparent endlessness of time. This proof says that no matter how far back you go, you cannot have an actual infinite series. What does that imply about the power and independence of the Creator who brought everything into existence from nothing? How can this realization affect your attitude when you feel overwhelmed by the world's complexity or your own problems?

The author poses a question and begins an answer about the difference between qadim and azali.

Core Idea
The terms qadim and azali are not necessarily synonyms; scholars differ.

This brief passage from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid raises a key terminological question: Are the divine attributes of being 'pre-eternal' (qadim) and 'eternal' (azali) distinct concepts? The author notes that scholars have offered three different opinions on this issue. Although the text cuts off before listing them, the question itself highlights the precision required in discussing Allah's attributes.
