Three Opinions on Qadīm and Azalī
Enumerates three opinions on definitions of qadīm and azalī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly definitions of qadīm and azalī and the logical relationships between them.

Core Idea
Qadīm and azalī are not always synonyms; their relationship depends on definition.

In Islamic theology, the terms qadīm (eternal with respect to existence) and azalī (eternal without beginning) are often used interchangeably, but scholars have nuanced differences. This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents three distinct opinions on how these terms should be defined and how they logically relate. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read through the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the three opinions are clearly demarcated.
The passage is enumerative, so look for 'أحدها' and 'والقول الثالث'.
2
Understand the first two opinions
The first two opinions both result in azalī being more general than qadīm. Identify the difference: the second opinion adds the condition of self-subsistence (qā'im bi-nafsih) to qadīm.
Both opinions yield the same logical relationship despite different definitions.
3
Contrast with the third opinion
The third opinion equates the two terms. Note the logical relationship here is equality. Reflect on why scholars might hold different views.
The third opinion simplifies but loses nuance.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? These definitions affect theological claims about Allah's attributes. For example, if qadīm requires self-subsistence, that excludes attributes not self-subsistent.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أن القديم :هو الموجود الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده ، وأما الأزلي فهو : ما لا أول له ، أعمُّ أن يكون عدميًّا أو وجوديًّا، وعلى هذا تكون النسبة بين القديم والأزليّ العمومُ والخصوص المطلقُ ، والأزلي أعمُّ ، فكلُّ قديم أزلي وليس كلُّ أزلي قديماً . وثاني الأقوال: أن القديم هو القائم بنفسه الذي لا أوّل لوجوده ، والأزلي: الذي لا أوّل له مطلقا، سواء أكان وجوديًا أم عدميًّا وسواء أكان قائماً بنفسه أم لم يكن، وعلى هذا تكون النسبة بين القديم والأزلي العموم والخصوص المطلقُ ، والأزلي أعمُّ ، فكل قديم أزلي وليس كل أزلي قديماً كالأول . والقول الثالث: أن القديم ما لا أول له مطلقا، والأزلي مثله ، وعلى هذا تكون النسبة بين القديم والأزلي التّساوي فكل قديم أزلي وكل أزلي قديمٌ.
One of them: that al-qadīm is the existent whose existence has no beginning. As for al-azalī, it is that which has no beginning, more general in that it may be non-existential or existential. On this, the relationship between al-qadīm and al-azalī is absolute generality and specificity; al-azalī is more general, so every qadīm is azalī but not every azalī is qadīm. And the second opinion: that al-qadīm is the self-subsistent which has no beginning to its existence, and al-azalī is that which absolutely has no beginning, whether existential or non-existential and whether self-subsistent or not. On this, the relationship is also absolute generality and specificity; al-azalī is more general, so every qadīm is azalī but not every azalī is qadīm like the first. And the third opinion: that al-qadīm is that which absolutely has no beginning, and al-azalī is the like of it. On this, the relationship between al-qadīm and al-azalī is equality, so every qadīm is azalī and every azalī is qadīm.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three scholarly opinions on the definitions of the terms 'qadīm' (ancient/eternal with respect to existence) and 'azalī' (eternal without beginning). The author explains each opinion and the resulting logical relationship between the two terms. In the first opinion, qadīm is defined specifically as an existent with no beginning of existence, while azalī is broader, encompassing any absence or existence lacking a beginning. Thus azalī is more general, and every qadīm is azalī but not vice versa. The second opinion adds the condition of self-subsistence (qā'im bi-nafsih) to qadīm, while azalī remains absolute lack of beginning; again azalī is more general. The third opinion equates the two terms: both mean absolutely no beginning. The author presents these without endorsing one, preparing the reader for their practical implications.

أزلي
العموم والخصوص المطلق
التساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming qadīm and azalī are synonymous without any nuance.
Correction: While the third opinion equates them, the first two opinions show that azalī can be more general. The relationship depends on the definition.

Checkpoint

A) Equality
B) Absolute generality and specificity with azalī more general
C) Absolute generality and specificity with qadīm more general
D) No relationship

Key Takeaway
The relationship between qadīm and azalī is not fixed; it depends on how each term is defined, with two opinions making azalī broader and one equating them.

Two Syllogistic Forms of the Independence Proof
Al-Shanwānī analyses the proof for independence from a specificator using syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof for Allah's independence from a specificator can be formalized as both a conjunctive syllogism and an exceptional syllogism.

Core Idea
The same theological truth can be proven through two different valid syllogistic forms.

Al-Shanwānī takes a concise statement from the matn and unpacks its logical machinery. He shows that the argument for independence from a specificator is not just a simple assertion but can be rigorously structured as two standard syllogistic forms. Understanding these forms deepens our appreciation of how kalam scholars used logic to defend doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Shanwānī introduces two distinct logical structures.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two syllogisms
The passage first presents a conjunctive syllogism (burhān iqtirānī) then an exceptional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī). Identify their premises and conclusions.
Both lead to the same conclusion.
3
Understand the implication (mulāzama)
The passage ends by explaining that dependence on a specificator implies origination. This is the key logical link.
This implication is used in both syllogisms.
4
Reflect on logical rigor
Consider why al-Shanwānī offers two formalizations. This shows the precision of kalam argumentation.
The different forms may appeal to different audiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لوجوب وجوده وقدمه أي وجوبهما مستلزم للاستغناء عن المخصص، لأنه لو احتاج إليه لكان حادثا، فينافي ما تقدم من وجوب الوجود والقدم. وهذا استدلال على الجزء الثاني من جزئي مفهوم القيام بالنفس ببرهان اقتراني تقريره: الله تعالى واجب له الوجود والقدم والبقاء، وكل من وجب له ذلك فهو مستغن عن المخصص، ينتج: الله مستغن عن المخصص. ويصح أن يكون إشارة إلى قياس استثنائي، تقريره: لو لم يستغن عن المخصص لما وجب وجوده وقدمه وبقاؤه، لكنه تعالى وجب وجوده وقدمه وبقاؤه، ينتج: أنه تعالى مستغن عن المخصص، وبيان الملازمة: أن كل موجود يفتقر إلى المخصص حادث.
His statement: (Because of the necessity of His existence and pre-eternity) meaning their necessity entails independence from the specificator, because if He needed it, He would be originated, contradicting what preceded of the necessity of existence and pre-eternity. And this is an inference regarding the second part of the two parts of the concept of self-subsistence by a conjunctive syllogism, its formulation: Allah the Exalted has necessary existence, pre-eternity, and permanence, and everyone for whom that is necessary is independent of the specificator; conclusion: Allah is independent of the specificator. And it is valid that it be an indication of an exceptional syllogism, its formulation: If He were not independent of the specificator, His existence, pre-eternity, and permanence would not be necessary, but He the Exalted has necessary existence, pre-eternity, and permanence; conclusion: He the Exalted is independent of the specificator. And the explanation of the implication: that every existent that depends on a specificator is originated.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a gloss by al-Shanwānī that analyzes the logical structure of the proof for Allah's independence from a specificator (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ), which is the second part of qiyam al-nafs. The gloss first reiterates the rationale: necessity of existence and pre-eternity implies independence because needing a specificator would entail contingency and origination (ḥudūth), contradicting divine necessity. Then it formalizes this as two types of syllogisms. The first is a conjunctive syllogism (burhān iqtirānī): Major premise: Allah necessarily has existence, pre-eternity, and permanence. Minor premise: Whatever has these is independent of a specificator. Conclusion: Allah is independent. The second is an exceptional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī) of the form modus tollens: If He were dependent, His necessary attributes would not hold; but they do hold; therefore He is not dependent. The gloss explains the implication (mulāzama) by stating that any existent dependent on a specificator is originated—thus if Allah were dependent, He would be originated, which is impossible. This demonstrates the rigorous logical grounding of kalam proofs.

Key Terms
برهان اقتراني
قياس استثنائي
ملازمة
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is just a simple straightforward statement.
Correction: It can be rigorously formalized as two distinct syllogisms, showing the logical depth of kalam.

Checkpoint
What type of syllogism does al-Shanwānī first present to prove Allah's independence from a specificator?
A) Exceptional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī)
B) Conjunctive syllogism (burhān iqtirānī)
C) Disjunctive syllogism
D) Categorical syllogism

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute of independence from a specificator is logically proven through syllogistic reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is independent of any cause,' is your conviction built on the same logical certainty as the syllogisms presented here, or is it merely a cultural belief? What would it take for you to internalize this proof so deeply that no doubt could ever shake it?

The proof shows that Allah's necessity implies independence. But have you ever considered how your own sense of need and dependency—on people, possessions, or status—contradicts the very nature of the One you worship? Are you willing to let this truth expose the idols of self-sufficiency in your life?

Why Some Say Knowledge Cannot Be Defined
Sanusi classifies two groups who deny definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions held by scholars who deny that knowledge (العلم) can be defined, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Two distinct reasons for denying a definition of knowledge: extreme clarity and extreme difficulty.

Before diving into definitions of knowledge, we must first ask: can knowledge be defined at all? Some scholars argue it cannot, but for different reasons. This passage from Sanusi's commentary introduces two groups that hold this view, setting the stage for the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the flow. Notice how it begins with 'أما الكلام على معنى العلم'.
The passage uses technical terms like 'ظاهر' and 'انكشاف'.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage divides those who deny definition into two groups. Find the description of each group and their specific reason for denying definition.
The first group is introduced with 'فريق ذهب إلى أن علة...' and the second with 'فريق ذهب إلى أن علة...'.
3
Compare the reasons
Compare the two reasons: one says knowledge is too obvious to need definition, the other says it is too difficult to define well. How do these differ?
The first reason is about self-evidence, the second about inaccessibility.
4
Reflect on the debate
Think about why these arguments are important for Islamic theology. Does denying definition affect how we use the term 'knowledge' in kalam?
This sets up the next lesson where the author gives a definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام على معنى العلم : فاعلم أولا أنَّ العلماء مختلفون في العلم مطلقا، هل يُعرَّف أو لا؟ هذه فمنهم من ذهب إلى أنه لا يعرف ، وهؤلاء انقسموا إلى قسمين: فمنهم فريقٌ ذهب إلى أنَّ علة عدم تعريف العلم: كونه ظاهرا غاية في الظهور، ألا ترى أنه يَكْشِفُ غيرَهُ ،ويوضّحه، فكيف يُظَنُّ أنه في حاجة إلى أن يَكشِفَهُ غيره، وكل شيء غيره ينكشف به ؟ ومنهم فريقٌ ذهب إلى أنَّ علَّة عدم تعريف العلم أنه يعسر تعريفه ، حتى إنه لم يذكر له تعريف إلا وفيه مناقشة وعليه اعتراض.
As for discussion on the meaning of knowledge: know first that the scholars differ regarding knowledge absolutely, whether it is defined or not? Among them are those who hold that it is not defined, and these are divided into two groups: a group holds that the reason for not defining knowledge is that it is extremely apparent; do you not see that it reveals other things and clarifies them, so how could it be thought that it needs something else to reveal it, while everything else is revealed by it? And a group holds that the reason for not defining knowledge is that its definition is difficult, such that no definition is mentioned for it except that it contains discussion and objection.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the debate among scholars regarding whether knowledge (العلم) can be defined. The author begins by stating that scholars disagree on this issue. He then describes two groups who deny the possibility of defining knowledge altogether. The first group argues that knowledge is so self-evidently clear (ظاهر غاية في الظهور) that it needs no definition; indeed, it is knowledge that reveals other things, so it cannot itself be in need of being revealed by something else. The second group argues that defining knowledge is extremely difficult (يعسر تعريفه), such that every proposed definition is subject to criticism and objection. These two positions represent the negative side of the debate, setting the stage for the opposing view that knowledge can and should be defined.

Key Terms
علم
تعريف
ظهور
انكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is obvious, then it definitively cannot be defined.
Correction: The passage only reports the opinion of a group that denies definition due to apparentness; it does not endorse it. The debate continues.

Checkpoint
According to the first group, why is knowledge not defined?
A) It is too obscure to define.
B) It is extremely apparent and reveals other things.
C) It requires a lengthy explanation.
D) It is impossible to define due to its complexity.

Key Takeaway
Two groups deny defining knowledge: one because it is self-evident, another because it is too difficult to define satisfactorily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on knowledge every moment, yet you may never have examined what it truly is. Which of the two groups' reasoning resonates with you—and what does that reveal about your intellectual habits? Will you remain comfortable with that, or will you push deeper?
?
Imagine you stood before Allah and He asked: 'What did you do with the knowledge I gave you?' Would you be able to say you truly understood its nature, or would you admit you never stopped to define it? What will you do today to change that?

Four Objections to the Commentator's Definition of Knowledge
Lists four objections to the commentator's definition of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four key objections raised against the commentator's definition of knowledge and their logical implications.

Core Idea
Defining knowledge requires precision: four objections reveal hidden logical pitfalls.

The commentator's definition of knowledge uses terms like 'is revealed' and 'known things'. But these terms carry subtle implications that can lead to logical contradictions. This passage lists four objections that scrutinize each part of the definition, revealing why some formulations can be problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: each objection begins with 'Minha' (among them) and targets a specific phrase of the definition.
2
Understand each objection
For each objection, identify which part of the definition it attacks and what logical flaw it exposes. The four flaws are: prior concealment, circularity, obtaining the already obtained, and temporal disconnection.
Use the commentary to clarify the meaning of each technical term.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
These objections show that definitions in kalam must be carefully worded to avoid contradictions. Consider how each objection forces a refinement of the definition.
Think about why 'تحصيل الحاصل' is impossible.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the objection about 'تحصيل الحاصل'. Then try the activity to reformulate one objection in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ تعريفه يَرِدُ عليه اعتراضات كثيرة، منها: أن قوله تنكشف يقتضي بظاهره سبق الخفاء، لأن معنی الانكشاف ظهور الشيء بعد أن لم يكن ظاهراً . ومنها أن قوله: «المعلومات جمع معلومة وهو اسم مفعول من العلم، ومعرفة المشتق متوقفة على معرفة أصل الاشتقاق ، فاشتمل التعريف على الدور المحال ومنها أن قوله: «المعلومات» يقتضي أنها مُنكشفة قبل الانكشاف الحاصل بالعلم، فإنها لا تكون معلومة حتى تكون منكشفة ، ويلزم على هذا: تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال . ومنها قوله: «عند تعلقها بها» يقتضي بظاهره : أنَّ العلم تارة يتعلق بالمعلومات، وتارة لا يتعلق بها وليس كذلك
For his definition is subject to many objections. Among them: that his saying 'is revealed' implies in its apparent meaning prior concealment, because the meaning of revelation is the appearance of a thing after it was not apparent. And among them: that his saying 'the known things' (plural of 'known') is a passive participle from knowledge, and knowledge of a derived term depends on knowledge of the origin of derivation, so the definition includes impossible circularity. And among them: that his saying 'the known things' implies that they are revealed before the revelation that occurs through knowledge, for they are not known until they are revealed, and this entails producing what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل), which is impossible. And among them: his saying 'when it is related to them' implies in its apparent meaning that knowledge is sometimes related to known things and sometimes not related to them, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage lists four specific objections to the definition of knowledge given by the commentator (الشارح). The first objection points out that the word 'is revealed' (تنكشف) suggests that the known object was previously concealed, implying prior ignorance. The second objection argues that the term 'known things' (المعلومات) is a derived term (اسم مفعول) from the root 'knowledge', and understanding the derived term requires prior knowledge of the root, leading to circularity (دور). The third objection states that 'known things' presupposes they are already revealed before the act of knowledge, which is impossible because it would mean obtaining what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل). The fourth objection targets the phrase 'when it is related to them' (عند تعلقها بها), which suggests that knowledge is sometimes related to its objects and sometimes not, but in reality, knowledge is always related to its objects. These objections aim to refine the definition of knowledge and eliminate any problematic implications.

Key Terms
انكشاف
خفاء
معلومات
دور
تحصيل الحاصل
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections are merely linguistic nitpicking without theological significance.
Correction: Each objection uncovers a logical flaw (circularity, redundancy, etc.) that would render the definition invalid in kalam reasoning, highlighting the need for precise terminology.

Checkpoint
Which objection points out that the definition implies knowledge of something already known, which is impossible?
A) The first objection about prior concealment.
B) The second objection about circularity.
C) The third objection about 'تحصيل الحاصل'.
D) The fourth objection about temporal relation.

Key Takeaway
The definition of knowledge must avoid implying prior concealment, circularity, redundancy, or temporal disconnection from its objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own faith or knowledge, do you ever rely on circular reasoning without realizing it? Where might your definitions assume what they are trying to prove?
?
Can you identify a concept you claim to 'know' that, if defined precisely, would reveal hidden assumptions or prior ignorance? How will you refine your understanding?
?
If you were challenged to define 'knowledge' without any logical flaws, what would your definition be? Write it down and test it against the four objections from this lesson.

An Alternative Definition of Knowledge: Certainty, Correspondence, and Stability
The author provides an alternative definition of knowledge and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative definition of knowledge that emphasizes three essential conditions: certainty, correspondence with reality, and stability, and how it excludes various types of uncertain cognition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a revelation that leaves no room for the opposite in mind, reality, or against doubt.

This passage presents a definition of knowledge that is more detailed than a simple 'belief that corresponds to reality'. The author carefully unpacks each phrase to show what knowledge includes and excludes, highlighting three necessary conditions. By examining this definition, you will learn how classical scholars distinguished genuine knowledge from conjecture, illusion, doubt, and even firm belief that might be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition and its translation. Notice the phrase 'does not admit the opposite in any way' – this is key.
2
Understand what the definition excludes
Identify which attributes (like power, will, life) and cognitive states (conjecture, illusion, doubt, firm belief) are excluded, and why.
The exclusion of firm belief shows that knowledge is more than just strong conviction.
3
Examine the three conditions
The passage derives three necessary conditions from the definition: certainty (jazzm), correspondence (muṭābaqah), and stability (thabāt). See how each condition excludes the opposite in a specific domain.
These three conditions are fundamental to the Islamic epistemology of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرف بعضهم العلم مطلقا بقوله: «العلم صفةٌ ينكشف بها ما تتعلق به انكشافا لا يحتمل النقيض بوجه من الوجوه، فقوله: «صفة كالجنس في التعريف يشمل جميع الصفات ، وقوله: ينكشف بها ما تتعلق به يخرج الصفات التي لها تعلق ولا تقتضي الانكشاف كالقدرة والإرادة، لأنهما صفتا تأثير كما مرَّ ، ويخرج أيضا الصفات التي لا تتعلق كالحياة ، والمراد بالانكشاف ما هو أعم من الانكشاف التّامّ، ولهذا أتى بعده بقوله: «لا يحتمل النقيض» لأجل إخراج الظن ، والوهم ، والشك ، والاعتقاد الجازم، سواء أكان مطابقا للواقع أم لم يكن مطابقا ، لأن متعلقات هذه الأشياء تحتمل النقيض، وقوله: «بوجه من الوجوه» أراد به: ألا يحتمل النقيض بحسب الدهن ، ولا بحسب الخارج ، ولا لأجل تشكيك مشكك ، وأشار به إلى أن العلم تلزمه أمور ثلاثة : الجزم، والمطابقة للواقع، والثبات على ما علمه، فالعلم بالشيء لا بد أن يكون جازما به وثابتا عليه، ولا بد أن يكون معلومه مطابقا للواقع ، فعدم احتمال معلومه للنقيض بحسب الذهن لأنه جازم به وعدم احتمال معلومه للنقيض بحسب الخارج لأنه مطابق للواقع ، وعدم احتمال معلومه النقيض بسبب تشكيك المشكك ؛ لأنه ثابت عليه، وفي هذا القدر كفاية.
And some defined knowledge absolutely by saying: 'Knowledge is an attribute by which what it is related to is revealed in a revelation that does not admit the opposite in any way.' So his saying 'attribute' is like the genus in definition, encompassing all attributes. And his saying 'by which what it is related to is revealed' excludes attributes that have relation but do not entail revelation, like power and will, for they are attributes of effect as has passed, and it also excludes attributes that have no relation, like life. And by 'revelation' is meant what is more general than complete revelation, and for this he followed it with his saying 'does not admit the opposite' in order to exclude conjecture, illusion, doubt, and firm belief, whether it corresponds to reality or not, because the objects of these admit the opposite. And his saying 'in any way' intended: that it does not admit the opposite with regard to the mind, nor with regard to external reality, nor due to the doubt of a doubter. And he indicated by this that knowledge entails three things: certainty, correspondence with reality, and stability upon what is known. For knowledge of a thing must be certain about it and stable upon it, and its object must correspond to reality. So the non-admission of the opposite of its object with respect to the mind is because it is certain about it; and non-admission of the opposite of its object with respect to external reality is because it corresponds to reality; and non-admission of the opposite of its object due to the doubt of a doubter is because it is stable upon it. And in this amount there is sufficiency.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative definition of knowledge in general (not specifically God's knowledge) and provides a detailed explanation of its components. The definition states that knowledge is an attribute by which its object is revealed in a way that does not admit the opposite in any respect. The author then unpacks the definition: the word 'attribute' (صفة) is a genus that includes all attributes; the phrase 'by which what it is related to is revealed' excludes attributes like power (قدرة) and will (إرادة) that have relation but do not entail revelation, and also excludes life (حياة) which has no relation. The term 'revelation' (انكشاف) is meant broadly, which is why the qualifier 'does not admit the opposite' (لا يحتمل النقيض) is added to exclude states such as conjecture (ظن), illusion (وهم), doubt (شك), and firm belief (اعتقاد جازم) that may not correspond to reality, since their objects admit the opposite. The phrase 'in any way' (بوجه من الوجوه) emphasizes that knowledge excludes the opposite in three domains: in the mind (ذهن) because of certainty (جزم), in external reality (خارج) because of correspondence (مطابقة), and against doubt (تشكيك) because of stability (ثبات). Thus, knowledge necessarily entails three things: certainty, correspondence to reality, and stability. The author concludes that this explanation is sufficient for understanding the definition.

ثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge is simply any firm belief, regardless of truth.
Correction: Knowledge requires not only firm belief (certainty) but also correspondence with reality and stability against doubt.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of adding 'does not admit the opposite' in the definition of knowledge?
A) To exclude illusions and conjectures.
B) To include all types of belief.
C) To emphasize that knowledge is complete.
D) To distinguish knowledge from attributes like power.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is an attribute that reveals its object with certainty, correspondence to reality, and stability, leaving no room for the opposite in any regard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When was the last time a doubt shook your knowledge of something you thought you knew? Did you reestablish stability, or did you ignore it?
?
If knowledge requires correspondence to reality, how often do you honestly examine whether your religious convictions correspond to the truth as it is, not just as you prefer it?

The Rational Impossibility of Acquired Knowledge for Allah
Definition and rational proof of impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of acquired knowledge (al-‘ilm al-muktasab) and why it is rationally impossible for Allah to have it.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge requires prior ignorance – impossible for the All-Knowing.

In this lesson, we examine the first proof presented by al-Sanūsī that Allah's knowledge cannot be acquired. We must first understand what 'acquired knowledge' means in the kalam tradition and why it entails a prior state of not knowing. This rational impossibility (istihala 'aqliyya) is the foundation for the legal ruling that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of acquired knowledge
Read the Arabic passage that defines 'muktasab' and 'kasbi' as knowledge obtained after reflection (nazar) and inference (istidlal). Familiarize yourself with the key terms.
Notice that acquired knowledge is a process-based knowledge.
2
Identify the proof of impossibility
The passage then states that such knowledge is impossible for Allah because it requires the precedence of ignorance (sabq al-jahl). Reflect on why this is impossible for Allah.
3
Connect the definition to the proof
Understand that the proof is built directly on the definition: if knowledge comes through a process, it presupposes a lack of that knowledge beforehand. Since Allah's knowledge is eternal and perfect, no such process can apply.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Apply the concept
Consider an example: a human proving the world's creation gains acquired knowledge. Contrast this with Allah's knowledge, which is not gained through any proof.
This reinforces the rational impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال الأول (۱) من استحالة كون علم الله مكتسبا (وَلَا يُقَالُ ) أي : ولا يجوز شرعاً (۲) أَنْ يُطلق (۳) على علمه تعالى بالمعنى (۱) قوله: (وهو أقوى في الاستدلال من الأوّل الأولى عندي: وهو أوضح في الاستدلال من الأوَّل لأَنَّهُ صَرَّحَ في الثاني بالقصد والاختيار ، ولم يُصرّح به في الأول مع كونه مُرادا، فلا يَردُ مثلا: نسج العنكبوت وبيوت النحل ؛ وإن جعلوهما وجه ضعف الأول ، وإنما لم يردا لأن فعلهما اتفاقي ، وفعل المولى تعالى قامَ الدَّليل على أنه بالقصد والاختيار، فعلى هذا مآل الدليلين واحدٌ ، وقيل: لا مانع من أنَّ المولى يجعل فيها علما إلهاميا إذ ذاك، على أنا نقول: الفعل في الحقيقة الله لا لها . الأمير. معنی (۲) قوله: (شرعا) ظاهره ويصح عقلا وليس كذلك. الأمير. قال الناظم في شرحه الكبير: (ولا) أي: لا يجوز عقلا أن يكون علمه تعالى مكتسبا .... ثم قال: وإذا امتنع عقلا عليه الاكتساب فلا يجوز أن ( يقال ) أي : يطلق على علمه أنه ( مكتسب) له تعالى ؛ إذ كل ما امتنع اتصافه به تعالى عقلا امتنع شرعا أن يطلق عليه تعالى وعلى صفاته الذاتية. انظر: «عمدة المريد) ج ١ ص ٤٤٢ . (۳) كلمة «يقال»: تطلق على المعنى الذي يعبّر عنه بنحو (يَصْدُق» و«يُطلَق» وربما أريد منها يُعتقد» ، فإذا فسَّرتَ هذه الكلمة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ـ كما فعل الشارح - صار المعنى: لا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلا أنْ يُطلَق ويحمّل لفظ (مكتسب» على علم الله تعالى فيقال: «علمه سبحانه مكتسب) ، وهذا ربَّما أوهم أنَّ المنهي عنه هو هذا القول مع أنَّ المعنى صحيح ؛ وأنَّ علَّة النَّهي: هي عدم ورود إِذْنِ مِن الشَّارع بالإطلاق ، ومن أجل هذا كان الأحسنُ أن نُفسّر كلمة «يقال» في عبارة المصنّف بالاعتقاد، وعلى هذا يكون المعنى: ولا يجوز لأحدٍ أن يعتقد أن علمه مكتسب ؛ وذلك لاستحالة ذلك بالنظر إلى الله تعالى مِن قِبَلِ أن المكتسب والكسبي في عرف أهل العلم: هو العلم الذي يحصل بعد النظر والاستدلال، فإن أقام أحدٌ دليلا على حدوث العالم؛ كان العلم الحاصلُ له بعد هذا الدليل علمًا مكتسبا أو كسبيًّا. وإنما كان العلم المكتسب مستحيلا في حقه تعالى، لكونه يقتضي سَبْقَ الجهل، إذ ما يحصل النظر والاستدلال لا يحصل العلم .
The first proof (1) of the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired: (And it is not said) i.e., it is not permissible legally (2) that it be applied (3) to His knowledge, may He be exalted, in the sense (1) His statement: (and it is stronger in proof than the first, the first in my view: it is clearer in proof than the first because in the second he stated intention and choice, and he did not state them explicitly in the first although they are intended, so it does not face, for example, the spider's weaving and the bees' hives; and they made them a point of weakness for the first, but they are not considered because their action is accidental, while the action of the Lord, may He be exalted, the proof has been established that it is with intention and choice, so on this basis the outcome of the two proofs is one. And it is said: there is no preventing that the Lord places in them an inspired knowledge at that time, though we say: the action in reality belongs to Allah, not to them. Al-Amir. Meaning (2) His statement: (legally) its apparent meaning is that it is valid intellectually, but it is not so. Al-Amir. The poet said in his large commentary: (And not) i.e., it is not intellectually permissible for His knowledge, may He be exalted, to be acquired... Then he said: And when acquisition is intellectually impossible for Him, it is not permissible that (it be said) i.e., that it be applied to His knowledge that it is (acquired) for Him, may He be exalted; for whatever is intellectually impossible to be attributed to Him, may He be exalted, is legally impossible to be applied to Him, may He be exalted, and to His essential attributes. See: 'Umdat al-Mureed, vol. 1, p. 442. (3) The word 'yuqāl' is used for the meaning expressed by something like 'it applies' and 'it is said', and perhaps it is intended by it 'it is believed'. So if you interpret this word by the first meaning — as the commentator did — the meaning becomes: it is not permissible, legally or intellectually, that the word 'acquired' be applied and carried upon the knowledge of Allah, may He be exalted, so that it is said: 'His knowledge, glory be to Him, is acquired'. And this might give the illusion that what is prohibited is this statement, while the meaning is correct, and that the cause of the prohibition is the absence of permission from the Lawgiver to apply it. For this reason, it was better that we interpret the word 'yuqāl' in the author's expression as belief. On this basis, the meaning would be: It is not permissible for anyone to believe that His knowledge is acquired; that is because of the impossibility of that with respect to Allah, may He be exalted, from the aspect that 'acquired' and 'acquisitional' in the terminology of the people of knowledge is the knowledge that occurs after reflection and inference. If someone adduces a proof for the temporal origination of the world, the knowledge that occurs to him after this proof is acquired or acquisitional knowledge. And acquired knowledge is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted, because it requires the precedence of ignorance, since what occurs through reflection and inference does not produce knowledge.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired. The author begins by stating the first proof (al-istidlal al-awwal) from the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired (istihalat kawn 'ilm Allah muktasaban). The phrase 'wa la yuqalu' (and it is not said) is explained: it means it is not legally permissible to apply the term 'acquired' to Allah's knowledge. The commentary then discusses the meaning of 'yuqalu' in detail. First, a footnote clarifies that the proof is stronger than the first one mentioned earlier because it explicitly involves intention and choice. Then, the meaning of 'shar'an' (legally) is examined: its apparent meaning might suggest that it is intellectually valid, but it is not; the poet in his larger commentary states that it is not intellectually permissible for Allah's knowledge to be acquired. The reasoning is that if acquisition is intellectually impossible for Him, then it is also legally impossible to apply the term to Him and His essential attributes. Next, the word 'yuqalu' is interpreted: it can mean 'it applies' or 'it is said' or 'it is believed'. The commentator prefers interpreting it as 'belief' to avoid the misconception that the prohibition is merely about the utterance while the meaning is correct. The underlying cause is the rational impossibility: acquired knowledge is defined as knowledge that comes after reflection and inference (al-nazar wa al-istidlal). For example, if someone uses a proof for the world's origination, the resulting knowledge is acquired. Such knowledge is impossible for Allah because it would require prior ignorance (sabq al-jahl). Therefore, the term 'muktasab' cannot be applied to Allah's knowledge either in speech or belief.

Key Terms
علم مكتسب
النظر والاستدلال
سبق الجهل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the prohibition is only about using the word 'muktasab' for Allah.
Correction: The prohibition is rooted in the rational impossibility: acquired knowledge entails prior ignorance, which is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of acquired knowledge?
A) Knowledge that comes without effort
B) Knowledge obtained after reflection and inference
C) Knowledge that is eternal
D) Knowledge that is innate

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, and it is impossible for Allah because it would imply prior ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah knows everything. But do you ever act as if you need to inform Him of your needs, as if He doesn't already know? How does this lesson's truth—that ignorance preceded by learning is impossible for Him—change the way you pray?
?
Think of the last time you learned something new. Now realize that Allah has never learned—He has always known. Does this awe you? Or does it make you feel distant? Sit with that feeling and ask yourself: am I content with a God whose knowledge is beyond my comprehension?
?
If acquired knowledge implies prior ignorance, and that is impossible for Allah, then what does that say about your own ignorance? Are you comfortable with being a creature that must learn, while your Creator never does? How does this humble you in your pursuit of knowledge?

The Ruling: Prohibition of Saying or Believing Allah's Knowledge is Acquired
Legal ruling and its grounding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal ruling that it is not permissible to say or believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired, and that this ruling is grounded in rational impossibility.

Core Idea
The legal prohibition flows from rational impossibility, not mere convention.

In this lesson, we explore the implications of the rational impossibility established earlier. The text states 'wa la yuqalu' — it is not said. But what does this prohibition encompass? Is it merely a linguistic formality, or is it a matter of belief? The commentators clarify that the word 'yuqalu' can mean both 'it is said' and 'it is believed', and the correct interpretation is that it is not permissible to believe that Allah's knowledge is acquired, because the rational impossibility makes such belief invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the ruling
Read the Arabic passage that states 'wa la yuqalu' and the commentaries that follow. Pay attention to the distinction between intellectual and legal possibility.
2
Understand the principle
The passage states: 'Whatever is intellectually impossible to attribute to Allah is legally impossible to attribute to Him.' This is a key principle.
3
Examine the interpretation of 'yuqalu'
The commentator notes that 'yuqalu' can mean 'it is said' or 'it is believed'. He prefers 'belief' to avoid the misconception that the prohibition is just about the word.
4
Connect back to rational impossibility
See how the legal ruling (not permissible to say/believe) is directly based on the rational impossibility (mustahil 'aqlan).

Source Text

الاستدلال الأول (۱) من استحالة كون علم الله مكتسبا (وَلَا يُقَالُ ) أي : ولا يجوز شرعاً (۲) أَنْ يُطلق (۳) على علمه تعالى بالمعنى (۱) قوله: (وهو أقوى في الاستدلال من الأوّل الأولى عندي: وهو أوضح في الاستدلال من الأوَّل لأَنَّهُ صَرَّحَ في الثاني بالقصد والاختيار ، ولم يُصرّح به في الأول مع كونه مُرادا، فلا يَردُ مثلا: نسج العنكبوت وبيوت النحل ؛ وإن جعلوهما وجه ضعف الأول ، وإنما لم يردا لأن فعلهما اتفاقي ، وفعل المولى تعالى قامَ الدَّليل على أنه بالقصد والاختيار، فعلى هذا مآل الدليلين واحدٌ ، وقيل: لا مانع من أنَّ المولى يجعل فيها علما إلهاميا إذ ذاك، على أنا نقول: الفعل في الحقيقة الله لا لها . الأمير. معنی (۲) قوله: (شرعا) ظاهره ويصح عقلا وليس كذلك. الأمير. قال الناظم في شرحه الكبير: (ولا) أي: لا يجوز عقلا أن يكون علمه تعالى مكتسبا .... ثم قال: وإذا امتنع عقلا عليه الاكتساب فلا يجوز أن ( يقال ) أي : يطلق على علمه أنه ( مكتسب) له تعالى ؛ إذ كل ما امتنع اتصافه به تعالى عقلا امتنع شرعا أن يطلق عليه تعالى وعلى صفاته الذاتية. انظر: «عمدة المريد) ج ١ ص ٤٤٢ . (۳) كلمة «يقال»: تطلق على المعنى الذي يعبّر عنه بنحو (يَصْدُق» و«يُطلَق» وربما أريد منها يُعتقد» ، فإذا فسَّرتَ هذه الكلمة بالمعنى الأوَّلِ ـ كما فعل الشارح - صار المعنى: لا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلا أنْ يُطلَق ويحمّل لفظ (مكتسب» على علم الله تعالى فيقال: «علمه سبحانه مكتسب) ، وهذا ربَّما أوهم أنَّ المنهي عنه هو هذا القول مع أنَّ المعنى صحيح ؛ وأنَّ علَّة النَّهي: هي عدم ورود إِذْنِ مِن الشَّارع بالإطلاق ، ومن أجل هذا كان الأحسنُ أن نُفسّر كلمة «يقال» في عبارة المصنّف بالاعتقاد، وعلى هذا يكون المعنى: ولا يجوز لأحدٍ أن يعتقد أن علمه مكتسب ؛ وذلك لاستحالة ذلك بالنظر إلى الله تعالى مِن قِبَلِ أن المكتسب والكسبي في عرف أهل العلم: هو العلم الذي يحصل بعد النظر والاستدلال، فإن أقام أحدٌ دليلا على حدوث العالم؛ كان العلم الحاصلُ له بعد هذا الدليل علمًا مكتسبا أو كسبيًّا. وإنما كان العلم المكتسب مستحيلا في حقه تعالى، لكونه يقتضي سَبْقَ الجهل، إذ ما يحصل النظر والاستدلال لا يحصل العلم .
The first proof (1) of the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired: (And it is not said) i.e., it is not permissible legally (2) that it be applied (3) to His knowledge, may He be exalted, in the sense (1) His statement: (and it is stronger in proof than the first, the first in my view: it is clearer in proof than the first because in the second he stated intention and choice, and he did not state them explicitly in the first although they are intended, so it does not face, for example, the spider's weaving and the bees' hives; and they made them a point of weakness for the first, but they are not considered because their action is accidental, while the action of the Lord, may He be exalted, the proof has been established that it is with intention and choice, so on this basis the outcome of the two proofs is one. And it is said: there is no preventing that the Lord places in them an inspired knowledge at that time, though we say: the action in reality belongs to Allah, not to them. Al-Amir. Meaning (2) His statement: (legally) its apparent meaning is that it is valid intellectually, but it is not so. Al-Amir. The poet said in his large commentary: (And not) i.e., it is not intellectually permissible for His knowledge, may He be exalted, to be acquired... Then he said: And when acquisition is intellectually impossible for Him, it is not permissible that (it be said) i.e., that it be applied to His knowledge that it is (acquired) for Him, may He be exalted; for whatever is intellectually impossible to be attributed to Him, may He be exalted, is legally impossible to be applied to Him, may He be exalted, and to His essential attributes. See: 'Umdat al-Mureed, vol. 1, p. 442. (3) The word 'yuqāl' is used for the meaning expressed by something like 'it applies' and 'it is said', and perhaps it is intended by it 'it is believed'. So if you interpret this word by the first meaning — as the commentator did — the meaning becomes: it is not permissible, legally or intellectually, that the word 'acquired' be applied and carried upon the knowledge of Allah, may He be exalted, so that it is said: 'His knowledge, glory be to Him, is acquired'. And this might give the illusion that what is prohibited is this statement, while the meaning is correct, and that the cause of the prohibition is the absence of permission from the Lawgiver to apply it. For this reason, it was better that we interpret the word 'yuqāl' in the author's expression as belief. On this basis, the meaning would be: It is not permissible for anyone to believe that His knowledge is acquired; that is because of the impossibility of that with respect to Allah, may He be exalted, from the aspect that 'acquired' and 'acquisitional' in the terminology of the people of knowledge is the knowledge that occurs after reflection and inference. If someone adduces a proof for the temporal origination of the world, the knowledge that occurs to him after this proof is acquired or acquisitional knowledge. And acquired knowledge is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted, because it requires the precedence of ignorance, since what occurs through reflection and inference does not produce knowledge.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired. The author begins by stating the first proof (al-istidlal al-awwal) from the impossibility of Allah's knowledge being acquired (istihalat kawn 'ilm Allah muktasaban). The phrase 'wa la yuqalu' (and it is not said) is explained: it means it is not legally permissible to apply the term 'acquired' to Allah's knowledge. The commentary then discusses the meaning of 'yuqalu' in detail. First, a footnote clarifies that the proof is stronger than the first one mentioned earlier because it explicitly involves intention and choice. Then, the meaning of 'shar'an' (legally) is examined: its apparent meaning might suggest that it is intellectually valid, but it is not; the poet in his larger commentary states that it is not intellectually permissible for Allah's knowledge to be acquired. The reasoning is that if acquisition is intellectually impossible for Him, then it is also legally impossible to apply the term to Him and His essential attributes. Next, the word 'yuqalu' is interpreted: it can mean 'it applies' or 'it is said' or 'it is believed'. The commentator prefers interpreting it as 'belief' to avoid the misconception that the prohibition is merely about the utterance while the meaning is correct. The underlying cause is the rational impossibility: acquired knowledge is defined as knowledge that comes after reflection and inference (al-nazar wa al-istidlal). For example, if someone uses a proof for the world's origination, the resulting knowledge is acquired. Such knowledge is impossible for Allah because it would require prior ignorance (sabq al-jahl). Therefore, the term 'muktasab' cannot be applied to Allah's knowledge either in speech or belief.

شرعا
اعتقاد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the prohibition is only about the word 'muktasab' and that the meaning is correct.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the prohibition is about belief, not just utterance, because the rational impossibility makes the belief itself invalid.

Checkpoint

A) Because the word 'muktasab' is not found in the Quran
B) Because acquisition is intellectually impossible for Allah
C) Because it would imply that Allah learns from others
D) Because it is a matter of convention

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of saying or believing that Allah's knowledge is acquired is ultimately based on its rational impossibility, not just lack of textual permission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this truth affect your humility in seeking knowledge, knowing that all your knowledge is ultimately a gift from the One whose knowledge is self-subsisting? Will you still take pride in your accomplishments after realizing this?
?
When you catch yourself speaking or thinking carelessly about Allah's attributes, do you feel the weight of the prohibition that arises from rational impossibility? How often do you reflect on the standards of speech about Allah before you open your mouth? What will you commit to checking your speech from this moment?
?
If the rational impossibility of Allah acquiring knowledge leads to a legal prohibition, what other beliefs or statements about Allah might be secretly irrational and therefore forbidden? Are you willing to examine your own assumptions about Allah's attributes with this same rigor, even if it means letting go of comfortable but incorrect ideas?

Defining Key Terms for Ambiguous Texts
Defines nass, tashbih, ta'wil, and tafwid in context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of nass, tashbih, ta'wil, and tafwid, and how they form the methodology for interpreting ambiguous Quranic and hadith texts.

Core Idea
Ambiguous texts are handled by interpretation or consignment, always intending transcendence.

This lesson establishes the foundational terminology needed to correctly approach verses that might suggest anthropomorphism. The passage defines four critical terms that form the basis of the Sunni methodology for dealing with ambiguous texts. Understanding these terms is essential for navigating theological discussions about Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the introductory rule followed by definitions of four terms.
The passage starts with a line from the matn summarizing the rule for ambiguous texts.
2
Identify the four definitions
Locate the definitions of nass, tashbih, ta'wil, and tafwid. Notice how each is carefully delimited in the context of ambiguous texts.
These definitions are technical and specific to this discipline.
3
Distinguish between ta'wil and tafwid
Focus on the difference: ta'wil involves specifying a suspected meaning, while tafwid leaves the true meaning to Allah without specification.
Both involve setting aside the apparent meaning, but they differ in the next step.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why these definitions matter: they protect divine transcendence while upholding the authority of the texts.
The goal is to avoid both anthropomorphism and rejection of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكُلُّ نصَّ أَوهَمَ التَّشْبِيها أَولُهُ أَو فَوَّضْ وَرُمْ تَنْزِيهَا. قسم الإلهيات. أما النص فالمراد به في هذا الموضع ما قابل الإجماع والقياس والاستنباط وهو منحصر في الدليل من الكتاب أو السنة سواء أكان صريحا أم ظاهرا. والمراد من التشبيه في هذا الموضع: المشابهة للحوادث. والمراد من التأويل هنا: حمل اللفظ على خلاف ظاهره مع بيان المعنى المراد، فيكون المكلف شيئان: أحدهما: أن يحكم بأن اللفظ مصروف عن ظاهره، وثانيهما: أن يؤول اللفظ تأويلا تفصيليا بأن يكون فيه بيان المعنى الذي يظن أنه المقصود من اللفظ. والمراد من التفويض: صرف اللفظ عن ظاهره، مع عدم التعرض لبيان المعنى المراد منه، بل يترك ويفوض علمه إلى الله تعالى، بأن يقول: الله أعلم بمراده.
And every text that suggests resemblance, interpret it or consign [the meaning] and intend its transcendence. Section of Ilahiyyat. As for the text (al-nass), what is meant by it in this context is what is opposed to consensus (ijma'), analogy (qiyas), and derivation (istinbat), and it is confined to evidence from the Book or the Sunnah, whether it is explicit (sarih) or apparent (zahir). And what is meant by resemblance (al-tashbih) in this context: likeness to contingent beings (al-hawadith). And what is meant by interpretation (al-ta'wil) here: carrying the word contrary to its apparent meaning while clarifying the intended meaning, so the obligated person has two things: one: that he judges that the word is turned from its apparent meaning, and the second: that he interprets the word with a detailed interpretation in which there is clarification of the meaning that he suspects is intended by the word. And what is meant by consignment (al-tafwid): diverting the word from its apparent meaning, without engaging in clarifying the intended meaning from it, rather leaving it and consigning its knowledge to Allah the Exalted, by saying: Allah knows best what He intends.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage defines four key terms necessary for understanding the treatment of ambiguous texts (al-nusus al-mutashabihat) that might imply anthropomorphism. It begins with the matn line stating the rule: for any text that suggests resemblance (tashbih), one must either interpret it (ta'wil) or consign its meaning (tafwid) while intending transcendence. Then the commentary defines 'nass' as any textual evidence from Quran or Sunnah, whether explicit or apparent, as opposed to consensus, analogy, or derivation. 'Tashbih' is defined as likeness to created things. 'Ta'wil' is explained as carrying the word away from its apparent meaning while specifying a likely intended meaning, requiring both a judgment that the apparent meaning is not intended and a detailed interpretation. 'Tafwid' is defined as diverting from the apparent meaning without specifying an alternative, leaving the true meaning to Allah alone. These definitions establish the two legitimate approaches for dealing with ambiguous texts.

Key Terms
نص
تشبيه
تأويل
تفويض
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'wil is arbitrary speculation without rules.
Correction: Ta'wil requires a reasoned suspicion (yuzann) of the intended meaning based on linguistic and contextual evidence, not free interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between ta'wil and tafwid?
A) Ta'wil specifies a likely meaning, while tafwid leaves the meaning to Allah.
B) Ta'wil is used by the Salaf, tafwid by the Khalaf.
C) Ta'wil abandons the apparent meaning, tafwid keeps it.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
The four definitions of nass, tashbih, ta'wil, and tafwid provide the methodological foundation for interpreting ambiguous texts while preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a Quranic verse that describes Allah's 'hand' or 'face', do you feel a pull to imagine it in a human way? What does this passage teach you about the responsibility to 'intend transcendence' in that moment? How can you train your mind to immediately reject any resemblance?
?
The passage defines ta'wil as requiring a 'suspected meaning'. Have you ever held onto an interpretation merely because it was comfortable or conventional, without honestly asking if it is truly 'suspected' based on evidence? Will you now examine your interpretations more critically?
?
Are you willing to admit that for some ambiguous texts, you simply do not know what Allah intends (tafwid)? Is your heart at peace with not knowing, or do you feel the need to have an answer? What does that need reveal about your trust in Allah's knowledge versus your own?

Defining Salaf and Khalaf and Their Methods
Defines Salaf and Khalaf and presents the famous maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical definitions of Salaf and Khalaf and the well-known maxim about the relative merits of their approaches to ambiguous texts.

Core Idea
The Salaf are safer; the Khalaf are more knowledgeable.

Before delving into the details of how early and later scholars handle ambiguous Quranic and Hadith texts, we need clear definitions of who the Salaf and Khalaf are. This lesson establishes those definitions and presents the famous comparison between their methods, setting the stage for understanding their deeper agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of Salaf and Khalaf
Notice the time periods given: before the end of the third or fifth century for Salaf, and after for Khalaf. Also note the key groups included in each category: Companions, Followers, four Imams for Salaf; later scholars for Khalaf.
The two centuries (3rd and 5th) reflect a range of opinion among scholars.
2
Understand the well-known maxim
Read the statement: 'The way of the Salaf is safer, and the way of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and more judicious.' Ask yourself why each is described that way.
3
Examine the reasoning behind the maxim
The passage explains that the Salaf avoid specifying any meaning that might not be intended, while the Khalaf provide clarity and refute opponents. Consider how both are valuable.
This sets up the next lesson on their agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد من السلف: من كانوا من أهل العلم قبل نهاية القرن الثالث الهجري أو الخامس، الصحابة، والتابعون، وتابعوهم، والأئمة الأربعة، وكبار علماء مذاهبهم. والمراد بالخلف من كان من العلماء بعد نهاية القرن الثالث الهجري أو الخامس. واعلم أنه اشتهر في ألسنة العلماء: أن طريق السلف في هذه المسألة أسلم، وطريقة الخلف أعلم وأحكم، ووجهه: أن طريقة السلف تشتمل على السلامة من تعيين معنى لا نستطيع أن نقول إنه مراد الله تعالى، وطريقة الخلف تشتمل على مزيد الإيضاح والرد على الخصوم. واعلم أيضا أن السلف والخلف متفقون على التأويل الإجمالي، وهو صرف النص الموهم عن ظاهره، بسبب أن ظاهره بحسب معناه اللغوي المعروف في الشاهد محال عليه تعالى، لكنهم مختلفون فيما وراء ذلك، مختلفون في التعرض لذكر المعنى المراد من النص، فالسلف لا يتعرضون لبيانه والخلف يتعرضون لبيانه. وحاصل هذه المسألة: أنه إذا ورد في القرآن الكريم أو في السنة المطهرة ما يشعر بإثبات: الجهة، أو الجسمية، أو الصورة، أو الجارحة، أو نحو ذلك مما هو من لوازم الحوادث، فإن أهل هذه الملة المحمدية خلفهم وسلفهم متفقون على وجوب صرف هذا النص عن ظاهره الذي يدل اللفظ عليه بحسب اللغة؛ لأنه يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عما دل عليه ظاهر هذا اللفظ، وخالف في هذه المسألة جماعة اشتهروا باسم (المجسمة)؛ لكونهم يثبتون الله تعالى ما هو من لوازم الأجسام، تعالى الله عما يقول المبطلون علوا كبيرا، سبحانه ليس كمثله شيء وهو السميع البصير.
And what is meant by the Salaf (al-salaf): those who were among the people of knowledge before the end of the third or fifth century AH, the Companions, the Followers, their followers, the four Imams, and the senior scholars of their schools. And what is meant by the Khalaf (al-khalaf): those among the scholars after the end of the third or fifth century AH. And know that it has become well-known among scholars: that the way of the Salaf in this matter is safer (aslam), and the way of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and more judicious (a'lam wa ahkam). Its aspect: that the way of the Salaf includes safety from specifying a meaning that we cannot say is intended by Allah the Exalted, while the way of the Khalaf includes additional clarification and refutation of opponents. And know also that the Salaf and Khalaf agree on the summary interpretation (al-ta'wil al-ijmali), which is diverting the ambiguous text from its apparent meaning, because its apparent meaning according to its known linguistic sense in the witnessed realm is impossible (muhal) with respect to the Exalted. However, they differ beyond that; they differ in engaging in mentioning the intended meaning from the text. So the Salaf do not engage in clarifying it, while the Khalaf engage in clarifying it. The conclusion of this matter: that if there occurs in the Noble Quran or the Purified Sunnah something that suggests affirming direction (jiha), corporeality (jismiyya), form (sura), limb (jarha), or the like, which are among the necessities of contingent beings, then the people of this Muhammadan community, their Khalaf and Salaf, agree on the obligation of diverting this text from its apparent meaning which the word indicates according to language, because it is obligatory to declare Allah the Exalted transcendent above what the apparent meaning of this word indicates. And a group known as 'the Corporealists' (al-mujassima) opposed in this matter, because they affirm for Allah the Exalted what is among the necessities of bodies. Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation. Glory be to Him, nothing is like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, All-Seeing.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms Salaf and Khalaf as historical categories of scholars, then contrasts their methods regarding ambiguous texts. It begins by defining Salaf as scholars before the end of the third or fifth century (the early generations), and Khalaf as those after that period. The passage then presents the well-known maxim: the method of the Salaf is safer (aslam) because they avoid specifying any interpretation that might be incorrect, while the method of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and judicious (a'lam wa ahkam) because it provides clearer explanation and refutes opponents. The passage clarifies that both groups agree on the essential point: the summary interpretation (al-ta'wil al-ijmali) which diverts the apparent meaning because the literal sense (e.g., direction, body, form) is impossible for Allah. Their disagreement is only about whether to specify a detailed interpretation: the Salaf refrain, the Khalaf engage. The conclusion reiterates the consensus on the necessity of diverting the text from its literal meaning when it implies anything like direction or body, as these are attributes of contingent beings. This consensus is contrasted with the deviant group al-Mujassima (Corporealists), who affirm such attributes for Allah. The passage ends with a declaration of Allah's absolute transcendence.

سلف
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main advantage of the Salaf's method?
A) It provides more detailed explanation.
B) It refutes opponents more effectively.
C) It avoids the risk of assigning an incorrect meaning to Allah's words.
D) It is more modern and applicable.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and Khalaf are defined by historical periods, and their methods differ: the Salaf prioritize safety from error, while the Khalaf prioritize clarity and refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse about Allah's attributes that you don't fully understand, do you feel compelled to interpret it to satisfy your intellect, or are you comfortable leaving its meaning to Allah? Which approach does your heart truly incline to—safety or explanation? What would it cost you to trust Allah's wisdom over your own reasoning?
?
The Salaf prioritized safety from error over the desire to explain. In your own faith, are there areas where you would rather have certainty through silence than risk being wrong through speculation? Can you name one belief where you are holding back from demanding an explanation, simply out of reverence for Allah?
?
The Khalaf aimed to clarify and refute opponents. Think of a time you defended your faith against a critic. Did your explanation come from a place of knowledge and judiciousness, or from pride? Reflect: are you more concerned with being right or with being safe from misrepresenting Allah?

Agreement on Summary Interpretation and Refutation of Anthropomorphists
Explains agreement on ta'wil ijmali and refutes the Mujassima.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Salaf and Khalaf agree on the necessity of diverting ambiguous texts from their literal meaning, and only differ on whether to specify a detailed interpretation, while the Mujassima deviate from this consensus.

Core Idea
Both Salaf and Khalaf reject the literal meaning of anthropomorphic descriptions.

While the Salaf and Khalaf differ in how much explanation they provide, they share a fundamental agreement: any text that seems to attribute direction, body, form, or limb to Allah must be interpreted in a way that upholds His transcendence. This lesson explores that agreement and highlights the deviant position of the Mujassima who insist on the literal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read about the agreement on ta'wil ijmali
Note that both groups agree on the summary interpretation: they turn away from the apparent literal meaning because it is impossible for Allah.
This is the core of Islamic orthodoxy.
2
Understand their point of disagreement
The Salaf do not specify a detailed interpretation; they leave the meaning to Allah. The Khalaf engage in specifying a meaning that is befitting of Allah.
Both are valid; the difference is in approach, not principle.
3
Identify the consensus and the deviant group
The passage concludes that all Muslims (Salaf and Khalaf) agree on diverting the text from its literal sense. Only the Mujassima (Corporealists) oppose this, affirming bodily attributes for Allah.
4
Reflect on the closing verses
The passage ends with a declaration of Allah's transcendence: 'Nothing is like Him.' This reinforces why the literal meaning cannot be accepted.

والمراد من السلف: من كانوا من أهل العلم قبل نهاية القرن الثالث الهجري أو الخامس، الصحابة، والتابعون، وتابعوهم، والأئمة الأربعة، وكبار علماء مذاهبهم. والمراد بالخلف من كان من العلماء بعد نهاية القرن الثالث الهجري أو الخامس. واعلم أنه اشتهر في ألسنة العلماء: أن طريق السلف في هذه المسألة أسلم، وطريقة الخلف أعلم وأحكم، ووجهه: أن طريقة السلف تشتمل على السلامة من تعيين معنى لا نستطيع أن نقول إنه مراد الله تعالى، وطريقة الخلف تشتمل على مزيد الإيضاح والرد على الخصوم. واعلم أيضا أن السلف والخلف متفقون على التأويل الإجمالي، وهو صرف النص الموهم عن ظاهره، بسبب أن ظاهره بحسب معناه اللغوي المعروف في الشاهد محال عليه تعالى، لكنهم مختلفون فيما وراء ذلك، مختلفون في التعرض لذكر المعنى المراد من النص، فالسلف لا يتعرضون لبيانه والخلف يتعرضون لبيانه. وحاصل هذه المسألة: أنه إذا ورد في القرآن الكريم أو في السنة المطهرة ما يشعر بإثبات: الجهة، أو الجسمية، أو الصورة، أو الجارحة، أو نحو ذلك مما هو من لوازم الحوادث، فإن أهل هذه الملة المحمدية خلفهم وسلفهم متفقون على وجوب صرف هذا النص عن ظاهره الذي يدل اللفظ عليه بحسب اللغة؛ لأنه يجب تنزيه الله تعالى عما دل عليه ظاهر هذا اللفظ، وخالف في هذه المسألة جماعة اشتهروا باسم (المجسمة)؛ لكونهم يثبتون الله تعالى ما هو من لوازم الأجسام، تعالى الله عما يقول المبطلون علوا كبيرا، سبحانه ليس كمثله شيء وهو السميع البصير.
And what is meant by the Salaf (al-salaf): those who were among the people of knowledge before the end of the third or fifth century AH, the Companions, the Followers, their followers, the four Imams, and the senior scholars of their schools. And what is meant by the Khalaf (al-khalaf): those among the scholars after the end of the third or fifth century AH. And know that it has become well-known among scholars: that the way of the Salaf in this matter is safer (aslam), and the way of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and more judicious (a'lam wa ahkam). Its aspect: that the way of the Salaf includes safety from specifying a meaning that we cannot say is intended by Allah the Exalted, while the way of the Khalaf includes additional clarification and refutation of opponents. And know also that the Salaf and Khalaf agree on the summary interpretation (al-ta'wil al-ijmali), which is diverting the ambiguous text from its apparent meaning, because its apparent meaning according to its known linguistic sense in the witnessed realm is impossible (muhal) with respect to the Exalted. However, they differ beyond that; they differ in engaging in mentioning the intended meaning from the text. So the Salaf do not engage in clarifying it, while the Khalaf engage in clarifying it. The conclusion of this matter: that if there occurs in the Noble Quran or the Purified Sunnah something that suggests affirming direction (jiha), corporeality (jismiyya), form (sura), limb (jarha), or the like, which are among the necessities of contingent beings, then the people of this Muhammadan community, their Khalaf and Salaf, agree on the obligation of diverting this text from its apparent meaning which the word indicates according to language, because it is obligatory to declare Allah the Exalted transcendent above what the apparent meaning of this word indicates. And a group known as 'the Corporealists' (al-mujassima) opposed in this matter, because they affirm for Allah the Exalted what is among the necessities of bodies. Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation. Glory be to Him, nothing is like Him, and He is the All-Hearing, All-Seeing.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms Salaf and Khalaf as historical categories of scholars, then contrasts their methods regarding ambiguous texts. It begins by defining Salaf as scholars before the end of the third or fifth century (the early generations), and Khalaf as those after that period. The passage then presents the well-known maxim: the method of the Salaf is safer (aslam) because they avoid specifying any interpretation that might be incorrect, while the method of the Khalaf is more knowledgeable and judicious (a'lam wa ahkam) because it provides clearer explanation and refutes opponents. The passage clarifies that both groups agree on the essential point: the summary interpretation (al-ta'wil al-ijmali) which diverts the apparent meaning because the literal sense (e.g., direction, body, form) is impossible for Allah. Their disagreement is only about whether to specify a detailed interpretation: the Salaf refrain, the Khalaf engage. The conclusion reiterates the consensus on the necessity of diverting the text from its literal meaning when it implies anything like direction or body, as these are attributes of contingent beings. This consensus is contrasted with the deviant group al-Mujassima (Corporealists), who affirm such attributes for Allah. The passage ends with a declaration of Allah's absolute transcendence.

مجسمة
جهة
جسمية

جارحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Salaf and Khalaf are in complete disagreement about how to handle ambiguous texts, with the Salaf accepting the literal meaning and the Khalaf denying it.
Correction: Actually, both agree on the necessity of turning away from the literal meaning (ta'wil ijmali). Their difference is only on whether to specify a detailed interpretation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what do the Salaf and Khalaf agree?
A) They agree on the detailed interpretation of all ambiguous texts.
B) They agree that the apparent meaning should be accepted literally.
C) They agree on the necessity of diverting the text from its literal meaning (ta'wil ijmali).
D) They agree that the Mujassima are correct.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and Khalaf are united in rejecting the literal meaning of anthropomorphic descriptions; only the Mujassima deviate from this consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'the Hand of Allah' or 'the Eye of Allah' in the Quran, do you feel any inner resistance to accepting that they cannot be taken literally? What does that resistance reveal about your own mental images of Allah, and what concrete step can you take to purify your conception of Allah from any trace of anthropomorphism?
?
This lesson shows that Salaf and Khalaf agree on the core principle of tanzih, even though they differ on methodology. Are there disagreements in your own community where you have focused on the differences and overlooked the shared principles? How can you shift your attitude to prioritize unity on fundamentals?
?
The Mujassima are condemned for affirming bodily attributes, but how often do you, in your daily thoughts, inadvertently imagine Allah as having human-like qualities? Commit to a specific practice—such as repeating 'laysa kamithlihi shay'—every time you catch yourself forming a mental image.

The Two Approaches to Ambiguous Texts: Tafwid and Ta'wil
Explains the two methods and the Salaf-Khalaf agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods for handling ambiguous scriptural texts, the three points of agreement between Salaf and Khalaf, and the single point of disagreement based on Quranic interpretation.

Core Idea
Tafwid and ta'wil both affirm tanzih; they differ only on specifying a figurative meaning.

This passage from a classical kalam text lays out the foundational hermeneutical principle for dealing with mutashabihat (ambiguous verses). It clarifies that both Salaf and Khalaf agree on declaring God transcendent and interpreting away the literal impossible meaning; their only dispute is whether to assign a specific figurative interpretation. As you read, pay attention to how the author distinguishes the two approaches and then summarizes the shared ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and the references to Quranic verses.
2
Identify the two methods
The passage begins by stating the two approaches: tafwid (consigning the detailed meaning to Allah) and ta'wil (figurative interpretation). Observe how the author gives examples (e.g., 'al-yad bi-l-qudra').
These examples illustrate tafwid in action.
3
Understand the Salaf's combined approach
The Salaf combine tafwid with tanzih (declaring transcendence). They affirm that the text is from Allah, but they consign the detailed meaning to Him while negating impossible attributes.
This shows that tafwid is not just silence; it includes a positive act of transcendence.
4
Recognize the three points of agreement
The author explicitly states that Salaf and Khalaf agree on: (1) tanzih from the impossible meaning, (2) ta'wil (taking the text away from its literal impossible sense), and (3) belief that the text is from Allah and brought by the Prophet. Find these in the text.
This is the key takeaway: they share these core commitments.
5
Pinpoint the disagreement and its basis
The disagreement is whether to specify a particular figurative interpretation (mahmal). This hinges on the interpretation of Quran 3:7: do the 'firmly grounded in knowledge' know the interpretation or stop at saying 'only Allah knows'?
This leads to the difference between tafwid (leaving it unspecified) and ta'wil (specifying a meaning).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك في النصوص الموهمة هو التفويض أو التأويل الصفة (۱) ، والوجه: بالذات أو بالوجود، واليد بالقدرة. وأشار لتنويع الخِلاف بقوله (أَوْ فَوِّض عِلْمَ المعنى المُرادِ مِن ذلك النَّصِّ تفصيلا إليه تعالى ، وَأَوَّلُهُ إجمالا كما هو طريق السلف (وَرُمْ) أي: اقصد واعتقد مع تفويض عِلْم ذلك المعنى (تَنْزِيها) له تعالى عما لا يليقُ به ؛ فالسَّلف ينزهونه سبحانه عمَّا يوهمه ذلك الظَّاهرُ مِن المعنى المُحال، ويفوضونَ علم حقيقته على التفصيل إليه تعالى، مع اعتقادِ أنَّ هذه النُّصوص من عنده سبحانه ؛ فظهرَ ممَّا قرَّرْنا اتِّفاقَ السَّلف والخلف على تنزيهه تعالى عن المعنى المُحال الذي دل عليه ذلك الظاهر، وعلى تأويله ، وإخراجه عن ظاهره المحال ، وعلى الإيمان بأنّه من عند الله جاءَ به رسول الله اختلفوا في تعيين محملٍ له معنى (۲) صحيح؛ وعَدَمِ تعيينه، بناءً على الوَقْفَ على قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ﴾ [آل عمران: ۷] ، أو على قوله: ﴿وَمَا يَعْلَمُ تَأْوِيلَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 7] .
The approach to texts that suggest [undesirable meanings] is either tafwid (consigning) or ta'wil (interpretation). [For example,] al-Sifah (the attribute) (1), and al-Wajh (the face): by the essence or by existence, and al-Yad (the hand): by power. And he indicated the division of the disagreement by his saying: 'Or consign the knowledge of the intended meaning of that text in detail to Him, Exalted, and interpret it in summary as is the way of the Salaf. And intend, i.e., intend and believe along with consigning the knowledge of that meaning, a tanzih (declaring transcendence) of Him, Exalted, from what is not befitting Him.' So the Salaf declare Him transcendent above what that apparent [meaning] suggests from the impossible meaning, and they consign the knowledge of its reality in detail to Him, Exalted, with the belief that these texts are from Him, Exalted. So it became clear from what we have established the agreement of the Salaf and the Khalaf upon declaring Him transcendent above the impossible meaning that the apparent [meaning] indicates, and upon interpreting it (ta'wil) and removing it from its impossible apparent meaning, and upon believing that it is from Allah brought by the Messenger of Allah, but they differed in specifying a sound interpretation (mahmal) for it and not specifying it, based on stopping at His saying: 'And those firmly grounded in knowledge' [Al 'Imran:7], or at His saying: 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah' [Al 'Imran:7].
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage discusses the two approaches to ambiguous scriptural texts that suggest anthropomorphism or other impossible attributes. The author states that the method is either tafwid (consigning the true meaning to God) or ta'wil (figurative interpretation). He gives examples: the attribute (sifah) is interpreted as the attribute itself or something else? Actually the footnote might elaborate. Then he explains the line of the poem that says 'Or consign the knowledge of the intended meaning in detail to Him, and interpret it in summary as is the way of the Salaf.' The author clarifies that the Salaf combine tafwid of the detailed knowledge with tanzih (declaring God transcendent). They affirm that the texts are from Allah and came via the Prophet. Then he shows that both Salaf and Khalaf agree on three things: tanzih from the impossible meaning, ta'wil (taking it away from the literal impossible meaning), and belief that the text is from Allah. Their disagreement is whether to specify a particular figurative interpretation (ta'yin mahmal) or not. This disagreement is based on the interpretation of Quranic verses about the 'firmly grounded in knowledge' – are they included among those who know the interpretation or not? So the passage presents the foundational hermeneutical principle for dealing with mutashabihat.
