Correction: The passage shows that tafwid is combined with tanzih (declaring God transcendent) and a general ta'wil (interpretation away from the literal impossible meaning). It is not a passive rejection but an active affirmation of transcendence and a commitment to the text's divine origin.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of agreement between Salaf and Khalaf regarding ambiguous texts?
A) They both specify a figurative meaning.
B) They both resort to tafwid only.
C) They both agree on tanzih and ta'wil but differ on specifying a meaning.
D) They both reject the texts.

Key Takeaway
Both Salaf and Khalaf agree on tanzih and ta'wil but differ on specifying a figurative meaning; tafwid is not a denial of interpretation but a combination of transcendence and consignment of detailed knowledge to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's 'hand' or 'face', do you feel a sense of peace in consigning the detailed meaning to Allah, or does your mind restless try to pin down exactly what it means? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom? Are you willing to stand in awe of a God whose nature you cannot fully comprehend?
?
This lesson teaches that both Salaf and Khalaf agree on tanzih. Is there any attribute of Allah you are tempted to imagine in a human-like way, despite knowing it's impossible? Would you be willing to surrender that mental image and simply say, 'I believe, but the reality is known only to You'?
?
The disagreement is merely over specifying a figurative meaning. Have you ever argued with a fellow Muslim over such a fine point, forgetting that the core commitment to tanzih unites you? How can you learn from this lesson to prioritize the common ground of transcendence over secondary differences?

The Eternal Speech of Allah and the Interpretation of Temporal Verses
The commentator states the Sunni position on the eternity of Allah's speech and interprets temporal verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni belief that Allah's speech is eternal and how verses indicating temporality are interpreted to refer to the recited words, not the divine attribute.

Core Idea
The Quran's words are created, but the attribute of speech is eternal.

This passage establishes the fundamental Sunni stance on the nature of the Quran. It clarifies that apparent temporal references in revelation must be interpreted as pointing to the recited text, which is created, while Allah's speech in His essence is eternal. This distinction protects the doctrine from Mu'tazilite claims that the Quran itself is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'kalam Allah' as eternal and 'Quran' as temporal in the verses.
The passage begins by stating the Sunni position against the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the two aspects of 'speech'
The passage distinguishes between the eternal attribute (kalam nafsi) and the temporally revealed words (lafz). See how the verses are carried to the lafz.
This is the key hermeneutic principle for understanding temporal references.
3
Understand the principle of carrying verses
Every verse that seems to indicate temporality is interpreted as referring to the recited word that points to the eternal attribute. Reflect on how this defends Sunni doctrine.
This protects from the Mu'tazilite conclusion that speech itself is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام الله تعالى قديم عند أهل السُّنَّة خلافًا للمعتزلة
حدوث القرآن ، مثل : ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ ﴾ [القدر: ١]، ﴿إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِّكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفِظُونَ ) [ الحجر : ۹] (احْمِلْ) أَيُّها السُّنِّيُّ ( عَلَى القُرآنِ بمعنى (اللَّفْظِ) المُنزَّل على نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم (الَّذِي قَدْ دَلَّا على تلك الصفة القديمة القائمة به ، يعني أنَّ كُلَّ ظاهرٍ مِن الكتاب والسُّنَّة وَرَدَ دالا على حدوث كلام الله تعالى، فإنَّه عندنا محمولٌ على أنَّ المُتَّصِفَ بذلك إنَّما هو اللفظ الدال على الكلامِ النَّفسي (١) ، لا على المعنى النَّفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى ؛ لأنَّه لا نزاع في إطلاق لَفْظَي «القرآن» و «كلام الله إما بطريق الاشتراك ، وهو الأرجح (۲) أو المجاز والحقيقة - على هذا المُؤَلَّف الحادث (۳) ، كما هو المُتعارف عند
(1) قوله : (المتصف بذلك إنما هو (اللفظ لكن منع الإمام أحمد أن يقال: لفظي بالقرآن حادث ، وإن كان صحيحاً في نفسه ؛ لكنه ربما أوهم ، وقد يُلبّسُ به المبتدع، ذكر ابن حجر في «فتح الباري أول من قال لفظي بالقرآن مخلوق : الحسين بن علي الكرابيسي أحد أصحاب الإمام الشافعي، فلما بلغ ذلك الإمام أحمد بدعه وهجره، ثم قال بذلك داود الأصبهاني رأس الظاهرية وهو يومئذ بنيسابور ، فأنكر عليه إسحاق ، وبلغ ذلك أحمد فلما قدم بغداد لم يأذن له بالدخول عليه . نعم يجوز ذلك في مقام التعليم فقط . الأمير . (۲) قوله: (وهو الأرجح) بدليل كفر من قال: هذه السورة ليست كلام الله. على أنَّ الأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة الأمير . (۳) قوله: (أو المجاز والحقيقة على هذا المؤلف الحادث في العبارة حذف الواو وما عطفت عليه، والتقدير على هذا المؤلف الحادث والمعنى القديم وهو من باب اللف والنشر المشوش ، فالحقيقة للمعنى القديم، والمجاز للفظ الحادث، لكن إطلاق القرآن على اللفظي أشهر من إطلاقه على النفسي ، وكلامه بالعكس . اهـ ملوي والظاهر : أنه مرتب ، والحاصل أن الذي ينبغي : أنّ المجاز راجع لعنوان كلام الله تعالى؛ فإنه قيل: إنه حقيقة في النفسي مجاز في اللفظي المؤلف، والحقيقة راجعة لعنوان القرآن فإنه قيل: حقيقة في المؤلف الحادث وفي القديم مجاز ، فكلا القولين يقابلان الاشتراك فيهما الذي ذكره أولا ، فتدبر المقال وافهمه على هذا المنوال ودع عنك ما قيل أو يقال ، ولا تنظر لمن قال . الأمير. وعند الشنواني ومحصل الأقوال في وضع القرآن وكلام الله لأي شيء وضع ثلاثة: فقيل:=
The speech of Allah, exalted, is eternal according to the People of the Sunnah, contrary to the Mu'tazila.
Temporality of the Quran, such as: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' [al-Qadr 97:1], 'Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance and indeed We are its preservers' [al-Hijr 15:9]. (Carry) O Sunni (upon the Quran meaning the word (lafẓ)) sent down upon our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, (which has indicated) that eternal attribute subsisting in Him. That is, every apparent text from the Book and the Sunnah that occurs indicating the temporality of Allah's speech, exalted, is according to us carried to the fact that the one described thereby is only the word indicating the inner speech (kalām nafsī) (1), not the old inner meaning subsisting in His essence, exalted; because there is no dispute in applying the two terms 'Quran' and 'Allah's speech' either by way of commonality (ishtirāk), which is the most correct (2), or by metaphor and reality - to this composed temporal thing (3), as is customary.
(1) He said: (the one described thereby is only the word). However, Imam Ahmad forbade saying 'my utterance of the Quran is temporal', even if it is correct in itself, but because it might lead to illusion, and the innovator might confuse with it. Ibn Hajar mentioned in 'Fath al-Bari' the first to say 'my utterance of the Quran is created' was al-Husayn ibn Ali al-Karabisi, one of the companions of Imam al-Shafi'i. When that reached Imam Ahmad, he declared him an innovator and abandoned him. Then Dawud al-Asbahani, the head of the Zahiri school, said that while he was in Nishapur. Ishaq objected to him, and that reached Ahmad. When he came to Baghdad, he did not permit him to enter upon him. Yes, it is permissible only in a teaching context. al-Amir. (2) He said: (which is the most correct) evidenced by the unbelief of the one who says: this sura is not the speech of Allah. And the principle in absolute usage is reality. al-Amir. (3) He said: (or metaphor and reality upon this temporal composed thing). In the expression there is omission of the waw and what is conjoined to it. The estimation is: upon this temporal composed thing and the old meaning, and it is from the category of disordered arrangement (laff wa nashr mushabbash). So reality is for the old meaning, and metaphor is for the temporal word. However, the application of 'Quran' to the verbal is more famous than its application to the inner, and 'His speech' is the opposite. End of Mulawi. And apparent that it is arranged. The conclusion is that what should be is that the metaphor returns to the title of 'Allah's speech': for it is said: it is reality for the inner and metaphor for the verbal composed. And the reality returns to the title of 'Quran': for it is said: reality in the temporal composed and in the old metaphor. So both sayings are parallel to the commonality (ishtirak) which he mentioned first. So reflect on the article and understand it in this manner, and leave aside what is said or to be said, and do not look at who said it. al-Amir. And according to al-Shanwani, the summary of the sayings concerning the institution of 'Quran' and 'Allah's speech' for anything is three: it was said:=
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the same line, focusing on the linguistic usage of the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah'. It begins by reiterating that Allah's speech is eternal according to Ahl al-Sunnah, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The commentator then explains that the apparent texts indicating temporality (like the verses of descent and the verse about 'newly revealed' mention) should be carried to the recited word (lafẓ) that indicates the eternal attribute. The key point is that there is no dispute that the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' are applied to the composed temporal thing either by way of commonality (ishtirak), which is the most correct view, or by metaphor and reality. The commentator then provides footnotes: The first footnote discusses that although it is correct that the recited words are temporal, Imam Ahmad forbade saying 'my utterance of the Quran is temporal' because it may cause confusion and be exploited by innovators. It mentions al-Karabisi and Dawud al-Asbahani who were criticized for such statements, but allows it in teaching contexts. The second footnote gives evidence for the superiority of ishtirak: disbelief of anyone who says 'this sura is not the speech of Allah', and the principle that original usage is reality. The third footnote analyzes the metaphor/reality view, explaining that if both are used, then reality is for the old meaning and metaphor for the temporal word, but the application of 'Quran' to the verbal is more common, while 'kalam Allah' to the inner is more common. The conclusion is that the two sets of views (ishtirak vs. metaphor/reality) are parallel. The passage ends with a reference to al-Shanwani that the total views are three, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran, in all its aspects, is created.
Correction: The recited words of the Quran are created, but the divine attribute of speech is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is meant by 'kalam Allah' when it is said to be eternal?
A) The recited words of the Quran
B) The inner attribute subsisting in Allah's essence
C) The physical pages and ink
D) The meaning understood by humans

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is one eternal attribute; the recited Quran is its temporal expression, and verses indicating temporality are interpreted accordingly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consciously differentiate between the eternal attribute of Allah's speech and the temporal words you utter? How does this distinction affect your reverence during recitation?
?
The passage teaches that verses about 'sending down' refer only to the verbal form. Is there any verse or concept you have misunderstood as indicating a change in Allah? How will you guard against such misinterpretations?
?
Knowing that Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated, what does that demand from you when you hear or read the Quran? Will you commit to treating every ayah as a direct address from an eternal, unchanging Lord?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

Applying the Terms 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah': Commonality or Metaphor?
The commentator explains the linguistic modes for applying 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' to both the eternal attribute and the temporal composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible ways the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' are applied: by commonality (ishtirak), by metaphor (majaz), and by reality (haqiqa), and why commonality is considered the most correct.

Core Idea
The terms 'Quran' and 'Allah's speech' are used flexibly; the safest view is commonality.

After establishing that Allah's speech includes both an eternal attribute and a temporal expression, this passage explores how we linguistically refer to these different realities. The commentator explains that the terms are used either by commonality (ishtirak) or by a combination of metaphor and reality, with ishtirak being the strongest view supported by evidence from disbelief rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two main views
The passage states there is no dispute that the terms apply to the temporal composed thing, but there are two (or three) ways: commonality or metaphor/reality. Find these in the text.
The phrase 'إما بطريق الاشتراك، وهو الأرجح' indicates the preferred view.
2
Understand the evidence for ishtirak
The commentator notes that disbelief is incurred by saying 'this sura is not the speech of Allah', which shows the term 'kalam Allah' applies to the sura in reality, supporting commonality.
This is a strong theological argument for ishtirak.
3
Analyze the metaphor/reality view
The commentator explains a detailed analysis where 'kalam Allah' is reality for the inner and metaphor for the verbal, while 'Quran' is reality for the verbal and metaphor for the inner. This is the laff wa nashr.
This view also respects the dual application but in a different manner.
4
Compare the two views
The conclusion is that both the ishtirak view and the metaphor/reality view are parallel ways of handling the dual application. Neither is wrong, but ishtirak is preferred.
The passage encourages reflection and not to be distracted by who said what.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام الله تعالى قديم عند أهل السُّنَّة خلافًا للمعتزلة
حدوث القرآن ، مثل : ﴿إِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ ﴾ [القدر: ١]، ﴿إِنَّا نَحْنُ نَزَّلْنَا الذِّكْرَ وَإِنَّا لَهُ لَحَفِظُونَ ) [ الحجر : ۹] (احْمِلْ) أَيُّها السُّنِّيُّ ( عَلَى القُرآنِ بمعنى (اللَّفْظِ) المُنزَّل على نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم (الَّذِي قَدْ دَلَّا على تلك الصفة القديمة القائمة به ، يعني أنَّ كُلَّ ظاهرٍ مِن الكتاب والسُّنَّة وَرَدَ دالا على حدوث كلام الله تعالى، فإنَّه عندنا محمولٌ على أنَّ المُتَّصِفَ بذلك إنَّما هو اللفظ الدال على الكلامِ النَّفسي (١) ، لا على المعنى النَّفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى ؛ لأنَّه لا نزاع في إطلاق لَفْظَي «القرآن» و «كلام الله إما بطريق الاشتراك ، وهو الأرجح (۲) أو المجاز والحقيقة - على هذا المُؤَلَّف الحادث (۳) ، كما هو المُتعارف عند
(1) قوله : (المتصف بذلك إنما هو (اللفظ لكن منع الإمام أحمد أن يقال: لفظي بالقرآن حادث ، وإن كان صحيحاً في نفسه ؛ لكنه ربما أوهم ، وقد يُلبّسُ به المبتدع، ذكر ابن حجر في «فتح الباري أول من قال لفظي بالقرآن مخلوق : الحسين بن علي الكرابيسي أحد أصحاب الإمام الشافعي، فلما بلغ ذلك الإمام أحمد بدعه وهجره، ثم قال بذلك داود الأصبهاني رأس الظاهرية وهو يومئذ بنيسابور ، فأنكر عليه إسحاق ، وبلغ ذلك أحمد فلما قدم بغداد لم يأذن له بالدخول عليه . نعم يجوز ذلك في مقام التعليم فقط . الأمير . (۲) قوله: (وهو الأرجح) بدليل كفر من قال: هذه السورة ليست كلام الله. على أنَّ الأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة الأمير . (۳) قوله: (أو المجاز والحقيقة على هذا المؤلف الحادث في العبارة حذف الواو وما عطفت عليه، والتقدير على هذا المؤلف الحادث والمعنى القديم وهو من باب اللف والنشر المشوش ، فالحقيقة للمعنى القديم، والمجاز للفظ الحادث، لكن إطلاق القرآن على اللفظي أشهر من إطلاقه على النفسي ، وكلامه بالعكس . اهـ ملوي والظاهر : أنه مرتب ، والحاصل أن الذي ينبغي : أنّ المجاز راجع لعنوان كلام الله تعالى؛ فإنه قيل: إنه حقيقة في النفسي مجاز في اللفظي المؤلف، والحقيقة راجعة لعنوان القرآن فإنه قيل: حقيقة في المؤلف الحادث وفي القديم مجاز ، فكلا القولين يقابلان الاشتراك فيهما الذي ذكره أولا ، فتدبر المقال وافهمه على هذا المنوال ودع عنك ما قيل أو يقال ، ولا تنظر لمن قال . الأمير. وعند الشنواني ومحصل الأقوال في وضع القرآن وكلام الله لأي شيء وضع ثلاثة: فقيل:=
The speech of Allah, exalted, is eternal according to the People of the Sunnah, contrary to the Mu'tazila.
Temporality of the Quran, such as: 'Indeed, We sent it down on the Night of Decree' [al-Qadr 97:1], 'Indeed, We have sent down the Remembrance and indeed We are its preservers' [al-Hijr 15:9]. (Carry) O Sunni (upon the Quran meaning the word (lafẓ)) sent down upon our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, (which has indicated) that eternal attribute subsisting in Him. That is, every apparent text from the Book and the Sunnah that occurs indicating the temporality of Allah's speech, exalted, is according to us carried to the fact that the one described thereby is only the word indicating the inner speech (kalām nafsī) (1), not the old inner meaning subsisting in His essence, exalted; because there is no dispute in applying the two terms 'Quran' and 'Allah's speech' either by way of commonality (ishtirāk), which is the most correct (2), or by metaphor and reality - to this composed temporal thing (3), as is customary.
(1) He said: (the one described thereby is only the word). However, Imam Ahmad forbade saying 'my utterance of the Quran is temporal', even if it is correct in itself, but because it might lead to illusion, and the innovator might confuse with it. Ibn Hajar mentioned in 'Fath al-Bari' the first to say 'my utterance of the Quran is created' was al-Husayn ibn Ali al-Karabisi, one of the companions of Imam al-Shafi'i. When that reached Imam Ahmad, he declared him an innovator and abandoned him. Then Dawud al-Asbahani, the head of the Zahiri school, said that while he was in Nishapur. Ishaq objected to him, and that reached Ahmad. When he came to Baghdad, he did not permit him to enter upon him. Yes, it is permissible only in a teaching context. al-Amir. (2) He said: (which is the most correct) evidenced by the unbelief of the one who says: this sura is not the speech of Allah. And the principle in absolute usage is reality. al-Amir. (3) He said: (or metaphor and reality upon this temporal composed thing). In the expression there is omission of the waw and what is conjoined to it. The estimation is: upon this temporal composed thing and the old meaning, and it is from the category of disordered arrangement (laff wa nashr mushabbash). So reality is for the old meaning, and metaphor is for the temporal word. However, the application of 'Quran' to the verbal is more famous than its application to the inner, and 'His speech' is the opposite. End of Mulawi. And apparent that it is arranged. The conclusion is that what should be is that the metaphor returns to the title of 'Allah's speech': for it is said: it is reality for the inner and metaphor for the verbal composed. And the reality returns to the title of 'Quran': for it is said: reality in the temporal composed and in the old metaphor. So both sayings are parallel to the commonality (ishtirak) which he mentioned first. So reflect on the article and understand it in this manner, and leave aside what is said or to be said, and do not look at who said it. al-Amir. And according to al-Shanwani, the summary of the sayings concerning the institution of 'Quran' and 'Allah's speech' for anything is three: it was said:=
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the same line, focusing on the linguistic usage of the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah'. It begins by reiterating that Allah's speech is eternal according to Ahl al-Sunnah, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The commentator then explains that the apparent texts indicating temporality (like the verses of descent and the verse about 'newly revealed' mention) should be carried to the recited word (lafẓ) that indicates the eternal attribute. The key point is that there is no dispute that the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' are applied to the composed temporal thing either by way of commonality (ishtirak), which is the most correct view, or by metaphor and reality. The commentator then provides footnotes: The first footnote discusses that although it is correct that the recited words are temporal, Imam Ahmad forbade saying 'my utterance of the Quran is temporal' because it may cause confusion and be exploited by innovators. It mentions al-Karabisi and Dawud al-Asbahani who were criticized for such statements, but allows it in teaching contexts. The second footnote gives evidence for the superiority of ishtirak: disbelief of anyone who says 'this sura is not the speech of Allah', and the principle that original usage is reality. The third footnote analyzes the metaphor/reality view, explaining that if both are used, then reality is for the old meaning and metaphor for the temporal word, but the application of 'Quran' to the verbal is more common, while 'kalam Allah' to the inner is more common. The conclusion is that the two sets of views (ishtirak vs. metaphor/reality) are parallel. The passage ends with a reference to al-Shanwani that the total views are three, but the text cuts off.

الحقيقة
المجاز
اللف والنشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' are used univocally, always referring to the same thing.
Correction: The terms are applied to both the eternal attribute and the temporal composition, either by commonality or by metaphor and reality.

Checkpoint
Which view does the commentator consider the most correct regarding the usage of 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah'?
A) They are used by way of commonality (ishtirāk).
B) They are used by way of metaphor (majāz).
C) They are used by way of reality (haqīqa) for both.
D) They are not used at all.

Key Takeaway
The most correct view is that the terms 'Quran' and 'kalam Allah' are used by commonality to refer to both the eternal attribute and the temporal words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator warns against being distracted by scholarly opinions. Have you ever used theological debates as an excuse to avoid acting on what you know? How can you focus on the essence of the Quran as Allah's speech rather than getting lost in technicalities?
?
If the Quran is literally Allah's speech in both its eternal essence and its temporal form, what does that demand from you when you hear it recited? Will you listen with the reverence due to a direct address from your Creator?

Why Imam Ahmad Forbade Saying 'My Utterance is Created'
The commentator reports Imam Ahmad's prohibition of saying 'my utterance is created' with historical examples and an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the caution of Imam Ahmad in forbidding the phrase 'my utterance of the Quran is created' and the historical context, including the cases of al-Karabisi and Dawud al-Asbahani.

Core Idea
Some true statements can be harmful if they lead to confusion or are exploited by heretics.

This passage recounts an important episode in early Islamic theological debates. Imam Ahmad strongly prohibited saying that one's utterance of the Quran is created, even though the statement is technically correct. Understanding the reasons behind this prohibition teaches us the importance of prudence in theological expression and the protection of sound doctrine from subtle misrepresentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Imam Ahmad's prohibition
The footnote explains that Imam Ahmad forbade saying 'my utterance of the Quran is temporal/created'. Read the passage and note the reasoning.
The prohibition is despite the statement being correct in itself.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
The reason is that the statement might lead to illusion (tawahhum) and could be exploited by innovators (mubtadi') to promote false beliefs.
This shows awareness of how statements can be misused.
3
Learn the historical examples
Two scholars—al-Karabisi and Dawud al-Asbahani—said such a statement and were denounced by Imam Ahmad and others. This illustrates the practical application of the ruling.
Al-Karabisi was a companion of al-Shafi'i; Dawud al-Asbahani was the head of the Zahiri school.
4
Note the exception
Imam Ahmad allowed such statements only in a teaching context where the precise meaning is explained clearly.
This exception shows the ruling is situational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you insisted on speaking a theological truth even though you knew it might confuse a weaker Muslim? Was your priority your pride in being correct, or the spiritual safety of your listener? What will you change today about how you speak about matters of faith?
?
Imam Ahmad was willing to sever ties with respected scholars for saying something factually true but potentially harmful. Is there a relationship or habit in your life where you are clinging to a 'right' position that is actually causing division or confusion? Are you ready to let it go for the sake of the ummah's unity?
?
The passage says that even a correct statement can be prohibited if it leads to illusion. Think of a belief you hold about Allah or the Quran that you have expressed forcefully. Could your expression have led someone to misunderstand Allah's attributes? Will you now examine your words and seek to clarify rather than just assert?

Negation and Universal: The Logical Rule and Its Exception
Al-Laqqani resolves an apparent contradiction using logical and contextual analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Arabic logical rules for negation and universality interact, and how context can override the default interpretation to express absolute negation.

Core Idea
Negation before universal usually allows exceptions, but context can flip it to absolute negation.

Al-Laqqani here tackles a potential logical objection to the matn’s claim that the servant never creates any action. The phrase 'ليس كلاً يفعل اختيارا' would normally imply the servant creates some actions, but he shows that the logical rule is not absolute—context can shift the meaning to absolute negation. Let’s follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the grammatical parsing and the logical rule presented.
The passage is a hashiah, so it assumes knowledge of the matn line.
2
Understand the default logical rule
Identify the rule: when negation precedes a universal particle (e.g., ليس كل), it negates the whole but allows for exceptions. The example 'ليس كل طالب يؤدي واجباته' helps illustrate.
This is standard logic in Arabic.
3
See the problem
Apply the rule to the matn's phrase: 'ليس كلاً يفعل اختيارا'. It would yield 'the servant creates some actions', contradicting the intended meaning.
4
Learn the exception
Al-Laqqani explains that the rule is a majority rule, not absolute. Sometimes negation before universal intends absolute negation (عموم السلب). The Quranic verse and the matn's own context serve as indicators.
The phrase 'ولم يكن مؤثرًا' is the key contextual clue.
5
Reflect on contextual indicators
Note how the Quranic verse 'والله لا يحب كل مختال فخور' uses the same structure to mean 'no one', not 'some'. Context decides.
Kalam often overrides pure logic with contextual reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) (يفعل» في قول المصنف وليس كلا يفعل اختيارا بمعنى: يخلق، بدلالة المقام، و«كلا» مفعول مُقدَّم ليفعل ، واختيارا» منصوب على نزع الخافض ، وظاهر هذه العبارة بحسب قواعد المنطق فاسد ؛ لأنَّ القاعدة أنَّه إذا اجتمع في الكلام أداة دالة على النفي مثل «لا» وليس» و «ما» وأداةٌ دالة على العموم مثل «كل» و «جميع» و«عامة»، فإن تقدمت أداة النفي على أداة العموم دلَّ الكلام على سلب العموم، أي: على أنَّ النفي مُسلَّطٌ على المجموع مِن حيث هو مجموع ، وهذا لا ينافي أنّ بعض الأفراد خارج من الحكم؛ فإذا قلت: «ليس كل طالب يؤدي واجباته فإنَّ المعنى أنَّ مجموع الطلاب من حيث هم مجموع لا يؤدون واجباتهم ؛ فلا ينافي أنَّ بعض الأفراد من الطلاب يؤدون واجباتهم، أما لو تقدمت أداة العموم على أداة النَّفي فإنَّ المعنى يَصير : أنَّ كلَّ فردٍ مِن الأفراد محكوم عليه بالحكم المنفي ، ويسمى عموم السلب ، فإن قلت: كلُّ مسلم لا يُقَصِّر في واجبه» فمعنى ذلك أنَّ كلَّ فردٍ مِن أفراد المسلمين لا يقصر في واجبه ، وإذا طبقت هذه القاعدة على قول المصنف: «ليس كُلَّا يفعل اختيارا أفاد هذا الكلام أنَّ العبد يخلق بعض أفعالِ نفسه الاختيارية ، مع أنَّ المراد أنَّه لا يخلق فعلا أبدا ، والجواب: أنَّ هذه القاعدة التي قرّرناها أغلبية ، يعني أنَّ الغالب أن يكون الأمر على ما ذكرنا، وقد تأتي أداة النَّفي مُتقدِّمَةٌ على أداة العموم، ويكون المراد عموم السَّلب، وما هنا من هذا القبيل، ونظيره قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ فَخُورٍ ) [الحديد: ۲۳] والقرينة على أن المراد بهذه العبارة عموم السَّلب قول المصنف فيما سبق ولم يكن مؤثرًا».
(1) 'Yaf'alu' in the statement of the author 'wa laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyaran' means 'creates,' by context, and 'kullan' is a fronted object of 'yaf'alu', and 'ikhtiyaran' is in the accusative due to removal of the preposition. And the apparent meaning of this statement according to the rules of logic is unsound, because the rule is that when a particle indicating negation such as 'la' or 'laysa' or 'ma' and a particle indicating universality such as 'kull' or 'jami'' or 'ammah' come together in speech, if the negation particle precedes the universal particle, then the speech indicates negation of the universal, i.e., that the negation is applied to the whole as a whole, and this does not negate that some individuals are outside the ruling. For if you say: 'Not every student performs his duties,' then the meaning is that the totality of students as a whole do not perform their duties, so it does not negate that some individuals among the students perform their duties. But if the universal particle precedes the negation particle, then the meaning becomes: that every individual is ruled by the negated judgment, and it is called 'universality of negation.' If you say: 'Every Muslim does not fail in his duty,' then the meaning is that every individual among the Muslims does not fail in his duty. And if you apply this rule to the author's statement: 'Laysa kullan yaf'alu ikhtiyaran,' it would yield the meaning that the servant creates some of his voluntary actions, while the intended meaning is that he never creates any action. The answer: that this rule we have stated is a majority rule, meaning that mostly the matter is as we mentioned, but sometimes the negation particle precedes the universal particle and the intended meaning is universality of negation, and this is of that kind. Its parallel is the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And Allah does not love every conceited boaster' [Quran 57:23]. And the indicator that what is intended by this statement is universality of negation is the author's previous statement: 'and he is not effective.'
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a footnote (hashiya) addressing a potential logical objection to the line 'ليس كلاً يفعل اختيارا.' It begins by explaining the grammatical parsing: 'يفعل' means 'creates,' 'كلا' is a fronted object, 'اختيارا' is accusative. Then it presents the logical rule: when negation precedes a universal (e.g., 'ليس كل'), it negates the whole but allows for exceptions. However, applying this rule to the line would imply the servant creates some actions, contradicting the intended meaning. The answer is that this rule is a majority rule, not absolute. Sometimes negation precedes the universal and the intended meaning is absolute negation (عموم السلب), as in the Quranic verse. The indicator here is the author's earlier statement 'ولم يكن مؤثرًا' (he is not effective). Thus, the phrase is understood as absolute negation: the servant never creates any action.

Key Terms
نفي
عموم
سلب
قاعدة أغلبية
قرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The logical rule that negation before universal always allows for exceptions is absolute and cannot be overridden.
Correction: The rule is a majority rule; context can indicate absolute negation, as shown by the Quranic verse and the author's prior statement.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Laqqani, what does the logical rule state when negation precedes a universal particle?
A) It implies absolute negation of every individual.
B) It negates the group as a whole but does not preclude some individuals from being excluded.
C) It has no effect on the meaning.
D) It always indicates the negation is applied to the universal as a whole, making exceptions impossible.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, logical rules are subject to contextual indicators, and absolute negation can be conveyed even when negation precedes a universal.

?

?
The author shows that even a respected logical rule can be overridden by a contextual clue from earlier text. Are there verses or statements in the Quran and Sunnah that you have interpreted too rigidly, missing the deeper intent? What would it take for you to humbly re-examine them?
?
The phrase 'ليس كلاً يفعل اختيارا' appears to contradict the mainstream Ash'ari position, but the author resolves it by looking at context. When you encounter apparent contradictions in Islamic teachings, do you dismiss them or do you dig deeper for resolution? Understanding the Specification of the Verse 'Everything is Perishing'
Al-Amidi explains the takhsis of the verse on perishing, listing exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars have dealt with the apparent generality of the Qur'anic statement that everything perishes, through either specifying exceptions or reinterpreting the meaning of 'perishing'.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's general statements can be specified or reinterpreted to harmonize with other doctrines.

The verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' seems to imply that all creation will be annihilated. However, Sunni theologians had to reconcile this with the belief that certain things like the soul and the 'ajb al-dhanab (coccyx) remain. In this passage, al-Amidi presents two scholarly responses: one that specifies exceptions, and another that reinterprets the term 'perishing' itself. Let's explore these approaches.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poem line that begins the passage.
2
Identify the two scholarly positions
The passage presents two responses to the verse. Find the first position (specification) and the second (reinterpretation of 'hālik').
The second position is introduced by 'وذهب محققو المتأخّرينَ'.
3
Understand the key terms
Define 'takhsīṣ' (specification), ''āmm' (general), and 'hālik' (perishing) as used in the passage.
These terms are essential for kalam reasoning.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how each position affects the belief in permanence of the soul, the Throne, etc. Which view do you find more coherent?
5
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to test your grasp of the later verifiers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكُلَّ شَيءٍ هالك قد خَصَّصوا عُمُومَهُ فَاطْلُبْ لِمَا قَدْ لَخَصُوا. ولمَّا كانَ القولُ ببقاء الرُّوحِ وعَجْبِ الذَّنب هو الرَّاجح ، أَجابَ عما يخالفه كقوله تعالى: (كُلُّ شَيءٍ مِنَ الكائنات جواهرها وأعراضها هَالِكُ) ، أي: زائلٌ فَانٍ إِلَّا وَجْهَهُ ﴾ [القصص: ۸۸]، أي: ذَاتَه ، مُقتضاه: أَنَّ كُلَّ ما سواه تعالى محكوم عليه بالهلاك ؛ لأنَّ الاستثناء معيار العموم ، وحاصل جوابه : أنَّ العلماءَ قَدْ خَصَّصُوا عُمُومَهُ أي : فَصَرُوا استغرَاقَه ؛ إذ التَّخصيصُ قَصْرُ العام على بعض أفراده، والعام: لفظ يَستغرِقُ الصَّالح له مِن غير حصر، فَاطْلُبْ، أي: توجه لِمَا قَدْ لَخَصُوا يعني الأمور الَّتي نَصُّوا عليها وَرَوَوْا أحاديثها. وهذا الذي سلكه الناظم رحمه الله تعالى. وذهب محققو المتأخّرينَ إلى أنّه لا استثناء ولا تخصيص ، وأن معنى «هالك قابل للهلاك مِن حيثُ إمكانه وافتقاره، كما هو معنى «فَانٍ» أيضاً.
And every thing is perishing; they have specified its generality, so seek what they have summarized. And since the statement about the permanence of the soul and the coccyx is the preponderant, he answered what contradicts it, like His statement: 'Everything from among the existents, their substances and their accidents, is perishing,' i.e., passing away, except His Face [Qasas 28:88], i.e., His essence. Its implication is that everything other than Him is judged to be perishing, because the exception is the measure of generality. The gist of his answer is that the scholars have specified its generality, i.e., narrowed its comprehensiveness; because specification is restricting the general to some of its individuals. And the general is a word that encompasses what is fit for it without limitation. So seek, i.e., turn toward what they have summarized, meaning the matters they have stated and the hadiths they have narrated. And this is what the author pursued. And the later method-verifiers held that there is no exception and no specification, and that the meaning of 'perishing' is capable of perishing from where it is possible and needy, just as is the meaning of 'passing away' also.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition of takhsis (specification) of general statements in the Qur'an, specifically the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' The author begins by citing the poem line that states that scholars have specified the generality of this statement. The explanation then provides the context: because the view that the soul and the coccyx (coccyx bone) remain is the preponderant one, the scholar answers the apparent contradiction of the verse. The verse literally implies that everything except God's essence is subject to perishing. However, the answer is that the generality has been specified. The author defines 'general' as a word that covers all appropriate individuals without exception. Then he mentions that later verifiers (muhaqqiqun al-muta'akhkhirin) took a different position: they deny any exception or specification. Instead, they interpret 'hālik' as meaning 'capable of perishing' due to its inherent possibility and neediness, not as actual destruction. Thus, the passage presents two interpretations: one that specifies exceptions (like the soul, Throne, etc.) and another that reinterprets the meaning of 'hālik' itself.

Key Terms
تخصيص (takhsīṣ)
عام ('āmm)
استثناء (istithnā')
هالك (hālik)
فانٍ (fānin)
عجب الذنب (ʿajb al-dhanab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse implies absolute physical destruction of all things, leaving no room for permanence of any creation.
Correction: Scholars clarify that the verse either is subject to specification (excluding certain entities like the soul) or the term 'hālik' means 'capable of perishing' rather than actual annihilation, thus preserving the permanence of some creation.

Checkpoint
What do the later verifiers (muhaqqiqun) say about the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face'?
A) They accept the specification of exceptions like the soul.
B) They hold that there is no exception and no specification, and 'perishing' means 'capable of perishing'.
C) They deny the verse entirely.
D) They say it applies only to material objects.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Everything is perishing' is understood by Sunni theologians either as being specified to exclude certain entities, or as describing the inherent possibility of perishing rather than actual annihilation.

?
The scholars specified the verse to protect the doctrine of the soul's persistence. Is there any belief or practice you are so attached to that you would reinterpret clear texts to preserve it? How would you feel if God asked you to let go of that attachment for His sake?

Refuting the Objection that Cutting a Limb Cuts the Soul
Objection and answer about the soul's indivisibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's subtlety allows it to remain intact despite bodily injury.

Core Idea
The soul's subtlety means it can withdraw before a cut, preserving its wholeness.

This passage defends the soul's survival after bodily harm against an objection that cutting a limb would cut the soul itself. The answer hinges on the soul's fine, non-corporeal nature, which allows it to instantly withdraw or rejoin. By studying this reasoning, you'll see how classical theologians argued for the soul's indivisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's argument: if a limb is cut, the part of the soul in that limb would be cut, so the soul is divisible.
The objection assumes the soul is spread throughout the body like a material substance.
2
Identify the key term 'latāfah'
Notice that the answer begins with the soul's subtlety (latāfah). This quality is the foundation of the response.
Latāfah means fineness, subtlety, or non-corporeality.
3
Follow the two possible mechanisms
The soul can either withdraw from the limb before the cut, or rejoin quickly after the cut. Both preserve its integrity.
The author adds a third point: subtlety causes the soul to gather to the remaining parts when a limb is cut.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection is refuted because it falsely assumes the soul is like a material body; its subtle nature avoids division.
This reinforces the doctrine that the soul remains after death and is not subject to physical fragmentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أُورِدَ عليه: مِن أَنَّهُ إذا قطع عُضو حيوانٍ لَزِمَ قَطعُ نَظِيرِه مِن الرُّوحِ؛ فلا يصح إطلاق القول ببقائها. يجاب عنه : بأنَّ لطافتها تقتضي سُرعة انجذابها من ذلك العضو المقطوع قبل انفصاله ، أو سرعة الالتحام بعد القطع ، كما أنَّ اللَّطافةَ مُقتضِيةٌ لانضمَامِهِ عند قطعِ عُضوِ الجَسد إلى باقي أجزاء الرُّوح .
And what was objected against it: that if a limb of an animal is cut, its counterpart from the soul would necessarily be cut; thus the statement of its remaining is not valid. It is answered: that its subtlety (latāfah) necessitates its rapid withdrawal from that cut limb before its separation, or rapid rejoining after the cut, just as subtlety necessitates its coming together when a body limb is cut to the remaining parts of the soul.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the soul's survival after bodily harm. The objection: if a living being's limb is cut off, then the part of the soul corresponding to that limb would also be cut, implying the soul is divisible and does not remain whole. Therefore, the claim that the soul persists after death is invalid. The answer hinges on the soul's subtlety (latāfah): because the soul is extremely fine and non-corporeal, it can either withdraw instantly from the limb before the cut, or rejoin quickly after the cut. The author adds that just as the soul's subtlety causes it to gather itself to the remaining body parts when a limb is cut, it remains intact. Thus, the objection fails because it assumes a material continuity that does not apply to the soul.

Correction: The soul's subtlety prevents division; it can withdraw or rejoin instantly, remaining whole.

The Reality of the Intellect and the Ruling on Speculating About It
Sanusi defines the intellect and discusses the two positions on investigating its reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the intellect according to the scholastic tradition, the two positions on investigating its reality, and the evidence that the recommended restraint is only a matter of adab.

Core Idea
The intellect is like the soul: an unseen reality best left uninvestigated, yet scholars disagree, showing inquiry is permissible.

This lesson examines the intellect, a key concept in kalam. The commentator Sanusi begins by giving its linguistic meaning, then compares it to the soul, highlighting that both are unseen matters. The passage presents two views: refraining from speculation as a sign of reverence, and the contrary view that encourages inquiry. Finally, the mere fact of scholarly disagreement proves that the prohibition is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the commentary.
2
Identify the definition and comparison
Note the linguistic meaning of 'aql (prevention) and how it is compared to the soul. Understand why the author favors refraining.
The Quranic verse is cited as evidence for refraining.
3
Contrast the two positions
Distinguish between the chosen view (refrain) and the teacher's view (delving). See how the author presents both.
The teacher's preference is opposite to the author's stated view.
4
Analyze the concluding evidence
The passage concludes that scholars' disagreement on the reality of the intellect shows that refraining is merely out of respect, not an absolute prohibition.
This is a key nuance in kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات [ حقيقة العقل] ٩٥ . وَالعَقْلُ كالرُّوحِ وَلكنْ قَرَّرُوا فيهِ خِلَافًا فانْظُرَنْ ما فَسَّرُوا (وَالعَقْلُ) لغةً: المَنع ؛ لمنعه صاحبَه مِن العدول عن سَواء السبيل (۱). (كَالرُّوحِ ) أي : كحُكم الرُّوح في طريقي الخوض في بيان حقيقته والوقف عن ذلك، وهذا هو المختار ؛ لأنَّه مِن المُغيَّبات التي لم (۲) يخبر عنها علام الغيوب، وكلّ ما هو كذلك فالأولى الكَفَّ عن الخوض فيه ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْفُ مَا لَيْسَ لَكَ بِهِ عِلْمٌ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٣٦] . ورَجَّحَ أُستَاذُنا في هداية المريد» (۳) طريق الخوض فيه عكس ما ذكرناه تبعًا للكبير (وَلَكِن قَرَّرُوا ) يعني العلماء مطلقا إسلاميين كانوا أو لا (فِيهِ) أي: في حقيقته خِلَافًا) أي اختلافًا ، فخوضُهم في حقيقته وتفسيرها دليل (٤) على أنَّ القائل بالوقف إنَّما هو على وجه الأدب فقط .
Section of the Sam'iyyat. [The Reality of the Intellect] 95. 'And the intellect is like the soul, but they determined concerning it a disagreement, so look at what they explained.' (The intellect) linguistically: prevention; because it prevents its possessor from deviating from the straight path (1). (It is like the soul) i.e., like the ruling of the soul regarding the two ways of delving into explaining its reality and refraining from that, and this is the chosen view; because it is among the unseen matters which the Knower of the Unseen has not informed about (2), and everything like that, it is better to refrain from delving into it, due to His statement: 'And do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' [Al-Isra:36]. And our teacher in 'Hidayat al-Murid' (3) gave preponderance to the way of delving into it, opposite to what we mentioned following the great one. 'But they determined' meaning the scholars absolutely, whether Islamic or not, 'concerning it' i.e., concerning its reality, 'a disagreement' i.e., a difference. So their delving into its reality and its explanation is evidence (4) that the one who says refraining is only out of respect.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage defines the intellect and examines whether one should investigate its essence. It begins with the poem stating that the intellect is like the soul regarding the disagreement about its reality. The commentary first gives the linguistic meaning: ‘prevention,’ because it prevents its owner from straying. Then it compares the intellect to the soul: both are matters where the optimal stance is to refrain from speculation, since they are unseen and God has not informed us about them. This is supported by the Quranic verse prohibiting pursuit of knowledge without evidence. However, it notes that the teacher in 'Hidayat al-Murid' preferred the contrary view, i.e., delving into it. Finally, it explains that despite the recommendation to refrain, the mere fact that scholars (both Islamic and non-Islamic) have disagreed and investigated its reality shows that the prohibition is only out of politeness (adab), not an absolute forbiddance.

Key Terms
عقل
وقف
خوض
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that refraining from speculating about the intellect is absolutely obligatory and that any investigation is forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows that while refraining is recommended, scholars have actually investigated and disagreed, which demonstrates that the restriction is only a matter of adab (respect) and not a binding prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence suggests that refraining from speculating about the intellect is only a matter of respect rather than an absolute prohibition?
A) The linguistic definition of the intellect.
B) The Quranic verse from Surat al-Isra.

D) The opinion of the teacher in 'Hidayat al-Murid'.

Key Takeaway
The intellect, like the soul, is an unseen matter; while refraining from speculation is recommended, scholarly disagreement shows that investigating it is permissible as a matter of adab.

The Linguistic Meaning of 'Aql and the Virtue of Refraining
The commentator gives the linguistic meaning of 'aql and presents the chosen view of refraining with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'aql as 'prevention', why some scholars prefer to refrain from defining its reality, and the evidence from the Quran.

Core Idea
The intellect restrains you; its true reality is best left to Allah.

The faculty of intellect is central to Islamic theology, yet its precise nature is debated. This lesson explores the linguistic root of 'aql, which means 'to prevent', and then examines the scholarly position that one should avoid delving into its metaphysical reality. You will see how a Quranic verse supports this cautious approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the definition
Read the Arabic text and translation. Observe that 'aql is defined as prevention, and that the commentator compares it to the soul. Notice the two approaches: investigation and refraining.
2
Identify the evidence for refraining
The commentator cites the Quranic verse 'Do not pursue that of which you have no knowledge' as support. Reflect on why this verse applies here.
3
Consider the scholarly disagreement
The commentator mentions that some scholars investigated the intellect's reality, and that his teacher favored investigation. Understand that refraining is a position of politeness, not a claim of impossibility.

The Intellect as an Accident According to Ahl al-Sunna
Commentator states Ahl al-Sunna consensus on the intellect as an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna position that the intellect is an accident and belongs to the category of knowledge.

Core Idea
For Ahl al-Sunna, the intellect is an accident, not a substance, and it is a form of knowledge.

In this brief passage, the commentator directs us to consult the definitions found in the books of the scholars, as the poem does not elaborate due to its brevity. He then gives a clear summary of the Ahl al-Sunna consensus: the intellect is an accident, and most hold it to be a type of knowledge. This lesson examines this foundational theological position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note the two parts: the instruction to look in scholars' books, and the consensus statement on the intellect.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the key claims
Find the two main claims: (1) the intellect is an accident, (2) most say it is from the category of knowledge. Understand what these terms mean.
An accident ('arad) is a property that does not subsist by itself.
3
Understand the significance
This distinguishes Ahl al-Sunna from other theological schools that might consider the intellect a substance or a separate entity. Reflect on why the nature of intellect matters for theology.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how classifying intellect as an accident and as knowledge relates to Allah's attribute of knowledge and the createdness of accidents.

Punishment of Some Major Sinners and No Eternal Hell
The poem states the doctrine of necessary punishment for some sinners and non-eternity, with commentary defining key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that some major sinners will be punished but not eternally, and that this belief is based on revelation and consensus.

Core Idea
Punishment of some sinners is certain, but no sin makes a believer stay in Hell forever.

This lesson examines a key line from the poem that affirms both the reality of punishment for some major sins and the ultimate salvation of all believing sinners. The commentary clarifies that this is a matter of belief established by revelation and consensus, not a statement about God's obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and translation
Start by reading the Arabic poem line and its literal English translation to get the gist of the doctrinal statement.
2
Understand 'obligatory' as a belief
Note that 'obligatory' here does not mean Allah is forced to punish, but that it is an established truth that believers must hold.
The commentary explicitly says it is the belief that is obligatory, not the act of punishment.
3
Define 'major sin' and its conditions
The passage defines a major sin as an intentional act or omission without a valid excuse, and specifies that the sinner died without repentance.
4
Grasp the nuance of 'unspecified'
The word 'unspecified' (غير معين) indicates that we do not know which particular sinners will be punished, leaving room for God's forgiveness.
5
Connect to the broader doctrine
The final phrase 'then eternity is avoided' affirms that even punished believers will eventually enter Paradise, not remain in Hell forever.
This distinguishes Sunni doctrine from those who claim eternal punishment for sinful believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كبيرة ثُمَّ الخلودُ مُجْتَنَبْ (وَ وَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضٍ)، أي: اعتقاد أَنْ يُعذِّبَ الله تعالى بعضاً عُصاة هذه الأمة ، غير مُعيَّنٍ ارْتَكَبْ كَبِيرَةٌ، أي: فعلا أو تركا عمدًا من غير تأويل يُعذرُ به شرعا ، ومات بلا توبة .. واجب، أي: ثابت وواقع سمعاً وإجماعا.
And obligatory is the punishment of some who committed a major sin, then eternity is avoided. (And obligatory is the punishment of some), i.e., the belief that Allah Most High will punish some of the disobedient of this nation, unspecified, who committed a major sin, i.e., an action or omission intentionally without an interpretation excusable by Sacred Law, and died without repentance... obligatory, i.e., established and occurring by revelation and consensus.
Source pages p. 563

Commentary
This passage is a definition and exposition of the doctrinal statement from the poem. The author first presents the poem line: 'And obligatory is the punishment of some who committed a major sin, then eternity is avoided.' Then he provides commentary. He explains that 'obligatory' here refers to the belief that it is an established truth that Allah will punish some – but not all – of the disobedient members of this Muslim community. The phrase 'who committed a major sin' is defined as any intentional act or omission that is a major sin, done without an exegetical interpretation that would excuse it in Sacred Law, and the person died without repentance. The punishment is described as 'obligatory' meaning it is fixed and actual, based on revealed texts and consensus. Importantly, the punished individuals are left unspecified ('غير معين'), leaving open the possibility that God may forgive some. The final phrase 'then eternity is avoided' indicates that even those punished will eventually be admitted to Paradise and not remain in Hell forever, thus affirming the non-eternity of punishment for sinful believers.

Correction: The passage states that it is obligatory for believers to believe that Allah will punish some major sinners, which is established by revelation, not that Allah is forced to do so.

Further Specifications on Punishment: Exceptions and Scope
The commentary specifies exceptions and inclusions in the necessary punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational specifications of Allah's threat of punishment: why the punished are unspecified, minor sins are excluded, and disbelievers are included, and how some must be punished while others are under God's will.

Core Idea
Punishment is not random; it is specified by divine justice and mercy.

This passage refines the earlier doctrine that some sinners must be punished. It clarifies that the punished individuals are not predetermined, minor sins are forgiven through avoiding major sins or by divine grace, and disbelievers are included if they are obligated by the law. The result is a nuanced view that a group from each type of sin will be punished while others are left to God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through. This passage is a commentary that lists exceptions.
The text is from a kalam commentary; notice its precise logical structure.
2
Identify the three specifications
The passage makes three key points: (1) the punished are unspecified, (2) minor sins are excluded, (3) disbelievers are included under a certain interpretation. Find each in the text.
The phrase 'غير معين' marks the first, 'الصغيرة' the second, 'الكافر' the third.
3
Understand the reasoning for each
For unspecified: because a specific person could be forgiven or granted repentance. For minor sins: they are forgiven by avoiding major sins, or even without that. For disbelievers: they are obligated by the branches of law, so included if 'nation' means all called to Islam.
The reasoning balances God's justice and mercy.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The verse concludes that a group (طائفة) from each type of sin must be punished, because Allah's threat is true. Those outside that group are under His will.
This refines the doctrine: not every sinner is necessarily punished, but some from each category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: غيرَ مُعيَّن لأنَّ المُعيَّن يجوز العفو عنه مطلقا ، أو توفيقه للتّوبة ، وخرج بقولنا: من غير تأويل يُعذر به الصَّغير ؛ لغُفرانها باجتناب الكبائر، وجواز العفو عنها وإن لم يجتنب الكبائر، ودخل في البعض : الكَافِرُ ، بناءً على أنَّ المُراد أُمَّة الدَّعوة ؛ لأنَّهم مُكلَّفون بالفروع ، فلا بُدَّ مِن نُفوذ الوعيد في طائفة من العصاة ؛ لأنَّه تعالى توعدهم، وكلامه صدْقٌ ، والظَّاهر أنَّ المُراد : طائفةٌ مِن كلِّ صِنفٍ منهم ، لأَنَّ الله تعالى توعد كلَّ صِنفٍ على حِدَته ، وما سوى تلك الطَّائفة .. فحكمُه : أَنَّه في المشيئة
And our statement: 'unspecified' because for a specified person, forgiveness is possible absolutely, or his being granted success to repentance. And excluded by our statement 'without an interpretation excusable' is the minor sin; because it is forgiven by avoiding major sins, and it is permissible to forgive it even if one does not avoid major sins. And included in 'some' is the disbeliever, based on the intended meaning being the nation of invitation; because they are obligated by the branches. So there must be execution of the threat upon a group of the disobedient; because He Most High threatened them, and His speech is truth. And the apparent meaning is that a group from every type of them is intended, because Allah threatened each type separately. And what is other than that group, its ruling: it is under the will.
Source pages p. 564

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary, providing detailed specification of the earlier doctrine. The author explains why the punished are left unspecified (غير معين): because a specific individual may be forgiven entirely or be granted the grace of repentance before death. Then he clarifies the exclusion: the phrase 'without an excusable interpretation' excludes minor sins (الصغيرة) because minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and even if one does not avoid major sins, it is permissible for Allah to forgive them. Next, he discusses inclusion: the disbeliever is included under 'some' if we interpret 'nation' as the nation of invitation (all people called to Islam), because they are also obligated by the branches of the law. From this, it follows that the threat of punishment must be carried out on a group (طائفة) of the disobedient, because Allah's threat is truthful. The apparent meaning is that a group from every specific type of sin (e.g., theft, adultery) will be punished, since Allah threatened each type separately. The remaining sinners (those not in that group) are subject to God's will (المشيئة): He may forgive or punish them. Thus, the passage refines the doctrine by specifying that some from each category will be punished, not that every individual sinner is necessarily punished.

Key Terms
غير معين
الصغيرة
أمة الدعوة
طائفة
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sinners who commit a sin will necessarily be punished in the afterlife.
Correction: The passage shows that punishment is not necessarily applied to every sinner. It is unspecified for individuals, minor sins may be forgiven, and only a group from each sin type is definitely punished; others are subject to God's will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are minor sins excluded from the necessary punishment?
A) Because minor sins are not sins at all.
B) Because they are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and they can be forgiven even without that.
C) Because only major sins deserve punishment.
D) Because the Prophet intercedes for minor sins.

Key Takeaway
The threat of punishment is fulfilled for a group from each type of sin, while others are subject to God's will, and minor sins are not necessarily punished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ash'ari Definition of 'Thing' as the Existent
The commentator defines 'thing' as the existent and proves the non-existent is nothing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of 'thing' as identical to 'existent' and the proof that non-existents are not things.

Core Idea
In Ash'ari thought, to be a thing is to be existent; nothing else counts.

This lesson introduces a foundational Ash'ari ontological claim: the terms 'thing' and 'existent' refer to the same set of entities. The commentator explains why the non-existent—whether possible or impossible—is not a thing, and grounds this judgment in rational necessity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on the nature of God's attributes and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concise style and key terms like شيء, موجود, معدوم.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to page break; the argument is still complete.
2
Identify the definition
Find where the commentator states that 'thing' is the existent. Note that he affirms this as the Ash'ari position.
3
Understand coextensiveness
The text says 'thing' and 'existent' are متساويان صدقًا (coextensive in truth). Grasp that they apply to the same things but differ in meaning.
This is not synonymy in concept, but in reference.
4
Grasp the ruling on non-existents
The commentator asserts that the non-existent (معدوم) is not a thing, whether possible or impossible. Understand the implication: only what exists is real.
This opposes the Mu'tazila who consider non-existents as 'things'.
5
Reflect on the proof from necessity
The proof is that existence is the very reality of a thing; removing it removes the thing. No intermediate state exists. This is known by necessity.
Ask yourself why reason cannot conceive of a third state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا الشيء هو الموجود وثابت في الخارج الموجود. ثُمَّ شرعَ في مسائلَ ينفعُ عِلمُها، ولا يَضرُّ جهلها في العقيدة لدعاء الحاجة إليها، فقال: (وعندنا) مَعاشِر أهل الحقِّ مِن الأشاعرة (الشَّيء هُوَ المَوْجُودُ) أي: اسمٌ للموجود الكائن الثابت، أنَّ الثابت، يعني: (الشَّيء) ومدلوله هو معنى الموجود ومدلوله؛ فهما متساويان صدقًا. فكلُّ شيءٍ موجود، وكلُّ موجود شيء، والمعدوم مطلقًا ـ ممكنا كان أو ممتنعًا - ليس بشيء، ولا ثابت في الخارج؛ لأنَّ الوجود نفسُ الحقيقة، فرفعه رفعُها، ولا واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وهذا الحكم ثابت عندنا بالضَّرورة؛ فإنَّها قَاضيةٌ بذلك، إذ لا يُعقل من الثبوت
And with us [Ash'aris], the thing is the existent and [it is] established in external existence. Then he began discussing issues whose knowledge is beneficial and whose ignorance does not harm in creed, due to the need for them, and said: 'And with us, the community of the people of truth from the Ash'aris, the thing is the existent.' That is, a name for the existent, the being, the established; that the established, meaning: the thing and its referent are the meaning of the existent and its referent; so they are coextensive in truth [i.e., reference]. So every thing is existent, and every existent is a thing, and the non-existent absolutely—whether possible or impossible—is not a thing, nor established in external existence; because existence is the very reality, so its removal is its removal, and there is no intermediate between the existent and the non-existent. This ruling is established with us by necessity; for [necessity] judges that, since no [other] state of establishment is conceivable.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari definition of the term 'thing' (al-shay') and proves that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is not a thing. The commentator begins by affirming the Ash'ari position: 'And with us, the thing is the existent (al-mawjud)'. He clarifies that 'thing' is a name for what is existent, established in external reality. Then he states that 'thing' and 'existent' are coextensive in their external referents (mutasawiyan sidqan)—every thing is existent and every existent is a thing. This means they are not synonyms in meaning (concept) but they refer to the same set of entities. Having established this, the commentator then addresses the non-existent: anything that is not existent—whether a possible non-existent (like a future creature) or an impossible non-existent (like a partner to God)—is not a thing, and has no establishment in external reality. The proof is: existence is the very reality of a thing (al-wujud nafs al-haqiqa); if you remove existence, the reality is removed. There is no third state between existence and non-existence. Finally, the commentator asserts that this judgment is known by necessity (bi-al-darura) from reason, because no intermediate state of establishment is conceivable. The passage thus grounds the Ash'ari ontological commitment that only what exists is a real thing—contra the Mu'tazila who consider non-existent 'things' to have some reality.

Key Terms
شيء (shay')
موجود (mawjud)
معدوم (ma'dum)
ثبوت (thubut)
الوجود نفس الحقيقة (al-wujud nafs al-haqiqa)
متساويان (mutasawiyan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that 'thing' and 'existent' are perfect synonyms with identical meanings.
Correction: The text clarifies they are coextensive in reference but differ in concept (thinghood vs. existence).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented, what is the relationship between 'thing' and 'existent'?
A) They are perfect synonyms with identical meaning and reference.
B) They are coextensive in reference (mutasawiyan sidqan) but differ in concept.
C) The non-existent is also a thing, so the relationship is that of a wider to narrower.
D) They are unrelated concepts from different categories.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that only existent entities are things; non-existents have no reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that only existent things are real. Is your attachment to the future—what is not yet existent—making you anxious or distracted from the reality of Allah's present existence? What would it mean to trust Him with what is not yet a 'thing'?
?
If non-existents are nothing, then your past sins, though once existent, are now gone. Do you still hold yourself to a standard of guilt for what no longer exists? How can this truth free you to focus on the only reality that remains: your current state before Allah?
?
The Ash'ari define 'thing' strictly as existent. Does your speech reflect this? When you worry about 'nothing', or fear 'nothing'—are you giving reality to what has none? How can you train your heart to treat non-existents as truly nothing, and direct all your concern to what is real: Allah and His commands?

Existence as Identical to Essence
Commentator presents the debate and affirms identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī position that existence is identical to essence (ʿayn al-māhiyya), not an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Existence and essence are one and the same.

This lesson examines a dense but crucial theological claim: that the existence of a thing is not something added to its essence but is identical to it. The commentator introduces this position within the broader debate on whether existence is additional to essence, affirming the identity view. Let's unpack the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal English translation. Notice the key terms: وجود, ماهية, عين الشيء.
The phrase 'وجود شيء عينه' is the core affirmation.
2
Identify the central claim
The passage explicitly states that the existence of a thing is its essence. This is a direct rejection of the view that existence is an additional attribute. Note how the commentator explains this.
The variant reading 'أن كل وجود شيء من الموجودات' reinforces the same idea.
3
Consider the theological stakes
This is not merely a linguistic point. Affirming identity avoids making existence an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in essence, which would compromise divine simplicity and the nature of created things.
The mention of the individual (al-fard) being originated (ḥādiṯ) ties to the atomism debate.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many assume existence is a property added to things. This passage guards against that error. Ask yourself: what follows if existence is identical to essence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في زيادة الوجود على الماهية وعدمه. الفَرْدُ حَادِتْ عِنْدَنَا لَا يُنْكَرُ ١٢٢۔ وُجُودُ شَيءٍ عَيْنُهُ وَالجَوهَرُ -١٢٣ وُجُودُ شَيءٍ عَيْنُهُ) يعني : أَنَّ وجودَ كُلِّ شيءٍ مِن الموجودات. وفي نسخة: أنَّ كُلَّ وجُودِ شيء من الموجودات.
The debate on whether existence is additional to essence or not. The individual (atom) is new/originated according to us, not denied. 122. The existence of a thing is its essence, and the substance (jawhar) ... 123. 'The existence of a thing is its essence' means that the existence of every thing from the existents. And in a copy: that every existence of a thing from the existents.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether existence (al-wujūd) is additional to essence (al-māhiyya) or identical to it. The verse begins by affirming the indivisible atom (al-fard) is temporally originated (ḥādiṯ) according to the author's school and is not denied. Then line 122 states that the existence of a thing is its essence (ʿaynuhu), and line 123 mentions 'the substance' (al-jawhar). The commentary explains that 'the existence of a thing is its essence' means that the existence of every existent thing is identical to its essence. A variant reading is noted: 'that every existence of a thing from the existents.' This brief passage asserts the position that existence is not an additional attribute but identical to the essence, a key doctrine in Islamic theology (especially in Ashʿarism) regarding the nature of existence.

Misconception: That existence is an accident (ʿaraḍ) added to the essence of a thing.
Correction: The passage affirms that existence is identical to essence (ʿayn al-māhiyya), not an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the verse, what is the relationship between existence and essence?
A) Existence is additional to essence.
B) Existence is identical to essence.
C) Existence is a property of essence.
D) Existence is separate from essence.

Key Takeaway
Existence is not an extra attribute but is identical to the essence of a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that existence and essence are one. Have you ever tried to separate your identity from your actions, believing you could be a 'good person' without actually doing good? How does this truth call you to live with integrity today?
?
The text says the individual atom is originated (hadith) and not denied. If even the smallest particle depends entirely on God for its existence moment by moment, where do you turn for security that is not rooted in this created realm?
?
Consider the debate: some say existence is additional to essence. In what areas of your life do you treat your existence as something extra or optional, as if your purpose could be added later? What would change if you fully embraced that your existence and essence are identical, given by God?

Defining 'Ird and Its Place Among the Five Universals
Footnote defines honor and discusses its inclusion among the five necessities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'ird (honor) and the scholarly disagreement about whether it should be included among the five universal necessities.

Core Idea
The status of 'ird as a universal is debated; some scholars add it as a sixth necessity.

The footnote begins by clarifying the lexical meaning of 'ird and then dives into a scholarly dispute about its status among the five universal necessities (al-kulliyat al-khams). This lesson explores the various positions, the arguments for and against including 'ird, and the definitions given by later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Read the lexical definition
Start by reading the lexical definition of 'ird: it is with a kasrah on the 'ayn and pluralized as a'rad. Notice that some scholars, including Ibn al-Subki, added it as a sixth universal.
2
Identify the scholarly positions
Identify the different positions on whether 'ird should be included or replaced with 'adyan (religions), ansab (lineages), or amwal (wealth). Note the objection that it is not agreed upon by all previous laws.
The passage refutes the claim that 'ird is absent from primary sources by citing al-Ghazali.
3
Study the conceptual definitions and ruling
Read the definitions from al-Amir and al-Shanwani, which describe 'ird as a conceptual attribute or the affairs by which a person rises or falls. Finally, understand the ruling that corrupting 'ird is punished by hadd for the chaste and ta'zir for others.

(۲) العرض بكسر العين، ويُجمع على أعراض كأقفال ، وهذا ذكره بعض العلماء وتبعهم عليه ابن السبكي، وعليه فالكليات سِةٌ ، واعترضه بعضهم بأنه ليس مما اتفقت الشرائع على تحريمه وإن كانت حرمته معلومة من شرعنا بالضرورة ، وبعضهم أسقطه لذلك ، وذكر الأديان بدله ، وبعضهم أسقط الأديان وذكر العرض، وبعضهم ذكر الأنساب وأسقط الأموال، و بعضهم أئمة أسقط الأنساب، وقول بعضهم: إنه لم ير ذكر العرض لمن يُرجع إليه من الأصول مردود بما قدمناه عن الغزالي وغيره، مع أن من حفظ حجة على من حفظ حجة على من لم يحفظ . انظر هداية المريد ١٢٦١/٢ . وبعضهم

(2) Honor (al-'ird) is with a kasrah on the 'ayn; it is pluralized as a'rad like aqfāl. This was mentioned by some scholars and followed by Ibn al-Subki, and accordingly the universals become six. And some objected that it is not something that all revealed laws agreed upon its prohibition, even though its prohibition is known from our law by necessity. And some dropped it for that reason and mentioned religions in its place, and some dropped religions and mentioned honor, and some mentioned lineages and dropped wealth, and some dropped lineages. And the claim of some that honor is not found mentioned in the reliable sources is refuted by what we have presented from al-Ghazali and others, and the one who memorizes is a proof against the one who memorizes against the one who did not memorize. See Hidayat al-Murid 2/1261. And some... (3) He said: 'locus of praise' is a conceptual attribute that good actions strengthen and bad actions discredit. Al-Amir. And according to al-Shanwani: 'locus of praise and blame in a person. If one says: I mentioned so-and-so's honor, it means: I mentioned his affairs by which he rises and falls, and for which he is praised or blamed. So his saying 'locus' means cause of praise and blame, and that is engaging in matters of perfection or deficiency. So engaging in acts of obedience or prohibitions is honor. (4) He said: 'it may not be violated by slander' means: it is not permitted to corrupt it by slander. Know that corrupting the locus of praise is by mentioning the opposite, and as for corrupting the locus of blame, it is by mentioning those blameworthy matters. So the conclusion is that corrupting honor is forbidden. For the chaste (hadd) and discretionary punishment for other than it.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a detailed footnote explaining the term 'ird (honor) and its place among the five universal necessities (al-kulliyat al-khams). It begins by giving the lexical definition: 'ird is with a kasrah on the 'ayn, plural a'rad. It then presents a scholarly dispute: some, like Ibn al-Subki, add 'ird as a sixth universal, making the kulliyat six. Others object that 'ird is not agreed upon by all previous laws (shara'i'), though it is prohibited in our law. Some scholars replace 'ird with 'adyan (religions), others replace 'adyan with 'ird, and there are further variations in replacing lineage or wealth. The author refutes the claim that 'ird is not found in the primary sources by citing al-Ghazali. He then provides two definitions from authorities: al-Amir defines it as a conceptual attribute strengthened by good actions, while al-Shanwani defines it as the affairs by which a person rises or falls. The footnote concludes by explaining that corrupting 'ird is forbidden: for the chaste person, the hadd is applied; for others, it is ta'zir.

Key Takeaway

?
The scholars debated whether 'ird belongs among the five universals. But your honor is not theoretical—it is violated every time you backbite or listen to backbiting. Will you commit to stopping yourself the next time you are about to participate in gossip, even if it means changing the subject?
